SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        Development of the vast unsettled areas of the western Sudan
has in the past been given relatively low priority, most investments in
agriculture being concentrated in the more modern agricultural sector along
the Nile rivers. Primary emphasis was given to the expansion of modern
mechanized dryland and irrigated crop production. Recently, however, Govern-
ment has committed itself to a reduction of inter-regional income and invest-
ment disparities, and has undertaken a comprehensive development program for
South Darfur Province in the western part of the country. This area has long
supported a growing population of migratory pastoralists along with a smaller
number of sedentary cultivators. In recent years, however, a rapid increase
in both the human and livestock populations has resulted in rapidly increasing
settlement and damage from poor land use practices. The proposed Savannah
Development Project would be an integral part of Government's long term
development program in South Darfur Province. It would constitute the first
stage of a program which, over a period of about twelve years, would institute
changes in customary land use practices aimed at ensuring the long term
conservation and improved utilization of soil and pasture resources.
ii.       The Project would over six years provide measures for improving
the way of life of a large proportion of the people in the area by allowing
a more efficient utilization of land resources. In particular, the Project
would initiate the implementation of a settlement program with the dual
purpose of opening up presently blocked stockroutes and of increasing crop
production. The opening up of stockroutes is vital for the annual migration
of the transhumant families and their livestock, and the rationalization of
local land use patterns in order to facilitate this migration would be one of
the principal goals of the settlement concept. The Project would also aim at
building up a development oriented local administration by helping to estab-
lish a Western Savannah Development Corporation (WSDC) which would have
overall responsibility for the organization and implementation of the Project
as well as the proposed ODM/Abu Dhabi project. Specifically the Project would
provide: (a) staff for the WSDC; (b) a settlement and crop production program
which would include extension services, adaptive research, and a pilot crop
marketing scheme; (c) range management and livestock development programs
including veterinary services, livestock-related adaptive research, and
non-formal education of livestock producers; (d) improved maintenance,
operation and further development of water facilities; (e) Project monitoring
and evaluation; (f) technical assistance for the Gum Arabic and Oil Seeds
companies; and (g) a human health survey.
iii.      An IDA Credit of US$17 million is proposed which would cover
US$12.0 million or 65% of the foreign exchange costs of the Project and



- ii -
US$5.0 million or about 25% of local costs over the six year Project period.
This would represent 45% of total Project costs of ESd 15.3 million (US$38.2
million) including taxes and duties. The Saudi Fund for Development (SFD)
would contribute 30 million Riyals (about US$8.5 million); this would finance
about US$6.5 million or 35% of the foreign exchange costs and about US$2.0
million or 10% of local costs. This contribution would cover about 22% of
total Project costs including taxes and duties. Together SFD and IDA would
finance 100% of foreign exchange costs and 35% of local costs, or 67% of total
Project cost including taxes and duties (79% excluding taxes and duties).
The remaining US$12.7 million would be contributed by Government; this
would be offset to the extent that Project costs include about US$6 million
in taxes and duties. The SFD, IDA and Government contributions would be
channelled directly by Government on a grant basis to the WSDC, which would
establish a separate bank account for the Project. Procurement of equipment
and supplies in orders over US$50,000 would be subject to international compe-
titive bidding (ICB) according to IDA guidelines; ICB would total an estimated
ESd 0.5 million (US$1.3 million). The construction of buildings, workshops,
training facilities and other structures which due to their dispersed loca-
tions would not attract ICB would be subject to local competitive bidding by
local building contractors and the Corporation for Public Buildings and Works.
In those cases where the buildings would be in localities so remote that no
reasonable bids would be received, the WSDC's Civil Works Division would
proceed on a force account basis. In both cases the WSDC would consult with
the Ministry of Construction and Public Works and if necessary request their
services. The total amount of local competitive bidding is estimated at about
ESd 2.3 million (US$6 million). Internationally recruited staff (US$2.6
million) required for technical assistance would be selected in accordance
with IDA guidelines.
iv.       The total annual incremental crop production at full development
would be about 50,000 tons of unshelled groundnuts and 3,400 tons of gum
arabic; this production would be valued at estimated March 1977 prices at
about ESd 3.4 million (US$8.5 million). The total annual incremental live-
stock production at full development would be valued at about ESd 5.4 million
(US$13.5 million). Incremental output was calculated on the basis of the
expected output from the Project as well as from a possible second stage
(Phase II) project; together these constitute the program. This approach was
considered appropriate as initial investments in Project infrastructure,
research, management, veterinary sevices, etc. would be high, and results
would materialize only gradually over a long period of time. The program
would also have a number of additional benefits which are more difficult to
quantify. These would include: (a) better nutrition for about 10,000 farming
families in the area; (b) more reliable, sanitary and accessible water sup-
plies for the population of the whole Province (about 0.6 to 0.8 million
persons); and (c) an institution building effect.
v.        The major benefits for farmers and pastoralists involved in the
settlement and range management schemes would be an increase in income from
expanded crop and/or livestock production. Overall the program would affect
an estimated 35,000 families or about 200,000 people. The average annual



- iii -
gross production of both farmers and pastoralists would more than double over
a thirty-year period; more important, the cash surplus of a program farm
family would increase from US$20 to about US$170, and that of a pastoralist
family would increase from US$100 to US$350. Most farm and pastoralist families
expected to participate in the program have incomes below the current poverty
income level in the Sudan (estimated at U$65).
vi.       The overall economic rate of return was calculated on the basis
of the costs and benefits of both the proposed Project and a possible Phase
II project. The calculation of an overall rate of return for Phase I (the
Project) alone would be misleading since it would not reflect the potential
benefits of the entire program - a period of about 12 years. In the first
phase, only a modest start-up production would have been achieved, and any
success in modernizing livestock production would also be modest. The
program's overall rate of return (ERR) is estimated at 14.5% and is a re-
flection of the rates of return of two components: (i) settlement and crop
production (ERR 9%) and (ii) livestock development (ERR 22.5%). These two
components, however, are not independent, as one of the main purposes of
the settlement program is the opening of trekking routes for nomadic cattle,
and any reduction of the settlement and crop component would adversely affect
the opening of these stockroutes. The small amount of incremental hired farm
labor involved would only be required in times of seasonal labor shortages and
was valued at market prices. Costs exclude taxes and other transfers within
the economy. Foreign exchange was shadow priced at a rate of ESd 1 = US$2
(compared to the current rate of LSd 1 = US$2.5) in the economic cost and
benefit calculations to reflect a more realistic value of foreign exchange
for the economy. An increase of 10% in all costs would reduce the ERR by
1% point from 14.5% to 13.5%. A decrease of 10% in benefits would also re-
duce the ERR by 1% point from 14.5% to 13.5%. The program as a whole would
thus be only moderately sensitive to changes in costs, benefits or the rate of
development, but would be seriously affected should the farmers and pastor-
alists in the area fail to adopt improved land use and management practices.
vii.      This would be the Bank Group's sixth lending operation for agri-
cultural development in the Sudan since 1956. These previous credits have
totalled some US$137 million and included two Mechanized Farming Projects, two
credits for the Rahad Irrigation Project, and a Southern Region Agricultural
Rehabilitation Project. After some initial delays, implementation of the
second Mechanized Farming project is proceeding well. The Rahad Project
experienced severe cost over-runs in its initial stages, which required
refinancing of the entire project with the assistance of other international
financing agencies; although implementation was slow getting underway, since
then reasonable progress has been achieved. Due to the remoteness of the area
and to the difficulties of attracting staff to the Southern Sudan, the Agri-
cultural Rehabilitation Project started slowly but disbursements are expected
to be completed on time.
viii.     On the basis of the assurances obtained, the proposed Project is
suitable for an IDA credit of US$17 million to the Government of the Sudan.






SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I.   INTRODUCTION
1.01      Development of the vast unsettled areas of western Sudan has in the
past been given relatively low priority, as most investments in agriculture
continued to be concentrated in the more modern agricultural sub-sector along
the Nile rivers. Primary emphasis was given to the expansion of modern mech-
anized dryland and irrigated crop production. Recently, however, Government
has committed itself to a reduction of inter-regional income and investment
disparities, and has undertaken a comprehensive development program for South
Darfur Province in the western part of the country. This area has long
supported a growing population of migratory pastoralists along with a smaller
number of sedentary cultivators. In recent years, however, a rapid increase in
both the human and livestock populations has resulted in rapidly increasing
settlement and damage from poor land use practices. The proposed Savannah
Development Project would be an integral part of Government's long term
agricultural and rural development program in South Darfur Province; as such
it would seek to introduce appropriate land use practices in parts of the area
by opening up and demarcating stockroutes and by rationalizing settlement of
farmers around established water facilities. The Project would constitute the
first stage of a program which over a period of about twelve years would
institute changes in customary land use practices aimed at ensuring the long
term conservation and improved utilization of soil and pasture resources. It
would also include provision of improved water supplies; expanded veterinary
services; adaptive research and extension; training; and improved crop market-
ing services on a pilot basis.
1.02      This would be the Bank Group's sixth lending operation for agri-
cultural development, in the Sudan since 1956. These previous credits have
totalled some US$137 million and included two Mechanized Farming Projects, two
credits for the Rahad Irrigation Project, and a Southern Region Agricultural
Rehabilitation Project. After some initial delays, implementation of the
second Mechanized Farming Project is proceeding well. The Rahad Project
experienced severe cost over-runs in its initial stages, which required
refinancing of the entire project with the assistance of other international
financing agencies; although implementation was initially slow, reasonable
progress has since then been achieved. Due to the remoteness of the area and
to the difficulties of attracting staff to the Southern Sudan, the Agricul-
tural Rehabilitation Project started slowly but disbursements are expected to
be completed on time. The completion report on the First Mechanized Farming
Project presents some findings which are pertinent to the Project. The main
lessons were the recognition of the need to intensify research efforts in
order to improve crop yields, select alternative crops, and to rationalize
crop rotations. Moreover, organized contact between farmers and research
centers was found to be essential.
1.03      The proposed program is based on findings of development proposals
put forward by Hunting Technical Services, Ltd. (HTS). The first such study
covered the Southern District of South Darfur Province and formed the basis



-2-
for a project financed by the British Ministry of Overseas Development (ODM),
the Abu Dhabi Fund for Arab Economic Development (Abu Dhabi Fund), and the
Government of the Sudan. HTZS also completed a second study of the adjacent
Eastern District of South Darfur Province. This latter study was financed by
UNDP/FAO and its findings and recommendations were similar in concept to those
for the Southern District. A project based on this study was identified and
prepared by RMEA staff and consultants.
1.04      This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission which
visited the Sudan in July/August 1976 comprising Messrs. S. Silbiger, P. Sihm,
F.M. Patorni, R. Wood (part-time), C. Collins (part-time) (IDA); and K.
Anthony, J. Moller, and P. Houlahan (part-time) (Consultants). At appraisal,
several changes were made in the Project as proposed by Government. The most
important of these were: (a) the extension of the development period from 4
to 12 years and the adoption of a two-phase approach; (b) deletion of the road
maintenance component and of the equipment needed for the ODM/Abu Dhabi
Project; and (c) inclusion of an extension and training component closely
linked with the adaptive research program. Another important change was the
separation of the livestock marketing component with the suggestion that it be
appraised separately as an independent project; this project was appraised by
an IDA mission in March 1977.
II. BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      The Sudan, the largest country in Africa, covers about 2.5 million km2.
The population is presently estimated at about 16.3 million persons, and
is growing at a rate of 2.2% per year; per capita GNP for 1975 averaged about
US$290 (Annex 1). Much of the country is unoccupied or sparsely populated,
and for the most part there is no population pressure on presently developed
land. Although a large part of the country consists of desert or semi-desert,
the Sudan nevertheless has great untapped potential for agricultural devel-
opment. Conservative estimates are that about 35 million ha (80 million
feddans) is suitable for arable crop production, 60 million ha (140 million
feddans) for rangeland and 30 million ha (70 million feddans) for forests.
Presently only about 20% of the country's arable land potential is utilized.
In addition, the availability of water from the White and Blue Nile rivers has
enabled the country to develop the largest irrigation system in tropical
Africa; the total irrigated area is now about 1.5 million ha (3.6 million
feddans), including the 0.8 million ha (1.9 million feddans) developed under
the Gezira irrigation scheme. The proximity of the Sudan to markets in the
Middle East places it in a favorable position for agricultural exports, and
oil-producing Arab countries have increasingly come to consider the Sudan as a
primary future source of agricultural products.
B. The Agricultural Sector
2.02      The agricultural sector dominates the economy, providing the
means of livelihood for around 80% of the population; it contributes about



- 3 -
40% of GDP, 95% of exports and over 50% of Government revenues. Cotton
is the dominant cash and export crop, accounting for 56% of all exports; it
is followed by sesame (9%) and groundnuts and gum arabic (each 8%). Exports
of livestock are small, ranging between 2% and 4% of total exports annually.
In the past five years there has been a large increase in wheat and groundnut
production, as well as smaller increases in the output of sorghum, the main
staple crop, and cotton. The expansion in agricultural production has been
largely the result of an increase in the cultivated area under non-cotton
crops. Most of the acreage expansion occurred in the traditional sector and
in the privately owned mechanized farms in the rainfed areas.
Agricultural Research and Extension
2.03      Agricultural research in the Sudan developed mostly in response
to the need for technology appropriate to irrigated cash crop farming, and
thus far only a limited effort has been directed toward traditional agricul-
ture. The Agriculture Research Corporation is mainly responsible for crop
research; it operates five regional research stations and five sub-stations.
Traditionally, irrigated cotton has received the most attention, and past
achievements in this area have been striking. More recently, work has been
undertaken on crop production systems for irrigated mechanized farming,
including research on sorghum, millet, sesame, groundnuts, and a variety of
other crops. The general application of the techniques developed on these
stations is still unclear, however. Even the introduction of such seemingly
simple technological advances as new crop varieties and better seeds may have
limited geographic application. More complicated innovations such as improved
cultural practices or new crop rotations developed at an experimental research
station generally require further adaptation and testing to meet actual
conditions on small farms. Livestock research is mainly the responsibility of
six production research stations, one veterinary research center and five
provincial veterinary laboratories under the direction of the Under Secretary
for Animal Resources in the Ministry of Agriculture. There is currently lack
of agreement on the scope, thrust, integration and location of research in
the Sudan, and the Government is in the process of designing a strategy for
strengthening its national research system. As part of this effort the
Association is assisting Government in preparing a National Research Program
(NRP) covering the western savannah area. It is expected that the prepara-
tion report for the NRP will be submitted to Government by June 1977 for
further consideration.
2.04      Agricultural extension is mainly the responsibility of the Depart-
ments of Agricultural Extension and Education (crops) and Animal Production
(livestock) in the Ministry of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources and,
to a lesser extent, provincial governments and public agricultural production
corporations. The existing bodies are not geared to the transfer of new
knowledge to the thousands of small-scale traditional farmers and pastor-
alists. Consequently, both the federal and provincial extension services are
with a few exceptions (e.g. veterinary vaccination programs in some provinces)
largely ineffective. The public production corporations, with some provincial
and federal assistance, have been the most important means of bringing about
change in the agricultural sector, but their efforts have to date largely been
limited to irrigated schemes.



-4-
Agricultural Credit
2.05      The Agricultural Bank of Sudan (ABS) was established in 1959, and in
its first ten years of operation served as the main source of credit for the
agricultural sector. In these years, however, its lending was almost exclusively
for cotton enterprises, although limited sums were lent for mechanized crops
schemes and for agricultural machinery. By 1969 ABS was forced to sharply
curtail its lending activities due to the almost ESd 4 million in overdue
loans to cotton schemes. Although ABS has since then begun to gradually
expand its lending to the agricultural sector, it has been replaced by the
various agricultural corporations as the primary lender for the major crops.
2.06      ABS does not have the administrative capacity to service small
farmers directly, and it has therefore concentrated on loans for large scale
enterprises. Small farmers are for the most part forced to rely on informal
credit channels to meet their recurring need for short term credit - a need
which is most pressing just prior to the harvest. Loans from traders are the
most common source of funds, and are usually secured by a lien on crops at a
low price. The effective rate of interest on this "sheil" system may exceed
100%. As part of an effort to strengthen ABS and to enable it to undertake
loans to smallholder farmers, IDA is now assisting in the preparation of an
Agricultural Credit Project.
C. Livestock and the Economy (Annex 2)
2.07      The Sudan has an estimated livestock population of about 14 million
cattle, 12.5 million sheep, 10.5 million goats and 3 million camels. The
livestock industry is of significant importance to the Sudanese economy, and
is one of the major sources of food and employment for the population. The
livestock are owned principally by nomadic herdsmen. In recent years, how-
ever, traditional pastoral nomadism has come under pressure from a number
of economic and climatic factors which are gradually bringing about changes
in the nomads' way of life and inducing settlement on an increasing scale.
Even with the increase in settlement, however, the nomads remain primarily
dependent on livestock.
2.08      The expansion of modern livestock production has been very slow.
There are a number of small commercial feedlots in the area of Khartoum; these
units vary from holding areas to intensive fattening operations. There is one
privately owned dairy farm, but dairy development near the three main urban
areas (Omdourman, Khartoum and North Khartoum) is state owned. A number of
parastatal livestock research units such as the Ghazala Gawazat ranch were
formed in earlier years, but these operations have met with only limited
'iccess.



-5-
D. Government's Future Development Plan
2.09      With its underexploited resources of arable land, range, livestock
and water, the Sudan could achieve rapid economic growth and a steady im-
provement in living standards over the next decade. A number of recent
studies have attempted to quantify the growth prospects and requirements in
the medium to long run, and have concluded that a real growth of Gross
Domestic Product of about 6-8 percent per annum seems attainable. The studies
emphasized that the actual performance of the economy will depend on the level
of external capital inflows, on Sudan's ability to generate increased domestic
savings, and on its success in adapting its administrati rc and technical
services to the higher levels of growth and investment. The Six-Year De-
velopment Plan (1977-83), recently adopted by the Sudan Socialist Union,
emphasizes inter alia: the better utilization of existing capacities in all
sectors; the creation of employment opportunities; improvement of project
preparation and implementation capabilities; and self-sufficiency in basic
commodities. To achieve these objectives, the total investment required for
the Six-Year Plan period is estimated to be ESd 2.6 billion (US$6.5 billion),
or an average of ESd 430 million (US$1.1 billion) per annum. It is estimated
that this volume of investment would produce a growth rate of GDP of 7.5%
annually. The Ten Year Basic Agricultural Development Program for the Sudan
prepared by the Arab Fund for Economic and Social Development would be of
great significance for the country in the realization of these investment
targets. Total cost of the program is estimated at US$5.7 billion, out of
which US$2 billion would be for agricultural development; it forms the core
of agricultural development within the Six-Year Development Plan. To help
implement this agricultural program, twelve Arab Governments on November 1,
1976 signed an agreement establishing an autonomous body, the Arab Authority
for Agricultural Investment and Development (AAAID), to implement the program.
The authorized capital of AAAID has been established at US$510 million, of
which 75 percent has been subscribed (Annex 1).
THE PROJECT AREA
A. General (Annexes 3 and 4)
3.01      The Project would be located in South Darfur Province (Province),
the most westerly province of the Sudan.  The Province's total area of about
160,000 km is divided between four administrative districts: Western, Central,
Southern and Eastern; the provincial capital is Nyala, which presently has
a population of about 60,000 persons.  The Project area would include about
65,000 km or slightly over 1/3 of the Province (Maps, IBRD 12474 and 12475).
The Project would constitute the first phase in a long-term development program
for South Darfur Province. As the benefits of the Project have been calculated
in terms of a two phase program (para 7.02), the Project area is likewise de-
fined in terms of the total area to be covered by the overall program.



- 6 -
3.02      Data from the 1975 aerial census show that a total of about 360,000
people are in the program area during the dry season, half of which are
migratory pastoralists and half sedentary cultivators. About 30,000 of the
pastoralists have a permanent homeland in neighboring areas. Almost all the
150,000 pastoralists (25,000 families) permanently living in the area would be
affected by the program. About 60,000 sedentary farmers (10,000 families)
would also benefit from the program; this would represent one-third of all
such sedentary farmers in the area. The main tribes in the program area are
the Rizeiqat and Ma'aliya. The Rizeiqat is the largest tribe, both in numbers
of people and in animal wealth; the Ma'aliya have a higher percentage of
settled population and are generally less dependent on animal wealth than the
Rizeiqat.
Water Resources
3.03      In the savannah belt, all settlements and stockroutes are located
close to or within easy reach of natural watering points or artificial water
supplies. Since 1947, the Rural Water Corporation (RWC) has constructed a
network of boreholes in the sandy soil areas (qoz). RWC also constructs
hafirs (artifically excavated and constructed reservoirs into which surface
run-off is diverted) which are usually located in the clay soil areas.
Land Use (Annex 4)
3.04      Under the Unregistered Land Act of 1970, nearly all land in the
program area is Government owned. Customary rights of access and use have
evolved over time, and are associated with specific local communities. Access
to these rights are dependent on community membership, usually by kinship
or marriage, although outsiders may be assimilated provided they are accept-
able to the local community. The customary laws governing land tenure vary,
and there is a distinction between rights to hoe, rights to inherit and rights
of access. Under customary law there is free access to the open range.
Livestock owners are liable when damage to crops occurs, but free access to
water and grazing is assured.
Administration (Annex 4)
3.05      South Darfur Province was established in 1974 when the former Darfur
Province was split in two. The 1971 Peoples Local Government Act introduced a
decentralized Government administration in an attempt to involve local people
in decision making. A pyramidal structure of Provincial, District, Town,
Rural, Village and Nomad Councils is now being created on which elected and
Government nominated members sit. The provincial administration is headed by
a Provincial Commissioner (PC) who is head of the Provincial Executive Council
(PEC), head of the Sudan Socialist Union (SSU) in the Province, and who has
ministerial status. Although the District and Rural Councils are ulti-
mately intended to replace the traditional authorities who have historically
regulated all aspects of tribal life, the traditional authorities still play
an important role in the daily affairs of their people. The local admin-
istrations will require significant strengthening of both management



- 7 -
and technical staff before they would be capable of undertaking the successful
management of a major development program.
B. Livestock and Crop Production
Livestock
3.06      There are some 1.3 million cattle, 600,000 sheep and 100,000 goats
in the program area.   These are owned mainly by the migratory pastoralists.
Stocking density varies with season, the highest concentrations occuring
during the winter, at which time migratory herds are grazing along the Bahr el
Arab. The pastoral community represents a way of life successfully adapted to
the environment of the savannah areas. It is the basis for organizing product-
ion, consumption and capital accumulation within the family. However, the
traditional pastoral system has come under pressure from a number of economic
and climatic factors which are inducing settlement on an increasing scale.
These new settlements have in turn put added stress on limited water resources
and have often blocked access to the stockroutes so essential to the free
migration of the pastoralists.
Crop Production
3.07      Rainfed crop production is largely confined to the sandy soils which
are easily worked and allow extensive areas to be cultivated by hand. Bulrush
millet is the prime staple, occupying around two-thirds of the annual cropped
area; the balance is planted almost entirely to groundnuts. The area is
generally self-sufficient in staple foods and produces substantial exports of
groundnuts and a minor surplus of sesame. Soils are usually cultivated
for 4-5 years and then allowed to revert to natural fallow for fifteen years
or more (in areas characterized by land pressure the fallow period may only
be four years); continuous cultivation is sometimes practiced. An average
family of six persons cultivates about 5 ha with hand tools. Millet yields
are low because local varieties are inefficient producers of grain and there
is significant weed competition; groundnut yields are generally good. The gum
arabic tree is an important naturally occuring cash crop.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. General Description
4.01      The Project would over six years attempt to reverse the trend
of ecological degradation within the Project area. It would provide measures
for improving the way of life of a large proportion of the people in the
area by allowing a more efficient utilization of land resources. In parti-
cular, under the Project a settlement program would be initiated with the dual
purpose of opening up presently blocked stockroutes and of increasing crop
production. The opening up of stockroutes is vital for the annual migration
of the transhumant families and their livestock, and the rationalization of



-8-
local land use patterns in order to facilitate this migration would be one
of the principal goals of the settlement concept. The Project would also
aim at building up a development oriented local administration by helping
to establish a Western Savannah Development Corporation (WSDC) which, would
implement the Project and the ODM/Abu Dhabi Project, which is to be carried
out in an adjacent area. Specifically the Project would provide for:
(a)  additional staff for the WSDC;
(b)  a settlement and crop production program which would
include extension services, program specific adaptive
research, and a pilot crop marketing scheme;
(c)  range management and livestock development programs
including veterinary services, livestock-related
adaptive research, and non-formal education of live-
stock producers;
(d)  improved maintenance, operation and further development
of water facilities;
(e)  Project monitoring and evaluation;
(f)  technical assistance for the Gum Arabic and Oil
Seeds companies;
(g)  a human health survey; and
(h)  buildings, construction, equipment, housing and staff
and all operating costs for the above categories.
The WSDC would have overall responsibility for the organization and implemen-
tation of the Project. The basic assumptions used in the development of the
various Project components are described in Annex 5.
B. Detailed Features
Project Management
4.02      The WSDC would be given overall responsibility for the implementa-
tion of the proposed IDA Project (para 5.01). At negotiations Government
presented to the Association a draft Order of Establishment for the WSDC which
was satisfactory to the Association. It would be a condition of credit
effectiveness that the WSDC had been established. The Project would pro-
vide a large number of staff for the WSDC, which would complement those
staff to be provided under the ODM Project. Some 200 professional and 700
supporting staff would be provided, in addition to a limited number of staff
for the Civil Works Division (Annex 13, Table 4).



-9-
Settlement and Crop Production (Annex 6)
4.03      A settlement program would be initiated under the Project in order
to help relieve population pressure in already settled areas and to safeguard
the north-south migratory stockroutes against encroaching cultivation. This
settlement would not create land pressure problems in later years, as it is
estimated that even at the end of a second phase settlement program, only 20%
of the available land would be under cultivation. Thirty new settlements
of up to 15,000 ha of cropping area each would be established around existing
wateryards. 1/ Some of these wateryards have been opened, and consequently
people are already moving into the surrounding area; the emphasis in these
settlements would be on the restriction of unauthorized migration into the
area, and on the introduction of improved farming methods. Assurances were
obtained at negotiations that no further permanent settlement in any area
would be permitted except as authorized by WSDC, in consultation with the
Association and on the basis of satisfactory settler selection criteria. An
early task of the WSDC would be to complete the designs for such settlement
area. Other wateryards would not be opened until the Land Management Division
of the WSDC had prepared a plan for the settlement process; the initial
emphasis in these cases would be on settler selection and design of the
settlement area. An aerial survey of the developmemt area would be made
during the first year of implementation. As far as possible settlements would
be occupied by settlers from a single community, with the selection of sett-
lers for the as yet unopened wateryards the responsibility of both the pro-
vincial administration and traditional tribal authorities. The areas around
the wateryards would be surveyed and divided on average into farms of between
20 and 30 ha. In addition to the cropping areas, each settlement unit would
contain 2,500 ha of communal grazing. It is estimated that by the end of the
Project investment period about 2,500 farmers would have been settled, 50,000
to 75,000 ha of land allocated, and about 3,500 ha cropped. (At full de-
velopment these 2,500 farmers would cultivate an area of about 16,000 ha.)
Most families would not be able to grow crops during the season they are
moved, and crop production in the second year of settlement may be insuffi-
cient to meet the family needs. Supplementary food rations would therefore
need to be provided for at least two years. The World Food Program has
indicated that it would look favorably upon a request for assistance, and
under a standard WFP agreement would supply food free to the port of entry.
Thereafter, it would pay 50% of internal transport and storage costs. Gov-
ernment would pay for the balance and provide local staff and these Government
expenditures have been included in Project cost. Pursuant to informal as-
surances obtained at negotiations, Government will make the necessary
arrangements with the World Food Program to obtain supplementary food for the
settlers.
4.04      The Land Management Unit located at Ghazala Gawazat would have
responsibility for the settlement program. The settlers would receive long
term land use rights containing covenants for a suitable cropping system.
1/   Usualiy consisting of a fully equipped borehole, an elevated reservoir
(50 m ), fountains for the human population and metal watering troughs
for animals; these are located in a fenced area of 3,000 to 4,000 m.



- 10 -
The terms, conditions, and duration of land use rights would be specified so
as to provide settlers with security of tenure, and assurances to this effect
were obtained at negotiations. 1/ These land use rights, their terms and condi-
tions would be recorded at a land registry to be established at Nyala; assur-
ances that this land registry would be established by September 1, 1978 were
obtained at negotiations. The rate at which the above measures could be intro-
duced would depend on the extent to which local and traditional institutions
could be mobilized. A Sudanese sociologist, who is well informed about the
local conditions and habits of social groups would be employed to assist the
Land Management Unit staff in developing settlement plans and in maintaining
a permanent dialogue between elders, the local authorities, the WSDC and the
professional field teams implementing the settlement program.
4.05      Extension.  Extension advice would be disseminated to farmers
through Nucleus Development Centers (NDC) to be sited at El Heid, (to serve
the Rizeiqat Qoz), Mazroub (to serve Ma'aliya Qoz) and Abu Matariq (to serve
the Baggara Land System). Control and supervision of extension and the
Nucleus Development Centers would be provided from Ghazala Gawazat. Each NDC
would be directed by a Senior Extension Officer. One Extension Officer would
be provided for every three settlements, and this officer would be assisted by
Extension Assistants, one of whom would be given responsibility for each
settlement. Additional Extension Assistants would be provided for extension
work in areas from which the new settlers have moved. Each NDC would contain
field trial and demonstration areas, and a small training school for farmers.
Extension staff would receive initial training at Ghazala Gawazat and would
receive in-service training at regular intervals. Extension policy and pro-
grams would be drawn up by the Senior Agronomist (Extension) at Ghazala
Gawazat in consultation with the Senior Extension Officers.
4.06      Research.  Program specific adaptive research and field trials would
be an essential part of the initial development program in crop production,
and the Project research program would be closely coordinated with that to be
carried out under the ODM/Abu-Dhabi project. Its objective would be to
provide the essential support required for adaptive crop research in the
Project area and to guide, instruct and monitor the experimentation and
demonstration that would be carried out on 6 Project related NDC's and 3
range stations. Adaptive research would be kept to the minimum and would
support only strictly Project related requirements. The adaptive research
program would in particular concentrate on selection of improved varieties
of currently grown crops for distribution to farmers, the identification
of possible new crops suited to local conditions, the testing and adaption of
improved crop production techniques for introduction in the area, and the
development of better farming systems. High priority would be given to this
adaptive research in order that a proven package of locally adapted technology
would be available for dissemination by the extension staff when needed by the
farmers. A Crop Experimental Section would be set up at Ghazala Gawazat with
1/   Settlers would initially have full rights to only half of the farm
area - usually 10-15 ha (Annex 5).



- 11 -
facilities for field experimentation and for trials at the NDC's on sites
typical of the different environments found in the program area. The trials
would initially concentrate on: (a) rapid screening of millet varieties and
comparative trials of those showing promise; (b) similar screening and testing
of other possible crop varieties with particular emphasis on forage legumes;
(c) testing of ox-drawn implements suitable for local manufacturers; (d) eval-
uation of methods for control of weeds including simple low cost chemical
control; (e) testing of alternative crop rotations and cultivation systems for
the sandy soils; (f) examination of ways to develop the naga'a soils, cultiva-
tion of which could reduce pressure on the Baggara land system. There would
be a continuous two-way flow of information through the extension service; it
would both provide the farmers with advice on improved methods of crop produc-
tion and keep the research staff informed on the farmers' adaptive research
needs and any problems associated with the acceptance of its recommendations.
Because of the urgency for developing and testing of new methods and the need
for close coordination, program-specific adaptive research is advocated. It
is however recognized that the establishment of a more far-reaching research
program for the savannah region (para 2.03) under a National Research Program
(NRP) would necessitate some accommodation between Project research and the
larger national effort. When the NRP has been agreed and is equipped to
become fully operational, the research related functional and organizational
responsibilities of the Savannah Development Project and of the NRP will be
reviewed with a view to assigning to the latter overall responsibility for
supporting and monitoring all research in the savannah areas. The timing
for this transfer between the NRP and the WSDC would be a matter for mutual
consultation at that time in the light of the implementation schedule of
both programs. The Association and other financing agencies would be given
an opportunity to comment on the precise proposals made at that time. The
above understandings are contained and recorded in the Agreed Minutes of
Negotiations dated April 8, 1977.
4.07      Pilot Crop Marketing Scheme.  Although the marketing system now
operating in the area is reasonably satisfactory, due to the difficulties in
collecting produce from large numbers of widely scattered farmers and the high
transport costs involved, returns to the farmers are relatively low. The
Marketing Division of the WSDC would therefore finance and administer a small
pilot crop marketing scheme to facilitate the farmgate to market flow of
crops. It would establish a small number (about 7) of Bonded Storage Areas
(BSA) in the producing areas - most probably in the new settlement villages.
The Marketing Division would operate a small revolving fund to purchase crops
coming to the BSA and would pay a proportion (perhaps 20 percent) of the local
minimum price upon delivery. The crops in the BSA would be sold by auction
to merchants or agents as at other markets, and the difference between the
realized price (less a nominal charge for storage) and the advance would be
paid to the producer. Assurances that such a small pilot scheme would be
started by the WSDC were obtained at negotiations.
Livestock Development (Annex 7)
4.08      The Project would seek to initiate modernized animal production in
the program area by assisting the predominantly nomadic livestock owners



- 12 -
to overcome present constraints to improve productivity; major constraints
include: blockage of traditional migratory stockroutes and encroachment of
cultivation, overstocking of pastures, lack of permanent stock water, animal
diseases and an inadequate marketing system. It would also aim at achieving
maximum involvement of the pastoralists through the establishment of a Live-
stock Development Committee of elected members representing the livestock
producers in the area; this Committee would act as a link between the pastora-
lists, the Project management and the local administration. Provision would
be made under the Order establishing the WSDC that such a Committee would be
established within the first year of Project development. The major compon-
ents of the livestock development program would include (a) data collection, a
nonformal education program and training; (b) adaptive research; (c) improved
veterinary services; and (d) establishment of a breeding herd.
4.09      Data Collection, Non-Formal Education and Training.  The Project
would initiate collection of data on migratory and sedentary livestock.
With the agreement of the pastoralists, the Project staff would establish a
master plan for range management in the various land systems of the program
area. Non-formal education of elders would be undertaken in connection with
data gathering during the dry season and would aim at explaining the prin-
ciples of range management, animal husbandry, animal health, and marketing of
surplus stock. Non-formal education of young stockmen would consist of
annual courses of 90 days duration to be given at a Project Training Center at
Ghazala Gawazat. The Project would also establish a range school at Ghazala
Gawazat which would conduct a 12 month course for 15 agricultural graduates
each year, and would award fellowships for overseas training for a degree in
range management. This education and training program would be coordinated
with the activities of three proposed Integrated Rural Education Centers to
be established in the area under the IDA financed Second Education Project.
4.10      Adaptive Research.  The adaptive livestock research program to be
instituted by the Project would concentrate on: (a) herd monitoring after the
introduction of certain improved techniques; (b) grass and crop residue
fattening trials; and (c) investigations into the possibilities of the integra-
tion of crop and livestock production within the farming system. A total of
11 months of consultant services would be provided to allow for the services
of an internationally recruited livestock research scientist to assist in
planning and supervising the research program. Collaboration with the NRP
when it becomes fully operational (para 4.06) and other international live-
stock research organizations would be sought for these and other Project
related livestock activities and in particular for the integration of crops
and livestock production.
4.11      Veterinary Services.  The Project would expand veterinary services
tn the program area. Six veterinary dispensaries and three mobile vaccination
units would be provided for in the first two years of the Project; also,
the District veterinary headquarters in Ed Da'ein would be expanded to include
a diagnostic unit. Vehicles and housing would be provided for Project staff.
Drugs, chemicals and equipment for the control of parasites would be made
available for sale to the livestock owners who would be instructed in their



- 13 -
proper utilization. The day-to-day implementation of this component would
be the responsibility of the WSDC's District Veterinary Officer at Ed Da'ein
who would cooperate and coordinate his activities with the Livestock Division
at Ghazala Gawazat; however, on technical matters he would be responsible to
the Veterinary Services Division of the Animal Resources Department of the
Ministry of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources in Khartoum.
4.12      Establishment of a Breeding Herd.  The Livestock Division would
assume responsibility for the existing herd of cattle at Ghazala Gawazat
and would initially cull heavily to relieve the present pressure on the graz-
ing blocks. It is expected that towards the end of the Project, two small
breeding herds of about 100 cows each (Kenana and Butana cattle respectively)
would be established from which bulls would be sold to the producers.
Water Facilities (Annex 8)
4.13      Hafirs.  About 20 hafirs would be constructed by the Civil Works
Division of the WSDC; provision would also be made for the rehabilitation
of about 20 existing hafirs which have become inoperative. Hafirs would be
constructed in those areas where groundwater is not available and where soil
conditions are favorable (i.e., where there are impervious soils). Sites
for the hafirs would be located by Civil Works Division Engineers with the
assistance of professionals from the Range Division so that they would have
the best locations from an engineering point of view and at the same time
be located in accordance with good range management practice.
4.14      Wateryards.  Wateryards are common focal settlement points in the
sandy soil regions. In order to facilitate the Project settlement program
(para 4.03), three wateryards would be opened in new settlement areas.
These facilities would be constructed by the Rural Water Corporation (RWC)
and would be supervised by the Civil Works Division. In each instance
provision would be made for: installation of a pump and engine; erection of
an elevated water reservoir; installation of piping and valves; a pump house;
fencing; installation of a water meter; disinfection of the well, piping and
reservoir.
4.15      Wateryard Maintenance and Operation.  A Wateryard Division would be
created within the WSDC in order to strengthen and improve the maintenance of
all wateryards in South Darfur Province. The division would be responsible
for the operation of wateryards with the view of making them self-supporting
during the Project period. A water charge of bSd 0.26 (US$0.65) per m of
water would be required to cover the 3total costs. This would represent an
increase of bSd 0.15 (US$0.38) per m of water or 136% as compared to the
present charge. Since it was felt that such a sizeable increase was beyond
what the new settlers woin1d be willing and able to pay, at this stage only
ESd 0.21 (US$0.53) per m was considered recoverable, and this rate was used
in calculating revenues from the wateryards. This charge would cover the
costs of replacement of vehicles, office equipment, spare parts, tools, well
pumps, engines and water meters and all operating costs. Items which were not



- 14 -
included (e.g. buildings, wells, reservoirs, etc.) have a long life and
normally would not need replacement during the lifetime of the Project. On
completion of the Project, and once the Wateryard Division becomes a viable
and an efficient organization, it could be transferred with its plant, houses,
equipment, vehicles, and staff to the appropriate Government organization.
Assurances were obtained from Government at negotiations that maintenance
and operating costs for the wateryards would be recovered to the fullest
extent possible by establishing an appropriate rate for water charges which
would be reviewed at regular intervals not exceeding 12 months with Government
during Project supervision and adjusted as necessary; all funds collected by
WSDC for water charges would be used exclusively for maintenance and operating
costs of wateryards.
Monitoring and Evaluation (Annex 9)
4.16      A monitoring and evaluation unit would be established in the Min-
istry of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources in the first year of Project
implementation to collect and evaluate data and other information on the
progress of the Project; this unit would serve both the proposed Project and
the ODM/Abu Dhabi project in the Southern District. The monitoring program
would enable the Project to be gradually adjusted in the light of changing
circumstances and would help to ensture its successful implementation. The
unit would collect data on which to base the decisions regarding the timing
and rate of implementation of a possible second phase project. The unit
would have a small permanent staff made up of an experienced Senior Agricul-
tural Economist and a Senior Sociologist; this staff would be assisted by
two internationally recruited consultants - an Agricultural Economist and a
Sociologist. If additional special expertise should be required, consultants
would be engaged for short periods.
Technical Assistance for Marketing Companies (Annex 10)
4.17      Technical assistance would be provided under the Project to assist
the Gum Arabic Company and the Sudan Oilseeds Company to improve their mar-
keting systems and to explore the possibilities of opening new export markets
for both gum arabic and groundnuts. Although both of these companies have
been established for 6-7 years, they are relatively inexperienced and require
assistance to allow them to expand their buying facilities, develop marketing
systems, and to evaluate the possibilities of undertaking further processing
staff under a consultancy arrangement. A marketing specialist would be
required for each company for a period of two years both to develop marketing
procedures and to train local staff. A third specialist would be recruited
to assist both companies for two years in developing expanded export markets.
Terms of reference for these staff would be drawn up by Government.
Human Health Survey (Annex 11)
4.18      Endemic tropical diseases, particularly Malaria and Bilharzia, are a
major problem in the program area. The lack of medical facilities and avail-
able staff and a temporary shortage of drugs constitute serious drawbacks to
the efficient control of diseases. Government has agreed to include specific



- 15 -
requirements for the program area within its proposed National Health Care
Program. However, the exact nature and the scope of the human health care
needs of the people living in the program area have not as yet been deter-
mined. Therefore, funds (US$100,000) have been included under the Project
for a health survey of the area, and for an evaluation of health priorities.
Government would prepare by September 1, 1978 terms of reference for the
study and a tentative list of research institutions which might undertake
this work.

D. Financing (Annex 13)
4.20      The financing of Project costs would be shared in the following
amounts and proportions:

The proposed IDA credit of US$17 million would be on standard terms to
Government and finance about 45% of total Project cost including taxes and
duties. This would cover about US$12.0 million or 65% of the total foreign
exchange costs and about US$5.0 million or about 25% of local costs. The
Saudi Fund for Development (SFD) would contribute 30 million Riyals (about
US$8.5 million); this would finance about US$6.5 million or 35% of the foreign
exchange costs and about US$2.0 million or 10% of local costs. This contri-
bution would cover about 22% of total Project costs including taxes and
duties. Together SFD and IDA would finance 100% of foreign exchange costs and
35% of local costs, or 67% of total Project cost including taxes and duties
(79% excluding taxes and duties); furthermore SFD and IDA would finance 100%
of capital and about 43% of operating costs including taxes and duties, or
100% and 64% excluding taxes and duties. The remaining amount of operating
costs US$12.7 million would be contributed by Government; this would be
offset to the extent that Project costs include about US$6 million in taxes
and duties. The SFD, IDA and Government contributions would be chanelled
directly by Government on a grant basis to the WSDC, which would establish
a separate bank account for the Project. Government would prepare and sign
a Subsidiary Agreement (Agreement) satisfactory to the Association with WSDC
so as to ensure that the purpose of the Credit will be accomplished by WSDC.
The signing of this Agreement between Government and WSDC would be a condition
of credit effectiveness. Within three months of credit effectiveness Govern-
ment would deposit into the WSDC's account an initial sum of 1Sd 400,000



- 17 -
for start-up expenditures. The WSDC would prepare an annual work plan and
budget three months in advance of the beginning of each fiscal year which,
after approval by the Board of the WSDC and (in the final stages) by the
Minister of Agriculture, would be forwarded to IDA and SFD for review and
comment. Based on the approved work plan and budget, at the beginning of
every six-month period, or earlier if necessary, Government would credit the
WSDC's bank account with the funds required by it for the next six months.
Funds required for technical assistance to the Oil Seeds and Gum Arabic
companies would be chanelled directly to these two companies. Funds required
for monitoring and evaluation would be transferred by Government to the
Ministry of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources. Assurances that the
above procedures would be followed were obtained at negotiations.
E. Procurement 1/
4.21      Procurement of earth moving equipment (US$0.5 million), generator
sets (US$90,000), 15 farm tractors (US$180,000) as well as other farm equip-
ment and supplies (US$0.5 million) in orders over US$50,000 would be subject
to international competitive bidding (ICB) according to IDA guidelines; ICB
would total an estimated ESdO.5 million (US$1.3 million) or about 8% of the
IDA Credit. Orders would be bulked to the maximum extent possible. The
construction of 295 buildings, workshops, training facilities and other
structures which due to their dispersed locations would not attract ICB would
be subject to local competitive bidding, under Government procedures which are
acceptable to IDA, by local building contractors and the Corporation for
Public Buildings and Works. In those cases where the buildings would be in
localities so remote that no reasonable bids would be received, the WSDC's
Civil Works Division would proceed on a force account basis. In both cases
the WSDC should consult the Ministry of Construction and Public Works and if
needed request their services. Building construction would be scheduled so
that completion of the houses is the first priority. Construction of the
other buildings could proceed at the same time as that of the houses, but it
should not interfere with the timely completion of the residences, since they
are the key to obtaining competent professional personnel on a timely basis.
Building construction contracts should stipulate that the houses have first
priority. The total amount of local competitive bidding is estimated at about
ESd 2.3 million (US$6 million). Internationally recruited staff (US$2.6 mil-
lion) required for technical assistance would be selected in accordance with
IDA guidelines. The WSDC's Procurement Division would be responsible for the
preparation of all contract documents and evaluation of bids, and Government
-would promptly grant the WSDC on request the necessary import licenses and
foreign exchange for Project requirements. Government would also expedite all
customs clearances and other formalities. Assurances that the above procure-
ment procedures would be followed were obtained at negotiations.
1/   Amounts are expressed without contingencies.

F. Disbursement (Annex 13)
4.22      The proceeds of the Credit would be disbursed over about six years
on the basis of:
(a) 86% of the total costs of building and construction
(US$6.4 million);
(b) 100% of the foreign and 80% of the local expenditures
for equipment and supplies (US$3.0 million);
(c) 100% of the foreign and 80% of local expenditures for
vehicle operating costs (US$2.4 million);
(d) 100% of total cost of internationally recruited staff
(US$2.6 million);
(e) 15% of total cost of local salaries (US$1.1 million); and
(f) an unallocated amount of US$1.5 million representing
contingencies on the above categories and transferable
to them as required.
Disbursement against (a) (b) and (d) would be fully documented. Disbursement
against (c) and (e) would be made against statements of expenditure certified
by the Executive Director and Financial Controller, the documentation for
which would not be submitted for review; such documents would be retained by
the Borrower and would be available for inspection by IDA in the course of
normal supervision missions. Any funds remaining in the Credit account upon
completion of the Project would be available for reallocation for financing
of supplementary expenditures, subject to the approval of IDA. An estimated
disbursement schedule as well as a Project implementation schedule are given
in Annex 13.
G. Accounts and Audit
4.23      An appropriate accounting system would be established for the WSDC
by the Administrative and Finance Division; this would include the establish-
ment of separate Project accounts for both the ODM/Abu Dhabi project and the
proposed IDA Project. Provision has been included under the ODM/Abu Dhabi
project for employment of an internationally recruited Financial Controller,
who would be primarily responsible for ensuring that these accounts were
prepared in accordance with the requirements of the various financing agencies
supporting both projects.  Government institutions and corporations as well as
other World Bank financed projects are currently subject to annual audit by
the Auditor General's office, which performs this work in a competent manner.
Assurances were obtained from Government at negotiations that certified
copies of the audited annual accounts of the WSDC would be furnished to IDA



- 19 -
not later than six months after the end of each fiscal year. The Ministry of
Agriculture and the Oil Seeds and Gum Arabic companies would establish special
accounts reflecting funds received and expenditures made under the Project;
assurances to this effect were obtained at negotiations.
H. Environmental Impact
4.24      The proposed strategy of the program rests on the assumption
that it is only by gradual amelioration of conditions within the existing
land use system that major environmental deterioration and human suffering
can be avoided in the long term, while at the same time making possible
the sustained increase in agricultural and livestock production. In an effort
to arrest and reverse the pervasive ecological degradation in the program
area, the Project would initiate improved land management practices in both
crop and range areas, restrictions on livestock numbers, water regulation
and protection measures, disease control through both veterinary and human
health programs, training of livestock owners in improved animal husbandry
techniques, and range management practices. None of the proposed measures
would have an adverse environmental impact and, if successful, they could be
replicated in other parts of the country with similar environmental problems.
In order to ensure that the above measures would not be jeopardized by activi-
ties which might not be in accord with the proposed development program,
assurances were obtained at negotiations that no further development in the
Project area would be carried out which might significantly and adversely
affect the pattern of land utilization except after due consideration among
Government, the Association and WSDC.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT (Annex 15)
5.01      The Sudan Government has determined that a Western Savannah Develop-
ment Corporation (WSDC) should be established which would have overall respon-
sibility for the organization and implementation of both the proposed Project
and the ODM/Abu Dhabi project. It believes that such a corporation would be
the most efficient means of carrying out the proposed development projects,
and of improving the administration of the Province over the long term. It
also believes that the WSDC's adaptability and its inbuilt responsiveness to
the prevailing political and administrative environment would allow it to
successfully undertake a large and extremely complex task. The WSDC would be
established under the Public Sector Corporations Act of 1976 (See Annex 15,
Appendix 1). It would be headed by a Board of Directors, .with the Provincial
Commissioner acting as Chairman. The Director General would serve as Secre-
tary to the Board; other members would include: the appropriate Assistant
Commissioners (including the executives of the Rural Councils in the Project
area), local leaders, and a representative from the Ministries of Finance and
National Economy, Planning and Agriculture, Farmers Union and Livestock Devel-
opment Union, Members of the Corporation and the Sudan Feminist Union. WSDC
Headquarters would be established at Nyala, although various staff would be



- 20 -
located elsewhere in the Province according to where they would be utilized
most efficiently.  A small office would be established in Khartoum to accel-
erate procurement of goods and services under the Project.  At negotiations
Government clarified the status, precise functions and power of the WSDC and
its relationship to: (a) the ODM/Abu Dhabi Fund financed project in South
Darfur Province; (b) the Provincial Government and its Administration; (c) the
Ministries of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources, People Local Govern-
ment, Public Administration and Administrative Reform, Health, Education and
Construction; (d) the Rural Water and Agricultural Research Corporations; (e)
the Gum Arabic and Sudan Oil Seeds companies; and (f) other institutions which
would possibly be involved in the Project's implementation. Furthermore,
Government presented the Association at negotiations wixith the draft Order of
Establishment for WSDC which was satisfactory to the Association.
5.02      The WSDC would be managed by a Director General  and would have (at
full development) some nine operating divisions (Chart, World Bank 16579).
Some of these Divisions would report directly to the Director General's office
at Nyala, and others would report to the Deputy Director General's office
located at Ghazala Gawazat. All staff employed by the WSDC would be financed
under either the ODM/Abu Dhabi project or the IDA Project. Most of the local
staff would be seconded to the WSDC by the Ministry of Agriculture; others
would be recruited directly. Before making an appointment to the position of
the Director General of the WSDC the Government would provide the Association
with an opportunity to review and comment on the qualifications and experience
of the person it proposes to appoint. Assurances to this effect were obtained
at negotiations. The WSDC would require some 200 professionals and 700 sup-
porting staff for the Project. About 10 to 15% of the professional and about
20% of the supporting staff would come from existing Government institutions
in the area. One of the major difficulties which the Project could encounter
would be to attract adequate staff to the Project area, which is remote from
Khartoum and from the eastern provinces of the country; the latter are more
developed and offer numerous opportunities for employment for some categories
of staff which would be required under the Project. This problem would be
partially overcome by paying all staff at the wage levels prevailing in cor-
porations in the Sudan operating predominantly on commercial principles (the
"commercial corporations"); this salary level would be provided for in the
WSDC's Order of Establishment. In addition, for certain posts, special allow-
ances (housing, furniture, travel, etc.) would be paid. This salary scale was
proposed by Government and would be effective immediately upon establishment
of the WSDC in order to ensure that the corporation would be able to attract
the necessary highly qualified staff. These salary levels and terms of
service would apply to all local staff either seconded (all secondments would
be for a period of at least 5-6 years) or recruited directly by the WSDC. The
WSDC would prepare a plan for staffing as needed (Annex 13).
5.03      Provision would be made under both the ODM/Abu Dhabi project and
the Project for the international recruitment of several key staff for the
WSDC. Seventeen such positions have been identified, eight of which would be
financed under the Project and nine under the ODM/Abu Dhabi project. Terms



- 21 -
of reference for the Project staff are given in Annex 15, Appendix 2. 1/
These staff would be phased out from in-line executive posts either com-
pletely or into advisory posts during the life of the Project. Selection
for replacement of staff inernationally recruited and training of local
staff would be planned by WSDC. The development of local management capa-
bility would be an important part of the Project, and internationally re-
cruited staff would be expected to train local staff to take over their
roles.
5.04      The WSDC's main functions would be to implement both the  Project
and the ODM/Abu Dhabi project, but could also be given responsibility for
future development projects. It would not, however, attempt to control all
aspects of development in the relevant project areas. For example, the WSDC
would not attempt to direct the development plans of ministries such as
Education and Health in the program area, although close coordination of the
activities of the various institutions would be sought. Particular care was
taken to ensure that the WSDC would not become another permanent fixture in
the Government bureaucracy; it was agreed that on completion of the Project,
and once each functional division of the WSDC had become successfully establi-
shed and a certain expertise gained, this division, together with its staff,
could be transferred to the Provincial institution with authority in that
area.
VI. PRODUCTION, MARKETS AND PRICES, AND FINANCIAL BENEFITS
A. Crops (Annexes 16 and 17)
Yields and Output
6.01      Total incremental output 2/ was based on a comparison of millet and
groundnut yields in the settlement areas under a "Without Program" and a "With
P?rogram" situation:
Without Program                 With Program

             ----___________-kg/ha-----------------

Groundnuts            900  900   700   540            900  950 1,190   1,210

2/   Incremental output was calculated on the basis of the expected output
