- 22
Crop yields and production are detailed in Annexes 5 and 6. With the program,
improved varieties and information on better farming methods would be made
available to the settlers in the program area (para 4.06); it was estimated
that over 30 years yields of millet and groundnuts would gradually increase
from 400 kg/ha and 900 kg/ha to 1,100 kg/ha and 1,210 kg/ha respectively.
6.02      At full development (about year 14 for millet and year 30 for
groundnuts) total annual production would be about 15,000 tons of millet and
50,000 tons of unshelled groundnuts. In the "Without Program" situation no
groundnuts would be produced as millet production needed for subsistence
would utilize nearly all the land available for agriculture. At full develop-
ment (around year 22) about 10,000 settlers would produce annually around
4,500 tons of gum arabic, as compared to about 1,100 tons without the program.
It was assumed that the present yield per tree of 0.22 kg would not increase
due to the program. However, the program would encourage organized plantation
tree planting combined with crop rotation on settlement farms; consequently a
family would be able to tap about three times as many trees annually than is
presently the case (about 2,000 as compared with 600 previously).
Value of Incremental Production
6.03      The total annual incremental crop production at full development
would be about 50,000 tons of unshelled groundnuts and 3,400 tons of gum
arabic; this production would be valued at estimated March 1977 prices
(Annex 19, Tables 7 and 8) at about InSd 3.4 million (US$8.5 million). The
value of crop production foregone on the small areas which would be converted
to stockroutes would be negligible.
Markets and Prices (Annex 16)
6.04      Throughout the western savannah, crop marketing is entirely financed
and managed by the private sector. This privately organized informal infra-
structure functions fairly efficiently, and provides all services vital to the
system's operation including credit, market intelligence, insurance and trans-
port. Government intervention in the marketing system is limited to control
of the larger local markets to assist them with the collection of taxes and to
the setting of floor prices (minimum buying prices) for groundnuts and gum
arabic. Mixed private/Government marketing companies (Sudan Oil Seeds Com-
pany, Gum Arabic Company, etc.) help maintain these prices through the direct
purchase of the crops at the line-of-rail. The Marketing Division in the WSDC
would attempt to improve this situation through the introduction of a small
pilot bonded storage scheme in the settlement areas which would be designed to
facilitate the movement of groundnuts between the farm and the line-of-rail.
The negligible incremental millet output from the program would be marketed
locally. All incremental groundnut and gum arabic production would be ex-
ported. The incremental exports of groundnuts would represent about 10% of
current exports from the Sudan and would have a negligible impact on world
market prices. The incremental natural gum arabic production of about 3,400
tons per annum would represent roughly 10% of recent average annual exports
from the Sudan or about 0.2% of the world trade in natural and synthetic



- 23 -
gums. It is difficult to assess future demand for natural gum arabic on the
major world markets; the Sudan however occupies a privileged position as its
gum is of the best quality presently available, and demand for this product is
expected to remain stable.
B. Livestock
Production
6.05      The program is expected to increase the number of cattle marketed
in South Darfur Province. This increase would be attributable to an increase
in the total offtake combined with a gradual shift of the age distribution
towards younger animals. Projections have been made for cattle only, because
the program's impact on the population of sheep and goats in the program area
(about 5% of total population in terms of livestock units) would be initially
small. Key statistics for livestock marketed in the program area are given
below:
Without Program                  With Program
Y1    Y10    Y30               Y1    Y1O    Y30
---------------0OO head--------------
Total Herd            1,300  1,200    900              1,300  1,600   2,100
Annual Sales /1           60     50     40                60      90     160
/1   Livestock units.
At full development (year 19), total incremental marketed production would be
about 120,000 head of cattle (livestock units) annually. These estimates are
based on available statistics for the program area and on experience gained
in similar areas elsewhere in East Africa. Basic parameters which were
used for estimating incremental production are as follows:
Full Development
Present            Without            With
Situation           Program           Program
Age of cow at first calf   4 years             4 years           3 1/2 years
Calving rate                50%                45%               60%
Calf mortality              30%                35%               20%
Adult mortality              10%                10%                8%
Offtake rate                  5%                 4%               8%
Breeding cows in herd       37%                41%               35%
It is possible that a larger milk supply would also result from the program,
but on the assumption that it would be consumed by the pastoralists, this
benefit was not quantified.



- 24 -
Markets and Prices
6.06      Livestock marketing is entirely in the hands of private merchants.
Agents from Omdourman, Khartoum and the east rely on local middlemen who are
in close contact with the producers and who buy from them on a commission
basis. Livestock traded at the small towns and villages are bought for local
consumption and for assembly for trekking to the main markets of Omdourman,
Nyala, El Obeid and Medani which serve the urban centers. The large markets
have an elaborate system of trading, ranging from tribal or village merchants
to the cattle wholesalers.  There are no weighing facilities at any of the
large markets; animals are selected for quality and their ability to survive
the trek to urban slaughterhouses. The main herds of cattle reach the buying
centers in the west between July and October, but the market supply of live-
stock fluctuates seasonally with the availability of grazing. The increasing
urban population is a key determinant in the steady demand for meat, parti-
cularly mutton, and the seasonal variations in supply are reflected in sharp
price changes.
6.07      Most of the incremental production attributable to the program
would be marketed at the traditional established rural markets and trekked or
railed to Khartoum and/or the coast for reconditioning, fattening, slaughter
or export. The draft Six-Year Development Plan (1977-83) also provides for
an investigation of the feasibility of establishing a quarantine station and
slaughter house in Nyala to facilitate export of meat by air to Zaire, the
Central African Empire, Chad and Libya. Although these new markets may appear
to be attractive at the moment, most of the capital which is committed to
livestock sector developments in the Sudan is expected to come from the
oil-rich Arab countries, with the prime intention of securing their own
supplies of meat to the end of the century. For this reason alone, the
primary markets for livestock from the western Sudan will in the foreseeable
future continue to be in the eastern Sudan; there the animals would be
slaughtered for local consumption or made ready for export to the Middle
East.
6.08      The total annual incremental livetock production at full development
would be valued at about tSd 5.4 million (US$13.5 million), which would repre-
sent 2% of agriculture's contribution to GDP. The value of the incremental
marketed production of cattle was based on an estimated March 1977 market
price in Nyala and Ed Da'ein of iSd 45 (US$113) per head, and no bonus was
calculated for eventual quality improvement. No export of cattle was assumed,
for even though the temporary ban on livestock exports was lifted in October
1976, the expanded production would be required to meet both present and
future domestic demand.
C. Producer Benefits
6.09      All program farmers and pastoralists involved in the settlement and
range management schemes belong to the lowest income segments of the popu-
lation in the Sudan. The major benefits for them would be an increase in
income from expanded crop and/or livestock production. Overall the program



- 25 -
would affect an estimated 35,000 families or about 200,000 people. The
average annual gross production of both farmers and pastoralists would more
than double over a thirty-year period; more important, the cash surplus of a
program farm family would increase from US$20 to about US$170, and that of a
pastoralist family would increase from US$100 to US$350 (Annex 17). Most farm
and pastoralist families expected to participate in the program have incomes
below the current poverty income level in the Sudan (estimated at US$65).
D. Government Cash Flow (Annex 18)
6.10      Government would derive revenues from taxes on crops and live-
stock, import taxes and duties, personal income taxes and water charges. The
scope of the problems involved and the wide area in which they must be imple-
mented make it inevitable that the development effort would have to be con-
tinued over many years, with only gradual and limited change being apparent in
the early stages. Farmers and pastoralists involved in the program would have
incomes below the poverty income level (para 6.09); however, they would pay
unsubsidized prices for veterinary services, operating and maintenance costs
for water, and taxes and duties on crops and livestock. Therefore, given the
type of program proposed, it was felt that at the present time any other cost
recovery from the settlers and pastoralists such as: land allocation or
use charges, higher water charges or increased crop and livestock taxes would
be premature, as it would place too heavy a burden on them. However, if at a
later date total production should increase significantly, or if the settlers
were to achieve higher incomes through other activities, Government might wish
to reconsider its options. At year 30 when it is estimated that the results
of the program's two phases would be consolidated, the net annual program
revenues would be about 3Sd 2.4 million (US$6.0 million) after debt service.
During the disbursement period (year 1 to 6), the net annual Government cash
flow would be negative, and a cumulative deficit of about ISd 1.6 million
(about US$4.0 million) would have been accumulated by the end of year 6. This
amount represents Government's contribution to the program less revenues from
the program, or about 13% of total program cost net of taxes. In estimating
Government's program related revenues it was assumed that collection of taxes
and duties on crops and livestock and of water charges would gradually and
moderately improve (Annex 19).
6.11      The foreign exchange effect of the program would vary during its
lifetime. During the Project investment period (first six years of the
program) it would be positive; however, if it were to be measured over the
course of the program's two phases combined (about 12 years), the foreign
exchange cash flow would show an annual deficit ranging between US$0.5 and 3.5
million in constant 1977 terms over a period of 5 years (from year 7 to 11).
From year 12 onward the annual foreign exchange cash flow would be positive
and by year 14 the cumulative deficit would be eliminated; by the end of the
program, in year 30, when it is expected that the program would be fully
developed, foreign exchange earnings would reach about US$26 million annually
in 1977 constant terms.



- 26 -
VII. ECONOMIC BENEFITS AND JUSTFICATION
7.01      The Project would constitute a first step in the long-term develop-
ment of South Darfur Province. As a first phase of a development program,
it is essentially an experimental one, in which suitable production procedures
would be devised through program specific adaptive research, and a non-formal
educational and extension effort mounted to motivate farmers and pastoralists
to accept measures which would improve the utilization of natural resources.
The main quantifiable benefits stemming from the program would be substantial
increases in marketed crop and livestock production; this incremental produc
tion would be valued at ESd 8.8 million (US$22 million) at the end of the
program in year 30 annually. The program would also have a number of addi-
tional benefits which are more difficult to quantify. These would include:
(a) better nutrition for about 10,000 farming families in the program area;
(b) more reliable, sanitary and accessible water supplies for the population
of the whole Province (about 0.6 to 0.8 million persons); and (c) an insti-
tution building effect.
Economic Rate of Return and Sensitivity Analysis (Annex 20)
7.02      The overall economic rate of return (ERR) for the program over 30
years is estimated at 14.5%; this return was calculated on the basis of the
costs and benefits of both Phases I and II (para. 4.19). The calculation of
an overall rate of return for Phase I (the Project) alone would be misleading
since it would not reflect the potential benefits of the entire development
program - a period of about 12 years. The ERR for the settlement and crop
components for Phase I only was estimated at 4.6%. In the first phase, only
a modest start-up production would have been achieved, and any success in
modernizing livestock production would also be limited in size and purpose.
Therefore calculation of a separate ERR for the livestock component of Phase I
only would not be meaningful.
7.03      The program's overall rate of return (14.5%) is a combination of the
rates of return of two components: (i) settlement and crop production (ERR
9%) and (ii) livestock development (EER 22.5%). These two components, how-
ever, are not independent, as one of the main purposes of the settlement
program is the opening of trekking routes for nomadic cattle, and any reduc-
tion of the settlement and crop component would adversely affect the opening
of these stockroutes. Therefore, a reduction of the settlement and crop
component would not necessarily improve the overall rate of return. Costs and
benefits were based on prices estimated as of March 1977. They include all of
the incremental capital and operating costs which would be incurred on behalf
of the program, with the exception of the costs of: (i) technical assistance
to marketing companies; (ii) the health survey; and (iii) operation of water
facilities not directly related to the settlement and livestock components of
the program. The economic effect of additional employment generated under the
program and the expected foreign exchange earnings have been included in the
rate of return calculations. The small amount of incremental hired farm labor
involved would only be required in times of seasonal labor shortages and was



- 27 -
valued at market prices. Costs exclude taxes and other transfers within the
economy. Foreign exchange was shadow priced at a rate of ESd 1 = US$2 (com-
pared to the current rate of ESd 1 = US$2.5) in the economic cost and benefit
calculations to reflect a more realistic value of foreign exchange for the
economy.
7.04      The sensitivity of the rate of return was tested against changes in
costs and benefits due to variations in prices, production, slippage of bene-
fits and non-adoption of improved farming techniques by settlers. An increase
of 10% in all costs would reduce the ERR by 1% point from 14.5% to 13.5%. A
decrease of 10% in gross benefits would also reduce the ERR by 1% point from
14.5% to 13.5%. The non-adoption of improved techniques by farmers would
reduce the ERR of the settlement and crop component by 4.5% points from 9% to
4.5%. A decrease of 10% in livestock sales would reduce the ERR of the live-
stock component by 3.8% points from 22.5% to 18.7%. The program as a whole
would thus be only moderately sensitive to changes in costs, benefits or
the rate of development, but would be seriously affected should the farmers
and pastoralists in the area fail to adopt improved land use and management
practices. The intensive non-formal education and training programs for
farmers and pastoralists during the Project's period, the establishment of
program specific adaptive research combined with extension services, and the
build-up of a local development oriented administration should lessen the
risks which could be created by a lack of cooperation of participants.
Employment Generation
7.05      The Project would ensure permanent employment for some 900 persons;
around 500 to 700 workers would be given temporary employment during the
Project implementation period (mostly in the construction of buildings and
other works). Furthermore, labor requirements per settler farm would increase
by 20% from 314 mandays to 382 mandays annually at full development. The
utilization of the annually available family labor potential which was esti-
mated at 466 days 1/ would increase from 58% to 68% excluding the small amount
of hired labor for weeding (estimated presently at 45 mandays per annum and
increasing to 67 mandays under the program). No increase in labor is expected
in livestock production.

Provincial Government and its Administration; the
Ministries of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources,

which would be reviewed at regular intervals not ex-

all such measures, determined in consultation with the
Association and on the basis of the information col-
lected by the Monitoring and Evaluation Unit, as shall
be required to make full economic and productive use
of investments included in the Project (para 4.19);
(g) Government will go on to implement further stages in
the agricultural development of the eastern district



- 29 -
of South Darfur Province in line with the objectives
and investments estimated for the development pro-
gram's second phase; details of this assurance are
contained in a supplementary letter (para 4.19);
(h)  the financing arrangements specified in para 4.20
would be adhered to;
(i)  the procurement procedures in para 4.21 would be
followed;
(j)  certified copies of the audited annual accounts of
the WSDC would be furnished to IDA not later than
six months after the end of each fiscal year (para
4.23); and
(k)  no further development in the Project area would be
carried out which might significantly and adversely
affect the pattern of land utilization except after
due consideration among Government, the Association
and WSDC (para 4.24).
8.03      During negotiations understanding was also reached with Government
that when the National Research Program (NRP) has been agreed and is equipped
to become fully operational, the research related functional and organiza-
tional responsibilities of the Savannah Development Project and of the NRP
will be reviewed with a view to assigning to the latter overall responsibility
for supporting and monitoring all research in the savannah areas. The timing
for this transfer between the NRP and WSDC would be a matter for mutual
consultation at that time in the light of the implementation schedule of
both programs. The Association and other financing agencies would be given
an opportunity to comment on the precise proposals at that time. The above
understandings are contained and recorded in the Agreed Minutes of Negotia-
tions dated April 8, 1977.
8.04      Conditions of credit effectiveness would be that:
(a)  the Western Savannah Development Corporation had been
established under an Order satisfactory to the Associa-
tion (para 4.02); and
(b)  the Subsidiary Agreement between the Government and
WSDC has been signed and is satisfactory to the
Association (para 4.20).
8.05      Subject to the above assurances, conditions and understandings,
the proposed Project is suitable for an IDA credit of US$17 million to the
Government of the Sudan.

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Basic Demographic and Economic Data
1.        The most recent census in the Sudan took place in 1973. 1/  While
many of the results are still considered as preliminary, they represent
the most reliable information which is currently available on the distri-
bution and characteristics of the Sudanese population. Some of these
figures must nevertheless be accepted with caution as the rate of urban-
isation or the pattern of internal migration may change significantly in
a short period of time (Table 1). Per capita GNP was estimated in 1975
at US$290 2/, and is currently growing at a rate of 4-5% annually.
2.        For the Sudan as a whole, approximately 18% of the population
reside in urban areas (towns of more than 5,000 population) or in centers of
administrative importance; 82% reside in rural area, out of which about 11%
are nomadic. This distribution does not, however, apply uniformly in all the
provinces. The distribution by province is shown in Table 2. Khartoum is the
most urbanised province, with nearly three-quarters of its population living
in urban areas. The next urbanized province - Red Sea - is a distant second
with only 36.4% living in urban areas. In the other provinces the urban
percentage ranges from 9.6% to 20.9%. The percentage for South Darfur-is on
the low side due to the fact that there are few towns.
3.        Although the urban economy is based on employment in various sec-
tors, the rural economy remains one which is primarily agricultural and
pastoral.
1/   The 1973 population census put the total population for that year at
14.9 million with a growth rate of 2.2 percent annually. This compares
with a population figure of 17.1 million (and growth rate of 2.8 percent
annually) for 1973 which had been previously estimated based upon the
results of the 1955/56 census. Data are being published province by
province and The Bahr El Ghazal volume was completed at the time of
the appraisal and the others were in preparation. The Darfur volume
was scheduled to be completed last because of the complexity of that
province (the nomadic population).
2/   No data are available about variations of the capita GNP in the Sudan.
Per capita GNP in the relatively developed eastern part of the Sudan is
decisive in calculating the country's overall estimated GNP. The GNP per
capita in the West is unquestionably lower than is the case in the East,
and could be compared with that of Chad - US$100 (1974); in its rural
areas it is probably even lower. The poverty income level in the Sudan
has in recent years, however, increased from US$40 to US$65 per capita.



ANNEX 1
Page 2
4.        Agriculture is the major contributor to GDP, but the productivity
per agricultural worker is substantially lower than for workers in other
sectors. For example, a worker in the construction industry contributes
4.2 and mining and manufacturing 7.0 times as much to the GDP of the Sudan
as does the agricultural worker.
5.        The agricultural potential of the country is substantial and cur-
rently underutilized; only a fraction of the arable land that could be
developed is utilized. If by the end of the century a total of about 35 mil-
lion ha of potentially arable land is brought under cultivation, the land/
man ratio would be about 3 ha per one inhabitant. Furthermore, it is esti-
mated that livestock production could over the same period be roughly
doubled; currently there are 7 ha of rangeland per livestock, which could be
more intensively utilized given better management (Annex 2, para 7).
6.        The Sudan's Six-Year Development Plan (1977-83), now under prepa-
ration, will require a total investment of about ESd 2.6 billion (US$6.5
billion), or an average of ESd 430 million (US$1.1 billion) per annum. It is
estimated that this volume of investment would produce a growth rate of GDP of
7% to 8% annually. The Ten-Year Basic Agricultural Development Program (Basic
Program) for the Sudan prepared by the Arab Fund for Economic and Social
Development would be of great significance for the country in the realization
of these investment targets. This Basic Program would be part of a 25 year
plan to make the Sudan the primary source of food for Arab countries; it
estimates that by 1985 the Sudan would provide 42% of the Arab world's total
vegetable oil consumption, 58% of its basic food and 20% of its sugar needs.
Total cost of the Basic Program over a ten year period would be US$5.7 billion,
out of which US$2.0 billion have been earmarked for agriculture and would form
the core of agricultural development within the Six-Year Plan (1977-83).
Twelve Arab Governments on November 1, 1976 signed an agreement establishing
an autonomous body, the Arab Authority for Agricultural Investment and Develop-
ment (AAAID), to implement the Basic Program. The authorized capital of AAAID
has been established at US$510 million, of which 75% has been subscribed.
January 31, 1977

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Livestock and the Economy
1.        Presently available estimates of livestock numbers in the Sudan
are based on extrapolations from various sources - market sales, slaughter
and export figures, sales and export of hides and skins, vaccination figures,
and livestock tax returns. A national livestock census by low-level aerial
survey, now underway, will provide the first nationwide data on livestock in
the Sudan. The table below shows the latest official estimates for the
coutnry by province.
Cattle     Sheep      Goats     Camels
------------million head-------------
1973/74
National Estimate                  14.1      13.4      10.5       2.7
Provinces
Bahr el Ghazal                     3.1        1.0       1.2        -
Equatoria                          0.6        0.5      0.9         -
Blue Nile                           1.2       3.6       2.4        0.3
Kassala                            0.4        1.1       0.7        0.6
Northern                            0.2       0.5       0.3        0.1
Khartoum                           0.1        0.1       0.3        0.1
Upper Nile                          1.8       0.7       1.2        -

Darfur /1                           4.7       2.9       2.5        0.4
/1   North and South.
Source:   National Planning Commission, July 1975.
According to the official estimates, North and South Darfur have the heaviest
concentration of livestock in the Sudan, with 33 percent of the national
cattle herd, 22 percent of the sheep, 24 percent of the goats, and 15 percent
of the camels. Parts of North Darfur are unoccupied, with the majority of
Darfur animals (other than camels) being concentrated in South Darfur for
most of the year.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
2.        The livestock industry is a significant sector of the Sudanese
economy. It is one of the main sources of food and employment for the
population and it is a modest contributor to foreign earnings. In 1975
it has accounted for about 16% of GDP and livestock exports have contributed
from 6% to 10% of the value of total exports. In December 1974, a temporary
ban was placed on the exports of livestock and meat in to stabilize do-
mestic supplies and prices. The ban was lifted in October 1976.
3.        It is estimated that about 2.6 million families own livestock.
In the Western Provinces, most livestock appear to be owned in groups of
about 30 to 50 cattle and around 40 to 60 sheep and goats. Sedentary
producers usually have a few animals only. The bulk of the livestock are
owned by two pastoral groups, the northern Arab Baggara tribes mainly in the
Kordofan and Darfur provinces and the southern Nilotic tribes.
Cattle Breeds
4.        The Sudan has some of the best livestock breeds of Africa
and the Near East. Cattle and sheep are relatively large-framed types and
capable of satisfactory growth rates under conditions of adequate feeding.
The Kenana cattle have been selected for milk yield, and production
exceeding 1,000 litres per lactation is not uncommon:
(a) Baggara Cattle. These are of East African Zebu type. They
are found in Kordofan and Darfur provinces and along the
White Nile, north of latitude 120 N and along the Blue Nile.
They number about 7 million head, and include sub-types such
as the Kenana, Butana and Rafan. The Kenana are grey in color
and are reported to give more milk than the predominantly
red-skinned beefier Butana animals. Their genetic potential
is good for production with wellfed mature animals of four
to five years of age achieving 500 kg liveweight. Well-fed
young stock can reach 250 kg, i.e. breeding weight within two
years, although normally heifers calve at three and a half
years or older.
(b) Nilotic or Southern Cattle. These are a smaller breed than
the Baggara. They are a long-horned small-humped type of Zebu.
They are believed to number about 5 million head, and they
inhabit the southern provinces of Bahr El Ghazal, Upper
Nile and Eastern Equatoria. The meat of this breed is said
to be of better quality than that of the Baggara because
more of the males are castrated and their environment provides
better grazing and more water. This is probably not a breed
difference but due to tribal management customs.
1/   Source:  Ministry of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
(c) Exotic Crossbreeds. There has been very little cross-
breeding with exotic blood. The Belgravia dairy close to
Khartoum milks about 500 cows, mostly crossbreeds of
Butana with Holstein, Ayrshire and Guernsey for average
daily production of 8-9 kg milk.
Livestock Production Systems
5.        Probably 60% of cattle production, up to 80% of sheep and goat pro-
duction and all camel production comes from migratory producers; much of the
rest comes from producers having a base in farming. The remainder comes from
small mixed, settled farmer operations. In the Western Provinces, herds of
cattle move between northern and southern locations. With the approach of the
wet season, cattle leave the wetter areas of the south to reach their summer
grazing areas in the north, crossing the railway line during July. Cattle
begin moving southwards again in September, cross the railway line in October
and reach the dry season grazing areas again about December. During the dry
season, nomadic herds are usually in remote areas. Most sales, therefore,
occur in the wet season between July and October. Nomadic sheep and goat
flocks remain during the dry season in areas close to the main migatory stock
routes, i.e. within the 400 mm to 600 mm rainfall belt, often browsing feed
left by cattle. In July, flocks begin moving north to spend the wet season
in areas which receive 150 mm to 250 mm of rainfall, about 200 km to 300 km
to the north and in the general direction of the markets in the east. Some
mixed crop livestock farmers are now settled but have transhumant livestock
which usually graze in the village croplands during the early rains when
the crops are planted and also during the dry season after harvest. During
the rains, these stock move northwards and once the water points have dried
up and crop residues have been consumed, they move southwards. Villages which
have permanent water tend to keep their animals around the village for as long
as possible, with consequent over-grazing and reduced livestock performance
due to under-nutrition.
Patterns of Ownership
6.        In recent years, traditional pastoral nomadism, particularly
among the Baggara, has come under pressure from a number of economic, pol-
itical and climatic factors which are gradually bringing about changes and
inducing settlement on an increasing scale. Settlers bring small herds
with them to agricultural settlement schemes; older members of the 'farig'
or family group remain as semi-sedentary cultivators with a small herd while
the young men, with the main herds, follow the traditional nomadic system.
The village-based herds are growing in importance and, while the problems
of maintaining such stock on surrounding grazing land are considerable, there
is ample opportunity and need to develop this pattern of management. In
addition to the traditional systems of management, there are number of small
commercial feedlots near of Khartoum. These units vary from holding grounds
to intensive fattening farms where cattle are fed on crop residues and supple-
ments for quick compensatory weight gains. There is one privately owned dairy



ANNEX 2
Page 4
farm but dairy development in the area of the Three Towns 1/ is state owned
and managed (e.g. the Kuku and El Qurishi farms). A number of parastatal
livestock production units such as the ranch on the Chazala Gawazat Research
Center, have been formed, but the experience has not met with success.
Feeding and Nutrition of Livestock
7.        The greater part of the Sudan consists of vast areas of grazing
lands utilized by primarily migratory stockowners. The savannah and the
southern flood lands together provide about 80% of the feed consumed by
livestock and consist of annual and perennial grasses and some browsing
shrubs. However, because of the short growing season (July - September),
and the shortage and uneven distribution of water (mostly shallow wells)
part of the feed remains unused and is burned - a factor which contributes
to livestock bodyweight fluctuations. Some of the land is used for farming
but the major part is grazed by livestock. The areas, by province, are as
follows:

Of this area, it is estimated that some 35 million ha are suitable for
crop production and the remaining 105 million ha for livestock production,
rangeland and forests. From these assumptions it is estimated that the
range could, in fact, support 1.5 times more animals at present i.e. about
25 million cattle, 25 million sheep, 17.5 million goats and 5 million
camels.
By-Product Feeding
8.        The Sudan produces a wide range of crops and crop by-products which
can be used for animal feeding (including cotton seed and other oil cakes,
groundnuts, sesame, sorghum, pulses, molasses, wheat bran). During recent
years a great increase in concentrate feeding of livestock, especially of
slaughterstock in feedlots, has taken place. In spite of this, large quanti-
ties of available feed for livestock are either unutilized or are exported.
Cotton seed cake and bran from local mills are in relative abundance and meet
the main demand for feeds arising in the urban areas. Oilseeds and oilseed
cake, cotton and gum arabic, constitute the main export commodities. A modern,
automated feed compounding with a capacity of 10,000 tons has been factory
established in Khartoum in association with a flour mill. In addition a num-
ber of commercial feedlots also have their own milling and mixing facilities.
The table below, compiled by the FAO 1975 Perspective Study of Agricultural
Development in the Sudan, gives an estimate of the grains, by-products
and crop residues available in 1970 and two alternative estimates for 1985.

ANNEX 2
Page 6
Forage Production on Irrigated Land
9.        Approximately 1.5 million of irrigated lands, mostly in the east,
contribute increasingly to the maintenance of livestock. In addition, herds
and flocks utilize the aftermath of crops on irrigated areas. There is also
considerable potential for developing specialized forms of production by
utilizing the rangelands for the production of store animals, such as young
bulls and male lambs, which can be finished in feedlots utilizing feeds,
fodder and agricultural by-products produced on the irrigated areas.
Preparing Animals for Export
10.       To hold and prepare animals for slaughter and export, simple live-
stock reconditioning operations have developed around Khartoum - Wad Medani
in recent years. Livestock are kept on bare land where they are fed ground-
nut hulls, cotton-seed cake and mature sorghum stalks. All these operations
have high cost due to the shortage of low priced, better quality feedstuffs
around Khartoum without which efficient fattening performances cannot be
attained. The high cost has been worthwhile in recent years as domestic
and export prices have been rising and a substantial margin has separated
buying and selling prices per kg.
Productivity
11.       Productivity in the cattle herds is low.  Calving rates 40% to 50%
and calf mortality rates of 10% to 15% reflect the stress which deteriorating
conditions on the range have put on breeding stock. The long distances
which cattle now have to travel to water, the practice of night herding,
depriving stock of full grazing time and the minimal subsistence grazing on
which they exist for long periods during dry season mean that animals take
longer to mature, and come into breeding condition much less frequently than
under better management conditions. The practice of milking the cows also
means that calves are starved, and contributes to increased death rates in
young stock. Sheep husbandry is generally of a higher standard than cattle
management and productivity in the flock of Sudanese desert sheep is high with
a reported average of three lamb crops in two years in the Blue Nile Province.
Off-take Rates
12.       Cattle off-take rates in the Sudan vary throughout the production
areas; for the Sudan as a whole estimates are ranging between 7% to 10%.
Sheep off-take rates show similar variations but on somewhat higher percentage.
Nnimal Health
i3.       Animal disease problems in the Sudan are similar to that of the
whole sub-saharan region of Africa extending to the Atlantic. The high
incidence of disease, transhumant and nomadic systems of production, the lack
of a road network and difficulty of wet-season transport and communication



ANNEX 2
Page 7
limit the extent to which the small but highly professional veterinary service
is able to cope with the problems of animal health. The diseases of major
economic importance which affect the livestock industry of the Sudan and which
affect the proposed production program area: rinder-pest; bovine and caprine
pleuropneumonia; blue tongue; foot and mouth disease; trypanosomiasis; the
tick borne diseases (including heart water); internal and other forms of
parasitism; haemorrhagic septicemia; blackquarters; anthrax; brucelosis; and
mal-nutrition.
14.       Government services for the livestock industry are centered on the
Ministry of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources (Annex 15). The Ministry's
Department of Animal Resources consists of: Animal Health Services, Quarantine
and Epizootiology Services, Veterinary Research and Laboratory Services, Animal
Production Research Services. The staff of the Veterinary Field Services com-
prise: 150 veterinary officers, 160 veterinary assistants and 1,000 stock-men,
working from 250 field facilities. A reporting system enables the monitoring
of the disease situation in the provinces. The Field Services are also
responsible for the further development of the stockroute from Nyala to
Khartoum and for making provision for water, fodder and disease control on
this 1,200 km long trek.
15.       The Quarantine and Epizootiology Services are responsible for:2
(i) the establishment of the disease free zone covering about 400,000 km
of the Sudan and the quarantine stations at Kadaro, Wadi Halfa and Port
Sudan; (ii) for the control of infectious disease (iii) for the abattoirs
at Omdourman, Kadaro and Port Sudan (to be constructed) and (iv) for the
training of 250 meat inspectors over the coming 5 year period.
16.       The Veterinary Research and Laboratory Services are responsible for
the running of the Central Veterinary Research Laboratory which includes
diagnostic vaccine production, research and training of laboratory staff. In
1973/74 the vaccine laboratory produced the following vaccines (in million
doeses): Rinderpest 4.4; Haemorrhagic Septicaeminia 2.2; Blackleg 0.8;
Newcastle 0.7; Fowl Pox 0.1; Fowl Cholrea 0.02+; C.B.P.P. 0.3. Five regional
laboratories have been designated and partly established and specific roles
in animal disease research have been allocated to each.
17.       In the export Quarantine station of Khartoum (Kadero) cattle
are vaccinated against haemorrhagic septicemia and anthrax in addition
to rinderpest while sheep are vaccinated against anthrax. The animals
are kept for two weeks in the quarantine station and they are then
exported. Should an outbreak of contagious disease occur during this
period, export is stopped. Preparation of consignments of meat, prior to
export by plane, are also subject to strict supervision by the Sudan
Veterinary Service until delivery has been effected.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
Slaughterhouses
18.       Until recently the only operational slaughterhouse in the Sudan
was the Municipal Slaughterhouse at Omdourman (Khartoum). A new export
slaughterhouse at Kadero with a daily capacity of 250 cattle and 1,000 sheep
(one 10 hour shift) is ready to go into operation when the export ban is
lifted. Its capacity will increase by 50% when the chilling department is
completed. Tenders have been invited for a new slaughterhouse at Port Sudan
planned for half the capacity of Kadero.
Quarantine and Export Control Measures
19.       The Sudan has developed a system of animal health precautions
applied during the collection of animals for export. For a number of years
special facilities have been provided at supervised quarantine yards at
Khartoum for feeding northern cattle for export, have been provided at super-
vised quarantine yards at Khartoum while a system of feedlot operations on a
commercial basis by the private sector has more recently been developed in
another area close to Khartoum. Movements of cattle for export are controlled
in the province of origin. The animals are vaccinated against rinderpest,
haemorrhagic septicemia and anthrax and, after a period of seven days, if they
are moving within the country, and after fifteen days if they are for export;
they are marked by branding.
Hide and Skin Processing
20.       There are many small rural tanneries which process about 1 million
hides and skins per year into crude leather for local use; there are also two
industrial tanneries in Khartoum, one with a capacity of 400 hides and 1,000
sheep skins per day and the other with a capacity of 850 hides and 3,000 sheep
or goat skins per day. A third tannery with a capacity of 1,300 hides and
3,250 skins per day, the majority to be sold for export in the pickled or wet
blue form, is also planned at Wad Medani. A pilot tannery at Nyala with a
capacity of 50 hides and 200 sheep/goat skins per day functions as a training
unit for rural tanners.
Major Livestock Institutions
The Animal Production Public Corporation
21.       This parastatal corporation was established in May of 1975
for the purpose of:
-    participating in the general planning of animal production
activities;
-    exercising supervision of production in the subsidiary
corporations within the framework of the country's general
policy for animal production;



ANNEX 2
Page 9
ensuring optimum utilization of available natural resources
and investment on sound commercial economic and scientific
lines.
The Livestock and Meat Marketing Commission
22.       On October 17, 1976 the Livestock and Meat Marketing Commission
was accepted by the Council of Ministers and established by Decree of the
Prime Minister, as a supporting agency to the Supreme Council for Produc-
tion and Trade under the supervision of the Minister of Agriculture, Food
and Natural Resources.
(a) The functions of the commission are to be:
(i) to undertake studies and surveys and to collect data on
external markets, production and local consumption;
(ii) to provide advice and consultancy services to Government
producers and consumers;
(iii) through its Board of Directors to be ultimately responsible
for the issuing of licenses in cooperation with the Ministry
of Trade and Supply, the Bank of Sudan and the Bureau of
the Public Prosecutor.
(b) The Board of Directors of the Commission would consist of:
The Director General of the Commission who will be the
Chairman Representatives of: the Department of Animal
Resources; the Ministry of Trade and Supply; the Ministry
of Finance, Planning and National Economy; the Ministry of
Local Peoples Administration; the Ministry of Transport and
Communication; the Ministry of Cooperatives; the Bank of
Sudan; the Director General of the Animal Production Public
Corporation; two Representatives of Livestock Producers and
Meat Traders, and a Technical Specialist.
23.       The objectives of the Commission would be the formulation and
implementation of a national meat policy, the organization of livestock and
livestock products marketing in the Sudan and abroad and to propose to the
government the relevant actions to be taken in this regard. The Government
has requested that the Railway component of the originally proposed Western
Savannah Development Project as well as the Technical Assistance Project
proposed for UNDP financing and other aspects of the Commission activities
be appraised as a separate project for financing by IDA. Appraisal was ac-
complished in March 1977. The establishment of the Commission is particularly
fortunate at a time when the Government is embarking on an expansion of its
livestock development program. The commission would be instrumental in
planning and coordinating the orderly development of this important industry
and thereby to gain the confidence of foreign private and public investors.

Training of Professional Staff
24.       To overcome the need for professional staff for the veterinary
services the faculty of veterinary medicine at Khartoum University is in-
creasing its graduate out-turn to 100 a year. Animal production training
at the professional level is given to undergraduates in veterinary medicine
and agriculture by the Department of Animal Production. Intermediate level
training in the animal production and health field is given at the Kuku
Veterinary and Animal Husbandry Technical Training Center established in 1972.
This Center is intended to turn out some 60 Veterinary and Animal Husbandry
Technical Assistants yearly after a two to three years course. The training
Center is at present handicapped by the lack of a well developed training and
demonstration farm of its own on which students can receive practical train-
ing. An additional training center will be established at Nyala. A training
course in meat inspection and hygiene at present being conducted at Omdourman
Abattoir will be expanded. and move to the recently opened Kadaro Abattoir.
Finally training of head stockmen and assistant stockmen is located at
Omdourman Veterinary Training School.

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
The Project Area 1/
A. General
1.        The Project would be located in South Darfur Province (Province),
the most westerly province of the Sudan. The Province's western boundary
forms the international frontier with2Chad and the Central African Empire. It
has a total area of about 160,000 km , divided between four administrative
districts: Western - main Central, Southern, and Eastern. The capital of the
Province is Nyala which has 2 population of about 60,000.  The Project area
extends over about 65,000 km or slightly over 1/3 of the Province; would in-
clude mostly the Eastern District, which is now subdivided into Abu Matariq
and Ed Da'ein Rural Councils. In addition, those eastern parts of Southern
District south of the 12th parallel, which were not surveyed during the
southern Darfur Land Use Planning Survey (HTS, 1974), have been included.
Furthermore during unusually wet years some pastoralists from the Project
area would continue to visit parts of North Darfur Province. These areas
were not, however, included in the Project area. (Maps, IBRD 12474 and .12475).
Vegetation
2.         The vegetation of the area is low rainfall woodland savannah which
occurs in the Sudan where rainfall ranges between 300 mm and 1,000 mm per
annum. In the drier parts, the majority of trees are thorny, with species
of acacia predominating. In the areas of greater rainfall, broad-leaved
deciduous trees become predominant, but the range of species is limited and
thorny trees are usually present. There are more annual than perennial
grasses in all land systems, with the exception of the Bahr and the regebas
within the Regeba land system. Three formations are recognised as belonging
to this low rainfall savannah type:
(a) thorn scrub formation of the drier parts of the
basement and Baggara land systems. Thorny acacia
species of low stature are dominant in this formation;
(b) thorny savannah formation occupies the more humid
parts of the basement land system and the majority of
the Baggara and Regeba land systems. Tall acacia
species are dominant, but other tree species, includ-
ing broad-leaved deciduous species are found;
1/   The Project area is identical to the "program" area, i.e. the total area
to be covered by both the Project and a possible Phase II project.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
(c) savannah woodland formation is confined to the gently
undulating sand sheet areas of the Habbaniya and
Ma'aliya Qoz in the Qoz Land System. Non-thorny
and broad-leaved trees are dominant.
Tribal Groups
3.        The main tribes in the Project area are the Rizeiqat and Ma'aliya.
The two tribes have much in common, but are distinguished by their different
degrees of dependence on animal wealth and cultivation and by the correspond-
ing differences in attitudes to the natural resources they possess. The
Rizeiqat is the largest tribe, both in numbers of people and in animal wealth.
About 50 percent of the tribe lead a nomadic life. The settled population
which are Rizeqat share most of the values of the nomads and have free access
to the natural resources (mainly land and water). The Ma'aliya have a higher
percentage of settled population and are less dependent on animal wealth
than the Rizeiqat. Recently, however, there has been an increase in animal
ownership, mostly for trading purposes or as an alternative investment.
Rights to cultivation are more rigidly controlled in the Ma'aliya area, where
permission to cultivate is given by a land sheikh under certain conditions.
Population
4.        Population figures for the Project area are highly unreliable and
are based on available best estimates. The findings of the 1973 census
are not yet released and the previus census, performed 1955-56, is obsolete.
Data about the population in the Project area are derived from the 1975 March
and October aerial censuses conducted using the techniques developed by
Resource Management and Research Ltd., of Nairobi (RMR). These showed that a
total of about 360,000 people are in the Project area during the dry season,
half of which are transhumant pastoralists and half sedentary cultivators.
About 30,000 of the transhumants should be considered to be only temporarily
in the area during the dry season; they are located mostly the north-east
part of the Province, but have a permanent homeland in neighboring areas.
They do not usually move as far south as the Bahr, but depend on the graz-
ing available around the wateryards. It is expected that almost all the
150,000 pastoralists or 25,000 families permanently living in the area would
be affected by the Project. Furthermore, about 60,000 sedentary cultivators
or 10,000 families would also be affected by the Project, or 1/3 of all such
sedentary farmers in the area. The degree of involvement would vary and would
gradually increase in intensity. The Project would initially render simple
services (animal health, etc.) and develop gradually toward organized pastoral
type advanced livestock production.
5.        Seasonal Distribution of Population.  The maximum density of popu-
lation in the Project area occurs when the nomads reach the dar 1/ area in
June/ July and again in October/November. In the dry season, the nomadic
population is concentrated in the Bahr and Regeba areas. During the rainy
1/   Homeland.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
season, part of the nomadic family, especially older women and children, join
the sedentary population until after the harvest when they rejoin the nomadic
camps (farigs). However, in the rainy season, great numbers of people move
north beyond the northern boundary of the area with their animals, so this
period records the lowest population density of the whole year (Annex 2).
Among the sedentary population, the degree of stability increases towards the
middle of the rainy season while people are cultivating.
6.        Migration into and out of the area is difficult to quantify.  Sur-
veys conducted by Hunting Technical Services indicate that in-migration ex-
ceeds out-migration in the last 20 years. Migration to the towns and agri-
cultural production schemes of East Central Sudan undoubtedly takes place.
Communities of Rizeiqat have been recorded at Qala on Nahal and Hawata in
Kassala Province (Kadouf, 1975). However, the Rizeiquat are relatively
prosperous compared with other seasonal migrants from the west. They share
with the Ma'aliya a dislike of manual agricultural labor. The recruiting
station set up at Ed Da'ein for the recruitment of seasonal workers for the
Gezira was closed due to lack of response. There are three main sources of
migration into the area: the West, Northern Darfur, and the Dinka areas to
the south. Many of these people come on a temporary basis as agricultural
laborers. The migrants from the West include people of West African origin.
They work in the main settlements taking various jobs at different times of
the year. Many of them work in the oil processing mills at Ed Da'ein. In
recent years, low rainfall in Northern Darfur has encouraged migration from
the north. These people originally came to the Rizeiqat homeland as laborers,
but many have settled as cultivators and have formed their own villages.
Water Resources
7.        In the Savanna Belt, all settlements are located close to or within
easy reach of natural watering points or artificial water supplies. The
stockroutes are carefully selected by nomadic pastoralists between dependable
water points, either natural or artifical. In the rural areas, the perennial
and seasonal streams and natural lakes and ponds supplemented by shallow
wells are the principal sources of water. The latter are usually located
in the dry beds of wadis.. Since 1947, the Rural Water Corporation (RWC)
has constructed a network of boreholes reaching into the Nubian and Umm
Rawaba sandstone aquifers under the sandy qoz areas. RWC also constructs
hafirs, which are artifically excavated and constructed reservoirs into which
surface run-off is diverted for storage. The hafirs are usually in the
clay soil areas.
8.        During the summer monsoon rain water is freely available in pools
lying on the surface and the population can therefore disperse over a wide
area. When the rains recede and surface water dries up, both people and
animals concentrate around perennial wells and natural or artificial pools.
For land to be cultivable, it must be within 12-15 km of permanent water,
as certain tasks have to be performed before the rains break and after
they have ceased (e.g. land clearing and harvesting). Approximately the



ANNEX 3
Page 4
same area limitations are imposed upon cattle herding, because although
cattle on migration can travel distances greater than 30 km in 48 hours,
the time spent walking is at the expense of grazing. The introduction
of tubewells in recent years has allowed sedentary communities to expand
into previously waterless and therefore uncultivated areas. Owners of
large herds of cattle are obliged to migrate southwards to the Bahr and
Regeba land systems during the dry season. Apart from a few pools, peren-
nial surface water for cattle is found only in the Bahr el Arab.
Land Tenure
9.        Under the Unregistered Land Act of 1970, nearly all land in the
Project area is Government owned. In Dar Ma'aliya in the north of Eastern
District, nearly all land is now allocated. Holdings are sub-divided between
heirs and rights are sometimes transferred with a form of payment. Continu-
ous cultivation may result in permanent acquisition of cultivation rights;
thus, absence may involve loss of rights (the exact circumstances under which
this would occur are imprecise). For example, among the Rizeiqat, rights to
a specific plot are not necessarily retained once cultivation is abandoned.
Thus the cultivator has no easy way of ensuring that others will not re-clear
the land before a planned period of bush fallow expires. Inheritance laws
are likewise vague. The customary view is that on death the right to culti-
vate passes to the descendant in most need. As scarcity of land increases,
so also does the number of disputes. In the event of a dispute, the prin-
ciples of Islamic law are applied and land is divided in equal shares among
male descendants and in half shares among females.
10.       Under customary law, there is free access to the open range.
Livestock owners are liable when damage to crops occurs, but free access to
water and grazing is assured. In practice different tribes tend to limit
their travels to more or less defined routes; flexibility prevails, however,
for rain pools and other water resources fill irregularly from year to year.
Rights to monopolise uncropped land by fence or force are not recognised;
frequent attempts to enclose are made by settled cultivators, but without
official sanction. These conflicting interests often given rise to bitter
disputes, and violence is at times the inevitable result.
January 28, 1977
