Page 1
SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Social and Political Organization in South Darfur Province 1/
I.  Social Institutions
A. Nomadic Pastoralists
1.        A complex social organization has evolved among the nomadic pas-
toralists; this organization of all phases of community life grew out of
the necessity to adapt to a sometimes harsh and uncompromising environment,
and out of the need to organize production, consumption and capital accumu-
lation at both the family and the tribal levels. The nomadic society is an
example of a way of life almost perfectly in tune with the precarious balance
of nature. The keystone of the survival of this social structure was the
total cooperation demanded by all members of the community. The pastoral
nomads move continuously with their families to find water and pasturage for
their animals. This cycle of movement is usually not a haphazard wandering
but is restricted to areas generally used by the specific tribe, and the
length of stay in one location is dependent on pasture conditions and water
supplies. The areas regularly used by specific tribes may be claimed as
tribal homelands. However, tribes do not have legal title to these lands; in
many cases, traditional rights are based on water point utilization.
The Farig
2.        The basic element of the social structure is the farig, or  camp
In the social sense it means a herding unit, nominally based on the agnatic 2/
relation of heads of domestic units traced from a common ancestor. The farig
is the minimal lineage. During the dry season when the nomadic groups are
concentrated in the South in the Bahr el Arab region, several farigs may
group together to form one "iyal ragl", - a group of descendants of one man,
usually composed of a maximum of three generations. Their houses are placed
next to each other, and they maintain one "rakuba" or "dara" - a place for
guests or visitors. The men who gather there are the farig elder, his
married sons, and occasionally his in-laws and other relatives.
1/   Based on findings of Huntings Technical Service and the National
Council for Research in the Sudan.
2/   Male decent on the father's side.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
3.        The elder is the spokeman by virtue of his age or wealth, and
has the authority to take decisions on behalf of his group. This is par-
ticulary true in cases with well known precedents, otherwise the other
members will be consulted. The herding policy is the concern of the whole
group but special attention is paid to the elders' opinion. Each married
man has the right of disposal over his own cattle. Animals owned by women
are marketed by male relative on their behalf. The large farig composed
of several iyal ragl is a dry season occurrence. In the rain season each
iyal ragl camps independently.
4.        There are several advantages gained from this grouping.  Division
of labor within the iyal ragl allows the men to follow interests, including
leisure time which is shared within the group. Secondly, through connection
with the groups, livestock owners can reduce their tax liability, for in
practice the tax assessment is a declining function of herd size; thus mem-
bers of the iyal ragl register under one name, that of the group elder
(para 5). Thirdly, within the farig, newly estabished households are
assisted through the early stages of development when their labor resources
are inadequate to manage independently.
Ownership of Cattle
5.        Almost all cattle owners originally obtained cattle from their
fathers or relatives as gifts on occasions such as birth, circumcision,
marriage, etc. Breeding animals owned by a child are not disposed of ex-
cept in extreme conditions. The father acts as guardian of his sons' wealth.
The number of animals owned expands through natural increase and purchase,
and later by inheritance. The main concern of the father is to build up
a herd for each son, and to test the boy's managerial ability during his
early life. About forty head of cattle is considered to meet the subsistance
needs of a domestic unit. Families with less must seek other income sources
or hold cattle for cultivating relatives. Due to these complex arrangements it
is extremely difficult to determine who owns a herd. For tax purposes, cattle
are registered under the name of the group elder. In theory, tax should be
paid on each adult animal, but in practice, taxes tend to be distributed
equally among owners without reference to the actual number of animals held
(Annex 19). This is acceptable to the nomads' value system in which there is
little social differentiation, ostentatious consumption or display of material
goods. It is well understood, therefore, that once tax has been assessed,
marginal increases in the herd have no effect on the tax levied. Small
owners therefore tend to avoid taxation by hiding behind their father, brother
or any close kin, nomad or settler.
Family Structure
6.        Within the transhumant community, the reproductive unit of husband,
wife or wives together with their children, does not necessarily correspond
to the domestic unit of the sedentary society. Although the ideal is for



ANNEX 4
Page 3
each domestic unit to be self-sufficient, cooperation and support is quite
common, especially in the early and later stages of family development.
All households are non-viable at their establishment. The husband's father
generally retains control of the cattle until the natural increase in the
herd allows the young family to manage and subsist independently. In farigs
which combine livestock and cultivation, it is often the newly married couples
who herd the cattle during the rains, while their parents or an elder brother
cultivate. Where the labor supply of the original family is plentiful,
the new household may cultivate from the time of its establishment in order
to avoid animal sales and to build up the herd with money obtained from the
sale of groundnuts. This depends on the presence of other sons in the farig
to assist with herding animals.
7.        In the next stage of family development, the nomadic household
should ideally subsist on the herd, as the length of marriage (ten years
or more) should have been sufficient to provide an adequate labor supply
and natural increase in the herd. A second marriage might increase family
numbers and extend the period of viability for the domestic unit. The
combination of stock-keeping and cultivating is easier for polygamous house-
holds, who need not depend on labor from other households. Often one wife
and her children cultivate, while the others move with the cattle during the
wet season to the North. In these cases, the husband may assist in the
initial stages of cultivation before moving north, but in general most of the
work and decision-making related to cultivation is the wife's responsibility.
Occasionally the elder son will be given the responsibility to take the cattle
north with his mother and young brothers and sisters, while his father stays
with the younger wife. Families reunite during the dry season at the Bahr el
Arab.
8.        As the children become of marriageable age, the cycle begins
again. If the married son takes over herding responsibilities, the original
family often cultivates larger areas of millet in order to provide grain for
their own food supply and for the newly established family of their son.
When most of the children of the original family are married and no more
births are likely, the aging parents become dependent on their married sons,
who, by this stage, have usually been given full control of the cattle they
are due to inherit. However, the authority and respect given to the parents
is not diminished. Divorced or widowed women are usually supported by a
brother, son or nephew. Viability of domestic units is rare in the establish-
ment and expansion phases, and in family decline. However, in the middle
phases, when family labor is plentiful, animal resources are usually adequate
to support a family without other income raising activities. When they are
inadequate, the flexible organization of the farig allows for cooperation
between households and specialization in the activity most suited to their
stage of development.
B. Crop Cultivation
9.        The migratory households need grain, which has to be obtained
from the market if there is insufficient surplus labor to produce it them-
selves. By agreement with their kin, some members might be released for



ANNEX 4
Page 4
cultivation to provide grain which would enable them to reduce sales of
breeding stock for grain purchases, and, if possible, raise money to pur-
chase productive females. In this case, cultivation would be considered
part of the family enterprise, the objective being the expansion of the
herd. Another common arrangement is for two brothers to alternate cul-
tivation and herding, one staying with the old father in the village and
the other migrating.
10.       For those families involved in crop production, the cultivation
of millet for household consumption is the first priority; to this end there
is close cooperation between the members of the domestic unit (i.e. parents
and children). The average family has a production target of about ten 100 kg
sacks of grain. Deficits are made up by "gubraka" crops; these are quick
matur-ing sorghum and okra cultivated by women in the vicinity of the house.
The average cropped area (excluding gubraka) per household is about 9 makhomas
(5.4 ha) of which two-thirds are devoted to millet (Annex 5).
11.       The area cultivated is related to the need and capacity of the
household, which depend on the stage the family has reached in the what
we may call "family development cycle". The first stage is the nuclear
family, composed of husband, wife and children working together. Generally
the husband makes the decisions relating to production, unless in polygamous
households a wife has a separate plot of her own. The second stage occurs
when a son wishes to marry. He may cultivate a cash crop on his own account
to raise money for the marriage, while his parents provide the grain supply.
In most cases, the father will assist his son in the cost of the marriage
and continue to be responsible for providing grain to the young family.
The son and his wife will concentrate on cultivating cash crops until their
own family expands (up to about four years). With the third stage they will
then become independent of the parents, choose their own place of residence
and cultivate both millet and groundnuts. The husband is now considered a
fully responsible man (ragil). The father retains some influence over the
son in community matters, as a member of the older generation, but this
does not extend to internal affairs of his son's household.
12.       The family is fully mobilized during the sowing and weeding period.
All other operations are divided between the sexes. For example, men are
usually responsible for land clearing and women for tending the gubraka,
looking after the house, threshing and winnowing. In the July/August period,
first weeding of millet and sowing of groundnuts coincide (in addition to
herding animals if the household has any). During this time the family might
move out from the village to save time travelling to the cultivated area.
There is little reliance on wage labor. Formerly, migrant labor came from the
Bahr el Ghazal, but recent disputes between members of the Rizeiquat and the
Dinka tribes have discouraged the Dinka from moving north in search of work.
The decline in the labor supply has effectively boosted wage levels, so that
few people are now able to afford hired labor. Payment for weeding is in-
variably based on piece work (i.e. ESd 4-5 per makhomas, US$17-21 per ha, at
the peak of the season), plus subsistence. This involves three or four days
work for an adult male. The working day does not exceed six hours, beginning



ANNEX 4
Page 5
after 6 a.m. and ending about noon. Large nafirs or communal work parties
are uncommon in the area but relatives and friends often give mutual support.
In those instances when nafirs are organized, it is on the basis of recipro-
city of phyical work.
13.       Those who have no savings in the form of stored grain and no live-
stock which can be sold for cash have no alternative but to hire themselves
to other producers or mortgage their crop to a merchant against advances
(sheil). This increases their dependence in subsequent years. This situa-
tion can develop following crop failure due to drought, pest and disease
outbreaks or illness. With the disintegration of the pre-market economy,
associated with the introduction of groundnuts as a cash crop in the last
fifteen years, more and more households are caught in this "poverty trap".
C. Interrelationships between Pastoralists and Cultivators
14.       Between the settled cultivators and the pastoralists there is
a network of social and economic relations. it is rare to find a settled
family which is not connected in some way to a nomadic one. Pastoralists
leave milking cows with the cultivators during the rains. This helps to
reduce expenditure by the cultivators on grain and provides cash from the
sale of milk and semn (butter oil). In turn the nomad family may receive
grain following the harvest. Due to the difficulties associated with.herd-
ing animals in the neighborhood of villages due to insufficient pasture and
water, cattle may be entrusted with a nomadic relative. The relative has
the benefit of the milk, butter oil, etc., and may receive additional in-
centives in the form of grain, animals, cash and clothing (often even a
herder may be provided by the owner). Partnerships exist where cattle is
loaned to a pastoralist against an agreed share in calves. People who work
in trade or government service and have relatives in the pastoralist sector
might send money for the purchase of cattle which are kept in the nomadic
herd.
15.       Thus it can be seen that the Savannah economy is not neatly
divided into two discrete sectors - crop production and livestock produc-
tion - but is in fact a continuum. The forms of social organization des-
cribed represent the ends of a spectrum in which the cultivator on depleted
soil without livestock is the most impoverished and the nomad with a large
herd the most prosperous. The basic of the social model is that cattle cons-
titute a store of wealth in an economy which offers no alternatives for saving
and investment. The simple technology of agriculture prevents a successful
cultivator from expanding his enterprise; cattle on the other hand can be
purchased by the sale of crop surpluses, and with good management they in-
crease in number and value. Thus a successful cultivator becomes first a



ANNEX 4
Page 6
transhumant, since he has to leave his village to obtain pasture for his
animals during certain times of the year, and if his herd increases to a
certain point, it becomes his main occupation.
16.       The opposite transition also takes place.  A nomad may lose many
of his cattle by disease, drought or other forms of natural disaster, so
that he is obliged to place greater emphasis on cultivation in order to
obtain the necessary resources to rebuild his herd. If he continues to
lose animals, he may become solely dependent on cultivation. Furthermore,
the decreasing returns from animal husbandry have resulted in the increasing
marginality of the pastoral economy.
17.       The validity of the model is borne out by a comparison of the
southern and eastern districts of South Darfur Province. In the former
the increasing erosion of the pastoral existence has lead to the adoption
of crop cultivation, which in turn has further aggravated the situation
and created a vicious circle. In the Eastern Council area the Rizeiqat
tribe (which constitutes the vast majority of the population by virtue of
its policy of expansion through assimilation of people from other tribes)
has thus far successfully delayed the deterioration of the nomadic system.
However, even in this area the equilibrium remains unstable, and if the
proposed development program should fail, this area too would follow the
pattern set in the south.
II. Political Institutions
A. Traditional Institutions
18.       Historically, an important feature of the traditional social
system has been the concentration of judicial and administrative authority
in the post of the omda (leader of the administrative sub-division of the
tribe). Apart from his official status, he was an hereditary leader to
whom people came to resolve disputes and obtain advice. In these tribal
matters he was accustomed to use his initiative, reporting to Government
only when he considered it necessary.
19.       In theory, The Peoples Local Government Act of 1971 diminished
the authority of the leaders; in practice, however, it may have increased it.
Although the sheikhs (Government-appointed officials traditionally respons-
ible to the omda) are now directly responsible to the Rural Councils (para 34)
and not to the omda, and his judicial authority no longer receives legal
sanction, he remains the acknowledged head of his tribal section. The people
still seek his arbitration in disputes rather than travel long distances
to the official courts and face delays and legal fees. The new arrange-
ments probably make him more careful in seeking solutions which will receive
popular support. Any judgements which might decrease his popularity, tend



ANNEX 4
Page 7
to be passed on to the legally recognised authorities. The omda is expected
to defend the group's interest in disputes with other groups. Following the
abolition of the post of nazir (tribal leader), the symbol of tribal solidarity
and power, the omda has become the rallying point for local (tribal) sec-
tional interests. The power of the omda has also increased as a result of
the pressure on land resources; it is his traditional right to allocate all
unsettled or unclaimed land within the omodiya.
20.       A sheikh is appointed by Government to collect the tribal animal
tax. Formerly he was responsible to the omda but now he must report to
the council officer, although he will often seek the advice of the omda
first in order to formulate the best strategy for the interview. His post
brings him little political authority; any importance he may have is the
result of his position as a tribal elder.
21.       The mandoub (originally a personal representative of the nazir,
responsible for the collection of certain dues) works with the omdas and
sheikhs in collecting the tribal animal tax, especially from the nomads.
In recent years the traditional duties of mandoub have not only survived
the abolition of the nazir's position, but have been extended to include
control over the use of pasture and hashab resources. It is noteworthy that
the administrative solution has been based on the extension of traditional
institutions rather than the creation of new offices within the framework of
the Local Government Act.
22.       There are five pasture mandoubs on the stockroutes.  Their job
is to supervise nomadic movements in the Bahr el Arab region and see that
Rural Council orders are adhered to. The post was introduced following the
1974 Safaha Conference to resolve the dispute between the Rizeiqat and the
Malwal Dinka tribes. These mandoubs are neither paid Government officials
nor elected tribal representatives, but partly combine both these somewhat
conflicting roles. The mandoubs are usually owners of large herds. Their
task is to communicate orders from the Rural Council to people in the area
and report back on the local situation. The mandoubs do not have legal
powers (such as arrest and detention) to enforce a Government order; neither
do they have the prestige enjoyed by paid officials such as sheikhs and
omdas.
23.       In addition to the tax collecting sheikhs (sheikh el daftar),
there are others who have honorary and unofficial titles as sheikhs. A man
may be given the title of sheikh as the first settler in an area, or because
he claims rights over large areas of land (sheikh el ard) and arranges its
allocation for annual cropping and hashab collection. These roles are local-
ized and usually hereditary or, perhaps, vested in the elder or wisest in the
community.
Arbitration Committee
24.       The Arbitration committees are unofficial committees which filter
out complaints and grievances before they become the subject of major dis-
putes, thus saving the administration and courts a great deal of time and



ANNEX 4
Page 8
trouble. Membership of the committee depends upon the gravity of the case.
If the dispute is of a local and petty nature, the committee will be made
up of elders of the farig or village, but when inter-tribal matters are
involved, members should be omdas, important sheikhs or people of standing
in the tribe. Unless these people are present, the decisions of the com-
mittee are not binding. Once the tribal leaders have consented to the
arbitrators' ruling, the honor of the tribe is at stake.
Land Rights
25.       The customary rights governing cultivation of crops, harvesting
of hashab, hunting, gathering or roots, fruits, honey, etc., and grazing
and watering of livestock have evolved over centuries. The tribal elders
are the custodians of these unwritten laws and their knowledge is the basis
of their authority. Every piece of land, however remote, is the subject
of customary rights. These rights are jealously guarded; wars are fought to
defend them. They consequently invoke the Unregistered Land Act of 1970
(U.L.A.) which declared that all waste, forest and unregistered lands are to
be regarded as Government Land.
26.       Kadouf (1975) has reported on customary land law in Kassala
Province, where the general principles appear to be the same as in Southern
Darfur. The main points are:
(a)  In former days, land was owned by the whole community
and held in trust by the Nazir. Following the U.L.A.
(1970) this ownership has passed to the Government.
It is widely recognized that all land as such is
Government property held in trust by the Omda and
therefore all other persons may be regarded as mere
interest holders. Although people recognise and
admit the above, they may at ti1es insist on their
personal right to use a piece of land on the basis
that it has been theirs for many years and this is
invariably upheld by the authorities.
(b) A clear distinction is drawn between the right to
cultivate the soil (i.e. sow and harvest annual crops)
The right to cultivate the soil is usually vested
in individuals once the land has been cleared from
bush. The ownership of hashab trees is also private
once it has been registered, but the enjoyment of the
right of way to water or grazing land are said to be
natural rights, open to any member of the community



ANNEX 4
Page 9
The rights to collect wood for domestic fires, fencing,
etc., are also common, except that only dead wood may
be taken from a hashab tree. Since ownership of lands
does not exist, it follows that the ownership of grass
does not flow from the possession of a genein (hashab
garden) or agricultural land automatically - that is
because cutting and using grass are communal rights. If
the owner of the field wishes to take grass from his
field for his own use, he must first cut it and bundle
it; in which case none may touch it.
(c) The rights to graze crop residues are likewise held in
common unless it (the gassab) is first gathered and
bundled. Nobody has the right to erect fences to keep
out animals from grazing natural pastures. People
often attempt to do so, but their right to compensa-
tion is not upheld by the courts should the fences be
broken and animals enter.
27.       Among the Ma'aliya, Birgid and Rizeiqat the customary laws govern-
ing land rights reflect the particular history of land settlement and use
of their tribal areas. At the time of the settlement of Dar Ma'aliya, the
perennial water resources were confined to scattered wells and Tebeldi
trees, hence there were no great concentrations of population. The Ma'aliya
thus acquired rights to vast areas of land.  The rights to clear and culti-
vate unallocated land were vested in the community, personified in the Omda
and delegated to land sheikhs. Cultivators seeking right to unallocated land
obtained them from the land sheikh.
28.       Among the Rizeiqat, interest in cultivation is relatively recent.
Hence the statement that all land is owned by the Government is more fre-
quently heard among those wishing to obtain land - although this attitude
quickly disappears as land is cleared and cultivated. The rate of clear-
ing is extremely rapid in South Darfur due to migration from North Darfur.
Further, interest in hashab planting has increased following recent price
rises. The present trend is towards the adoption of land tenure system
similar to that of their close neighbors.
Role of the Land Sheikh
29.       The land sheikh (Ma'aliya) or village sheikh (Rizeiqat) is the
person responsible for allocating land. In recently settled areas (e.g.
in the area of new boreholes) the post of land sheikh is honorary, con-
ferring status on the holder. The post of land or village sheikh is held
by an elder of the village. Before the sheikh may allocate cultivation
rights certain conditions must be fulfilled: the land must not be in dis-
pute; it must not restrict the extension of another person's cultivation;



ANNEX 4
Page 10
and the cultivator must agree to abide by customary law, e.g. payment of
zakat to the Land Sheikh. The sheikh has no authority to refuse to grant
a cultivator rights if the above conditions are fulfilled unless there
are grave reasons and these are upheld by the local community. Such an
incident rarely occurs. In cases where disputes over land reach the court,
the land sheikh will be called as a witness. Sheikhs often sit on arbitra-
tion committees for the settlement of disputes between neighboring villages.
(para 24).
Rights of the Cultivator
30.       Once permission has been granted according to local custom and
the area demarcated by the land sheikh, a newcomer can begin to clear his
land. In areas where the tenure system is not rigid, as in Dar Ma'aliya,
the cultivator will be simply told to demarcate the land himself. The
nearest land sheikh or his deputy should be aware of the boundaries in
case disputes arise later. The cultivator has full rights to sow and har-
vest once the area has been agreed and demarcated. In cases where the land
allocated is part of another cultivator's holding, the original right holder
may define certain conditions. This may involve a share of the produce
or, simply, akil go'om (feed and go) in which the cultivator can use the
land, but if he leaves the village he retains no rights over it., Cultivation
rights cannot be transferred without the permission of the original owner.
The rules which apply if a cultivator leaves the village differ according to
the place. In Dar Ma'aliya, the rights tend to continue, mainly because of
hashab, unless the cultivator expresses that he has no intention of returning.
The general rule elsewhere is that rights cannot be transferred until three
cultivating seasons have elapsed since the date of the last harvest. This
allows a cultivator the opportunity to return to his village should he
fail to establish himself in a new area. In Dar Ma'aliya there is no
limitation on the area which can be held after clearing the land, but in
Dar Rizeiqat the holding is limited to the cultivated area. Adjacent land
may be reserved by the cultivator to enable him to extend his cultivation.
Cultivation rights may be transferred from one person to another without
the authority of the sheikh. Thus on the infirmity or death of the right
holder, land naturally passes to relatives.
Communal Rights
31.       A fundamental precept of the Baggara (cattlemen) is that grazing
and water are free; no man may monopolise them by fencing or force. This
survival principle ensures that in years of inadequate or irregular rainfall,
a herder can move freely in search of pasture. Free access to free-standing
water is also guaranteed. Exceptions are:
(a) hand dug wells and adjacent clay hods (drinking trough
for animals) which are considered private to the builder
and the site is reserved from season to season; and



ANNEX 4
Page 11
(b) the dry season farig site, which is customarily reserved
from year to year.
In times of stress, the fraternal attitude among tribes inevitably breaks
down and attempts are made to intimidate outsiders. For example, the
Rizeiqat omdas blame other tribes for the overgrazing in Dar el Rizeiqat,
particularly the Fellata Mbororo.
32.       The customary laws described above were no doubt adequate for
safeguarding the production process in the pre-market economy and before
livestock and human populations built up to their present levels. Dis-
putes over land rights, particularly hashab, and conflicts between different
interest groups (tribes) over land rights are increasing. These problems
will not be solved by ad hoc local Government orders which are inevitably
based on inadequate information and often fail to take account of the con-
straints imposed by prevailing systems of land use. The way must be pre-
pared for fundamental changes in land law; these changes should ideally
allow for:
(a) Government ownership with individual rights of usufruct
for farmers in order to encourage the introduction of sus-
tained agricultural production. The leaseholder 1/
must have the right to rest part of the holding under
bush fallow for an adequate period without fear of los-
ing it. The conditions of land use rights must in-
clude also private rights over the bush fallow, crop
residues and forage, and the right to erect a fence
(Annex 5).
(b)  Government grant of long-term right of usufruct of the
range in order to permit the introduction of range manage-
ment and controlled stocking.
(c) Revision of the present systems relating to gumarabic
tree usage rights which are being abused as de facto
cultivation rights.
1/   This term may be somewhat misleading as the settlers are not
expected to be required to pay any rent over the Project period.
Rent would be collected only at a later date when the settlers
have become successfully established on the land. Land use
would most probably be organized as a right of usufruct.



ANNEX 4
Page 12
B. Modern Local Administration
33.       Before the Condominium Government 1/ gave official recognition
to tribal leaders, the leadership of the Baggara was dynamic but unstable.
Loyalty was based partly on agnatic kinship and partly on a client-patron
relationship. The Condominium Government froze this situation by recognis-
ing groups and leaders as they existed at that time. In the case of the
Ma'aliya, where the power structure was diffused, leaders were imposed
upon the people. The importance of agnatic descent increased because the
Government decided to work through agnatic groups and because security made
conflict between political and agnatic loyalty less likely. The standard
pattern was that of a nazir, supported by several omdas with a large number
of sheikhs below. All had magisterial powers exercising customary law,
and matters such as tax assessment and collection were their responsibility.
They were the agents of Government policy. The role of these leaders was
ambiguous. There was a belief that they could be used as part of the admin-
istrative hierarchy and still represent the people. However, few would claim
that it was an innovative bureaucracy, geared for the implementation of rural
development.
34.       In 1951 the Sudan adopted the Marshall report and created a nation-
wide system of Rural Councils. A Rural Council was set up at Ed D'ein which
served the area now called Eastern District Council. The objective was
to dismantle the scaffolding of central administration and allow self-governing
democracy to take root through: (a) the creation of a large number of local
councils to manage their own affairs in a spirit of self-reliance and to
initiate local development projects; (b)- the granting of greatly enhanced
powers to provincial commissioners, largely through ministerial decentral-
ization. For a number of reasons, the outcome was not as successful as was
expected.
35.       South Darfur Province was established in 1974 when the former
Darfur Province was split in two. Since the Peoples Local Government Act
of 1971, a process of decentralized. Government administration has been
introduced in an attempt to involve local people in decision making. A
pyramidal structure of Provincial, District, Town, Rural, Village and Nomad
Councils is being created on which elected and Government nominated members
sit. The provincial administration is headed by a Provincial Commissioner
(PC) who is head of the Provincial Executive Council (PEC), head of the
Sudan Socialist Union (SSU) in the Province, and who has ministerial status
(Chart, World Bank 16620). The PEC is responsible for compiling and admin-
istering the provincial budget (including all local and Government services)
and for legislation and administration. Assistant Commissioners represent
each of the major Government departments and receive technical support
1/   A governing body which exercised authority over the Sudan before
the country's independence (1956); it consisted of representatives
of the governments of Egypt and Great Britain.



ANNEX 4
Page 13
from parent ministry departments in Khartoum.  District and Rural Councils
are now in the process of being established throughout the Province; these
are ultimately intended to replace the traditional authorities who have
historically regulated all aspects of tribal life. At the present time,
however, the traditional authorities still play an important role in the
daily affairs of their people.
36.       The proposed Project would be located in the Eastern District of
South Darfur Province. This District now has a District Council, which pre--
sides over two Rural Councils - the Ed Da'ein and Abu Matariq Rural Councils.
In terms of Government services, Ed Da'ein dominates the Eastern District
Council. The Assistant Commissioner is based in Ed Da'ein and the rural
council has an executive officer and four assistant executive officers. Abu
Matariq has only one assistant executive officer at present.
37.       Each of the Rural Councils oversees several village/Nomad/
Section Councils. Ed Da'ein has 24 councils; these are:
Large                      Mledium                       Small
Ed Da'ein                 Kileikli                    Gad es Sid
Abu Karinka               Augabo                      El Gallabi
Adila                     Dar es Salaam               Akulkul
Hazrub                    Hillal                      El Ligam

Shaqq el Leyun              Umm Qereigo

Abu Matariq has 15 councils; these are:
Abu Matariz                      Haraza
Abu Gabra                        Far' el Habil
Hadkuk Hamad                      Safaha
Madkuk el Bashir                 Sarafaya
Abu Sinedira                      Sahala Nakata
Furdus                           Sinun
Goghan                           El Farash
El Rutrut
These councils are intended to be the primary local Government units.
Councillors are recruited from the various popular organizations (Village
Development Committees, Sudan Socialist Union, Youth and Women's Organiza-
tions), and Government officials in the area (school teachers, wateryard
clerks, etc.). One quarter of the total membership should be filled by



ANNEX 4
Page 14
women. The Rural Council Executive Officer is the secretary of the
councils. Meetings should be held bi-monthly. The Local Government
Act does not lay down the functions of the village councils, but basi-
cally they provide a forum for the mutual exchange of information between
the prople and the local government authorities, and help mobilise com-
munity support for the improvement of social services.
38.       It appears that the function of the councils is not yet clear
to the people or the councillors. Most of the day to day problems involv-
ing the rural economy are handled by the old institutions. A basic problem
arises from the fact that the Village Council has no budgetary powers.
Proposals from the council for services are inevitably turned back to the
councillors to implement on a "self-help" basis because of the shortage of
rural council funds. Moreover, effective development of the new adminis-
trative structure has been impaired mainly due to a lack of development
funding; for example, in 1974/75, the Province's approved capital budget
was iSd 200,000, of which bSd 150,000 had to be used for recurrent expenditures
due to a shortfall in local revenues. The implementation of these changes has
also been hampered by the lack of an overall planning framework. It is
evident that the local administrations will require significant strengthening
of both management and technical staff before they are capable of undertaking
the successful management of a major development program.
C. Future Role of Modern Local Government and
Traditional Insitutions
39.       As Abdel Ghaffar Muhammed Ahmed (1973) 1/ has pointed out, the word
"tribe" (or the Arabic synonym "gabila") suggests a primitive group, bound
by kinship under chiefly authoritarian rule, and this definition does not
fit the groups found in Central Sudan. The word "tribe" suggests a soli-
darity and uniformity which in reality does not exist. For example, the
Rizeiqat are popularly conceived as a nomadic pastoral group and the Ma'aliya
as a sedentary group of cultivators (Annex 3, para 3). These ideas are held
by outsiders as well as the people themselves, but they obsecure important
variations within the groups. The Rizeiqat and the Ma'aliya are best de-
fined as loosely affiliated political groups - shifting alliances of vested
interests bent on the exploitation of particular territorial resources. By
fostering and maintaining an identity, they are better able to retain control
over these land resources and extend them.
40.       The most pressing problem of this stage is in what manner Government
should link these groups to the development effort, for there is no reason
to believe that strong local interests are inimical to development provided
1/   Source:  National Council for Research in the Sudan.



ANNEX 4
Page 15
they are given the necessary responsibility, guidance and resources. First it
seems necessary to utilize to the fullest extent the traditional institutions
which clearly serve vital needs. In 1971 there was an attempt to sweep them
away with the Local Government Act. The attitude of Central Government was in
many respects justified, because they were associated with systems of land use
which Government wished to replace; however, the attempts to replace the
traditional rural institutions have thus far not been completely successful
(para 34). The norms, values and organization of the so-called "past-rule"
underline the response of local groups to Government initiatives. The majority
of political disputes are still settled in accordance with the traditional
systems. Although decisions may be finalized by the new Government institu-
tions, such as the People's Councils, People's Courts, etc., these bodies, to
a large degree, retain the procedures of the old system.
41.       The persistence of the traditional institutions is evidence of
their relevance to the prevailing systems of resource use. The omdas,
sheikhs, mandoubs, arbitration committees, etc., regulate production and
consumption and the distribution of wealth to a large extent within the rural
community. The Village Councils, whose powers have yet to be clearly defined,
appear to the mass of people to have little immediate relevance to the serious
business of stockkeeping and cultivating. It is no accident that the only
place that the Councils have taken root is in the principal market centers,
i.e. in a semi-urban environment, where they can influence Government policy
in relation to the provision of services (schools, hospitals, water supplies,
etc.).
42.       The envisaged modernization of agricultural production creates a
need for meaningful local participation in planning and implementation and
provides an opportunity to establish the Councils in rural areas. Wherever
possible, care should be taken to fashion the traditional roles of omdas,
sheikhs, mandoubs, etc., to fit the new system. The Local Government Act
(1971) must, however, provide the ideological and operational framework within
which these changes are to be brought about.
43.       In terms of the Project, this approach is particularly important
and relevant in relation to resettlement of farmers and the introduction of
agricultural production and range management systems including the demarcation
of migratory stock routes. Since it is clear that the allocation of grazing
right to individual groups of pastoralists goes against traditional concepts
of free grazing, it appears that the only valid approach would be to seek the
full participation of the pastoralists both during the planning and implemen-
tation stages and eventually to rely on them to supervise and enforce the
accepted interventions. It would appear that the traditional institutions have
a major role to play in all aspects of this development.

Housing
10.       It was assumed that housing would consist of Sudanese Government
standard houses. Staff earning from b Sd 3,000 per annum and above would
be provided with the standard Senior Grade House which has three bedrooms
and is designated as T-1. The T-2 or Middle Grade house has two bedrooms and
would be provided to staff whose salary ranged from L Sd 1,601 to L Sd 2,999.
Staff earning from L Sd 900 to E Sd 1,600 per year would be entitled to a
Junior Grade house of one bedroom and designated as T-3. Some ancilliary
staff earning less than that i Sd 900 would be entitled to a thatch house.
1/
II. Crop Production
Traditional Farms
11        It is estimated that at present an average farm family consists of
six persons (2.6 labor units) and cultivates 5.4 ha of crops with hand
tools. Two-thirds of the cropped area is sown with millet and one-third with
groundnuts. Sesame, sorghum and other minor crops are grown on only 3% of
the cropped area; they make a small contribution to crop production, and have
not been assessed separately in calculations of benefits.
Crop Yields
12.       There is great variations in crop yields from site to site and season
to season. The average yield of millet on traditional farms is estimated to be
400 kg grain per ha and the mean yield of groundnuts to be 900 kg unshelled.
Crop Production Without the Program
13.       Without the program, increasing population and a corresponding
increase in the cropped area will lead to reduction of the fallow period and
a decline in yields. Assuming that the present 6.25% per annum rate of popu-
lation growth continues, 2/ (3% natural increase and 3.25% due to immigration)
reserves of virgin land in the Baggara Land System will be exhausted in about
1985. Thereafter, there is likely to be movement of population out of the
area, but with a shorter fallow period, crop yields of the remaining farmers
will decline.
1/   The assumptions on crop production are based on data given in the prepara-
tion report and largely collected by Hunting Technical Services Limited
during Surveys of Eastern and Southern Districts of South Darfur Province
in 1974 and 1976. Data have been checked during appraisal and where found
necessary appropriate modifications have been made.
2/   HTS estimates.



ANNEX 5
Page 15
14.       Two alternative assumptions are made concerning crop yields after
year 10 of the program. Assumption I is that crop yields will decline at a rate
of 2.5% per annum and Assumption II is that they will decline at a rate of 5%
per annum. It is also assumed that annual subsistence production of 1,440 kg
of millet per family p.a. will be maintained as long as possible by increasing
the area sown to millet at the expense of the groundnuts area and that the
mean millet yield will fall to 240 kg grain per ha from the current estimated
level of 400 kg without the program at Year 30.
15.       It is assumed that in the Baggara Land System a family taps 600
hashab trees per annum and on the Qoz Land System 300 trees per annum, or a
total of 130 kg and 65 kg of gum arabic respectively (0.22 kg per tree)-see
Annex 6, para 12.
The Settlement Unit
16.       There would be eleven new settlement units, each based on a single
wateryard serving a cultivation unit of 500 families. A further 19 settlements
would be based on wateryards that have already been opened. Because unregula-
ted settlement has begun at opened wateryards, they would only be able to
accommodate 250 families each; no incremental production has been assumed for
the already settled families as their attitude toward the Project is as yet
untested.

19.       The cropping period will be four years.  Each year 1.5 ha of new
land would be opened for cropping and after year 4 , 1.5 ha would be allowed
to revert to bush fallow dominated by gum arabic. No equipment would be
needed for land clearing. In certain instances a small amount of hand labor
would be needed which the settler and his family would be able to provide. No
cost has been calculated for this work.
20.       Gum arabic will be intersown between millet and groundnuts on third
year land to give 400 trees per ha. A total of 1,200 seedlings will occupy
20% of the area of crops in third and fourth year land. A family will be able
to maintain two livestock units on their fallow land and have the right to
maintain a further livestock unit on communal grazing land within the settle-
ment. It is assumed that families will continue to maintain the number
of livestock they held before settlement and be permitted to run livestock, in
excess of those permitted in the settlement area, with the nomadic herds. No
incremental income from livestock has been assumed at this stage.
Settlement Crop Production
21.       In calculating settlement crop production the following assump-
tions have been made:
Assumption 1: Using traditional farm methods a family will adopt

per ha. Tapping of mature gum arabic trees will commence five
years after sowing. The mean gum yield of a tree is estimated
to be 0.22 kg. per season and a family will be able to tap 2,000
trees (5 ha) per season yielding a total of 440 kg gum.
Assumption II: Improved varieties and information on better
farming methods will become available from the Crop Experimental
Unit at Ghazala Gawazat and from year 9 will result in the
following annual increases in crop yield:

Calculation of Benefits
26.       In calculating benefits two production assumptions were made:
(a) that without the Program crop yields will decline by 2.5%
per annum after year 10; and
(b) that with the Program improved varieties and farming methods
would result in improvement of crop yields from year 9.
These are modest estimates of the benefits resulting from improved land
utilization and a strong adaptive research input and of the likely deteriora-
tion in production without the program. An estimate was made of the possible
effect of adoption of ox-drawn implements on crop production but is too
hypothetical to be used in the calculation of program benefits.

28.       It was assumed that no assistance would be needed for settlers to
build new houses on the settlements. In the program area, farmers are
building simple houses from local material and no special skill and/or
material which could not be found on the spot would be needed. Household
belongings of settlers would be transported free of charge to settlers under
the program from the old to new areas, and during the start-up period a small
amount of thatching straw would be provided if necessary.
29.       Long-term land use arrangements under the program would provide
adequate security for the settlers and a desirable element of flexibility in
land management. Such arrangements would build upon and fit into traditional
and local tenure systems and contribute to the achievement of development
objectives. This would also make it possible to leave open for a later stage
obligation for the settlers for cost recovery.
III. Livestock Parameters
30.       Based on the survey carried out by Huntings Technical Services
in Southern District (1974) and in the Eastern District Project Area (1975)
the basic assumptions regarding livestock parameters utilized in program
calculations are the following: Assumptions have only been made for
cattle for two reasons: (i) The program area population of sheep and goats,
in terms of livestock units, represents only 5% of the total livestock popula-
tion; and (ii) In a "Without Program" projection the numbers of sheep and
goats would increase and these would gradually replace the cattle population;
benefits would, however, be very nearly the same.



ANNEX 5
Page 20
A. With Program Assumptions
31.       The total number of cattle at the start of the Project is estimated
at 1.3 million. This figure was arrived at from the result of the 1975 survey
estimating the total cattle population at 1.9 million less 0.6 million cattle
found in but originating outside the program area (Annex 7, para 2). Proj-
ections assume that the age of first calving is four years, calving percentage
50% calf mortality 30%; adult mortality 10% and marketed offtake 5%.
32.       From Year 3 improvement of veterinary services would result in an
increase in the calving rate to 55%, a decrease in calf mortality to 25%,
and a decrease in adult mortality to 8%. Producers sales habits are expected
to remain unchanged.
33.       From Year 5  herd parameters would remain unchanged, but stockman
training, non-formal education and demand combined would bring about a 10%
points increase in the numbers of sales of 4-5 year old steers to 20% and a
12% points increase in the number of sales of 1-2 year old steers to 15%, and
a 2% point increase to 5% in the number of sales of other males.
34.       From Year 10 age at first calving would drop to 3 1/2 years,
the calving rate would increase by 5% points to 60%, calf mortality would drop
by 5% points to 20%, sales of 4-5 year old steers would increase further by
10% points to 30% and of 1-2 year old steer by 15% points and to 30% and by 5%
points for other age groups of males, and culling of cows would increase by 1%
point from 2% to 3%. These assumptions reflect a continuous improvement of
services, husbandry practices and nutrition originating from introduction of
range management and fodder crop cultivation practices as well as a steadily
increasing response of the livestock producers to education.
35.       From Year 19 culling of mature female stock would increase by a
further 3% points to 6% and herd growth at that time is expected to stabilize.
Stabilization of the herd model in year twenty has resulted from increasing
the sale of breeding age females (which is expected if grazing/ forage re-
sources are the limiting factor).
36.       The above assumptions are conservative since it is possible that
by year 20 forage production under flood cultivation or mechanization and the
availability of by-products, combined with selling of male animals (surplus to
breeding requirements) as yearlings would allow for a considerable increase in
total numbers and a higher proportion of female breeding stock.
B. Without Program Assumptions
37.       The initial situation would be identical to that outlined for the
"With Program" situation. From Year 5 due to increasing encroachment



ANNEX 5
Page 21
of cultivation and gradual deterioration of grazing, calving rates would drop
by 5% points to 45%, calf mortality would increase by 5% points to 35%.
Expected Trends Regarding the Pastoralist Populations in the Area.
38.       With the program the human population in the program Area engaged
in transhumant livestock production (150,000) is assumed to remain stable.
The natural increase in population is assumed to be absorbed by out migration
of young pastoralists to other sector of the economy; and an increase in the
sedentary population engaged in crop production due to more attractive
prospects.
39.       Without the program it is assumed that about 40 head of cattle
represents the lower limit of what is necessary to maintain an acceptable
pastoralist family way of life. Consequently, 15,000 more families would have
to out migrate or take up crop cultivation by project year 10, a further
18,000 by year 20, and a further 12,000 by year 25 in addition to the natural
population increase.

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Crop Production, Settlement, Extension Services,
Crop Research
I. Crop Production
Environment
1.        Climate.  The climate of Eastern District is typical of the low
rainfall woodland savannas of Africa. Crops are rainfed and the amount and
distribution of rainfall are major factors affecting yield. The rainy season
begins in early June and extends 120-140 days with a slight peak in late
July/early August. The mean annual rainfall in the crop producing area for
1941-1970 was 550 mm, but with 20% probability of a rainfall less than 450
mm. Because of variation in the date of onset of the rains, the optimum crop
sowing period varies from year to year and place to place. In response,
farmers stagger their sowing dates.
2.        During the last ten years the mean rainfall of Eastern District has
been 20% below the average for 1941-1964, and has resulted both in reduced
crop yields and increased migration into the District from drier areas in the
North and Northwest.
3.        During the crop season the mean daily temperature is about 26 C,
with mean maxima of 30-35 C and mean minima of 19-22 C.  Evaporation rates
are high, 6-7 mm per day, and crop water stress occurs after quite short
periods of drought, due to high evaporation and low soil moisture holding
capacity.
4.        Soils.  There are four main soil types:  qoz, atmur, naga'a and
buta. 1/ In the Eastern District Project area cultivation is confined
to the aeolian qoz and atmur sands of the Qoz, Baggara and Basement Land
1/   Qoz    - Extensive and uniform sandy soils laid down during the late
Pleistocene.
Atmur - Sandy soils of ridges within Baggara Land System. They are
younger than the Qoz soils.
Naga'a - Non-cracking clays over which sheet wash occurs.
Buta   - Dark grey cracking clays found in depressions.



ANNEX 6
Page 2
Systems (Map, IBRD 12474). These soils have low fertility and poor moisture
retention, but are favored by farmers because they are easily cultivated with
hand tools and are easily penetrated by crop roots in search of moisture in
deeper horizons. They cake badly and require repeated hoeing to prevent
run-off. When this is done water penetration is good.
5.        An important feature of the Baggara Land System is the Naga'a
alluvial non-cracking clays, which occur on gentle slopes, over which sheet
wash takes place, in association with stabilized sand soils (atmur) and with
cracking clays (buta). Naga'a soils are not cropped because of the difficulty
in cultivating them with hand implements. Severe sheet erosion and compaction
is caused by dry season grazing pressure, and the end result of overstocking
of naga'a soils is an almost treeless and grassless plain with a hard, smooth
surface.
6.        Buta cracking clays occur in small depressions within all the land
systems.  The depressions are flooded during the rains and, although they are
sown to crops in Southern District (ODM/Abu Dhabi Project Area) after the
receding flood, buta areas are not utilized in Eastern District.
Cropping Patterns
7.        About 300,000 ha of crops, all rainfed, are sown annually in the
Project area. About 135,000 ha are cultivated in the Baggara Land System
and a similar area in the Rizeiqat and Ma'aliya Qoz (Map 12474). The main
crops are bulrush millet and groundnuts. Minor crops include sesame, sor-
ghum, lubia (Lublab niger), roselle (Hibiscus sabderiffa), melon and various
vegetables.
8.        Millet.  About 70% of the cropped area is occupied by millet.
Millet is not exported but, as an insurance, farmers sow slightly larger areas
than are needed to provide their subsistence needs; millet grain is therefore
always available in the local markets. The straw is used for thatching and
fencing. Millet yields average only 0.4 ton grain per ha. Current varieties
are inefficient grain producers with a poor grain straw ratio, and yield 10-15
tons straw per hectare. Varieties take 140 days from sowing to maturity,
which is long for an average 120-140 days rainy season, and yields are seriously
reduced when the rainy season is shorter than usual. Other major factors
limiting millet yields are the prevalence of buda (Striga hermonthica), loss
of crop to birds, and damage by stem borer (Chilo zonellus) and American
bollworm (Heliothis armigera).
9.        Groundnuts.  The development of commercial groundnut production in
the area is associated with the construction of the railway to Nyala in 1960,
and groundnuts now occupy about one-third of the cropped area. Groundnuts
are well suited to the qoz sands. Germination and crop growth are usually
good and, apart from a little fungal leaf spot at the end of the season, the
crop is exceptionally pest and disease free. The current commercial variety,



ANNEX 6
Page 3
Barbeton, has a 90-100 days growing season. Yields average about 0.9 ton
unshelled nuts per hectare. The haulm is usually left in the field after
harvest and provides valuable animal feed.
10.       Sesame is grown as a minor cash crop, but local varieties are slow
maturing and large amounts of seed are lost by dehiscence of capsules during
harvesting and drying so that the average yield for the Project area is only
0.2 ton per hectare. Small plots around the homestead are sown with sorghum
and lubia. Local lubia is a vigorous grower and preliminary trials show that
it has potential as a fodder crop. These crops occupy about 3% of the total
crop area.
11.       Gum Arabic.  Gum arabic, derived from hashab (Acacia Senegal), is
the second most important cash crop in the development area. When prices are
high, production is supplemented by the inferior quality gum from talh (Acacia
Seyal). Rights to tap trees are allocated to individuals, and areas support-
ing hashab gardens cannot be cleared for cultivation by others. Plant com-
munities rich in hashab are found throughout the area and hashab occurs widely
in the Baggara and Basement Land Systems. Some cultivators sow hashab after
abandoning cultivated land but, more commonly, hashab communities result from
selective clearing of land (Annex 4).
12.       There are two tapping seasons.  Early tapping begins with the onset
of tree dormancy, usually after the end of the rains in October. Gum is
collected 3-6 weeks after tapping and then at 10-15 day intervals. Usually
four pickings are made. If late rains or a heavy crop of millet or groundnuts
does not leave time for early tapping of hashab before the beginning of cold
weather in December, it is postponed until the end of the cold spell in March.
The average yield of gum per tree is about 0.22 kilograms. A survey by
Hunting Technical Services in 1976 found the average amount of gum collected
by a family in the Baggara Land System to be 130 kg. The same survey found
that in the Baggara Land System a family taps 600 hashab trees per annum and
on the Qoz Land System 300 trees.
Farm Operations
13.       There are small areas on which land clearing by cutting and
burning is carried out one or two years before the first crops are sown.
No equipment is needed for this purpose. During the burning of brushwood,_
nitrogen and sulphur are lost to the crop-fallow system.
14.       Seedbeds are prepared by hand hoeing and crops sown on the flat.
The first sowing of millet usually takes place into dry soil; the remainder
of the crop is sown after the first reliable rains. Millet and sorghum are
usually sown by the end of June, groundnuts and sesame in July and August.
Poor early rains can lead to poor crop stands, or even crop failure, and
necessitate substantial resowing. Early sown millet is most vulnerable, and
the poor stands of this widely spaced crop (1 meter x 1.5 meters between
hills) often contrast with the excellent stand of the later and more closely
placed (40 centimeters x 40 centimeters) groundnuts crop.



ANNEX 6
Page 4
15.       Subsequent weeding and harvesting is carried out with hand labor.
Millet and sorghum heads are cut and thre hed with a flail and groundnuts
lifted, stacked to dry and the pods removed by hand. Cash crops are usually
bagged and sold within a few weeks of harvest. Millet and sorghum may be
stored in sacks in clay grain stores above ground or clay-lined pits.
Labor Organization
16.       Family labor provides the bulk, often the whole, of farm labor
needs and only limited use is made of hired labor. There is a heavy demand
for labor during sowing and weeding in the second half of June and the
second half of July; the periods vary from year to year and depend on rain-
fall. Similar labor peaks occur during harvesting in November. Farmers
traditionally hire migrant,Dinka laborers, but these are now in short supply
(following disputes between the Dinka and the Baggara) and their hire is
costly (Annex 4). Traditional work parties, providing their members with
work in exchange for food and drink may be organized, but the standard of
work performed by these groups is usually poor and work parties are rarely
available when most needed (Annex 4). As a result of shortage of labor early
in the crop season, and despite the long hours worked at this time of year,
farm operations cannot be completed at the best time or satisfactorily.
Priority given to preparing land for groundnuts and sowing the crop usually
results in a delay in weeding millet.
The Natural Fallow System
17.       When adequate land is available, it is cropped for 4-5 years and
then allowed to revert to natural fallow for as long as is possible (periods
vary from 4-15 years). Increasing incidence of weeds, particularly buda, is
a major factor deciding the length of cropping. On land where millet and
groundnuts are grown in alternate years the build-up of buda is slower and
the cropping period can be extended to about 8 years. Natural fallow provides
excellent control of weeds and buda and some recuperation of soil fertility.
However, the recuperative value of a long fallow on the sandy qoz soils,
which have low capacity to hold nutrients and are subject to heavy leaching,
is easily exaggerated. Increasing land pressure in the Baggara Land System
and northern qoz areas is leading to a situation where less than half the
area can be maintained in fallow; yields have already begun to decline in
many areas.
18.       Farmers are aware of the value of animal manure and often keep
cattle on their millet fields for a few days at the beginning of the season.
However, no fertilizer is used in the Project area, nor is there any con-
clusive experimental evidence on which to base fertilizer recommendations to
farmers. Experience elsewhere in the savanna zones of Africa suggests that
crop yield responses will be will be found to phosphate and sulphur, but
unless fertilizers are heavily subsidized their use is unlikely to appeal to
farmers at present crop prices.



ANNEX 6
Page 5
II. Settlement Program
19.       A settlement program would be initiated under the Project in order
to help relieve population pressure in already settled areas and to safeguard
the north-south migratory stock routes against encroaching cultivation. The
settlements would be organized around existing wateryards. 1/ Some of these
wateryards have been opened, and consequently people are already moving into
the surrounding area; the emphasis in these settlements would be on the
rationalization of land use, the restriction of unauthorized migration into
the area, and the introduction of improved farming methods. Other wateryards
would not be opened until the Land Management Division had prepared a plan
for the settlement process; the initial emphasis in these cases wouold be on
settler selection, design of the settlement area, etc.
20.       For historical reasons, the Rizeiqat and Ma'aliya peoples would be
most reluctant to settle alongside one another. Consequently, during the
Project period, 9 already opened wateryards in the Ma'aliya area would be
brought into the settlement program, as would 3 as yet unopened wateryards in
Rizeiqat territory. Tentative projections have also been made for the exten-
sion of the settlement program over a possible Phase II Project. Phasing of
the overall settlement program is given below. The timing of the program was
worked out in accordance with the need to establish an effective land manage-
ment unit in the district before settlement can proceed, and in light of the
fact that it was thought advisable to allow for a modest and well monitored
pace of development in the early years, so as to ensure the smooth implemen-
tation of the overall effect (Map IBRD 12473).
1/   Wateryards consist of a borehole, a diesel pumping unit and a 50 m
elevated water storage tank, fountain! and watering troughs in a
fenced area of about 3,000 to 4,000 m.
