21.       In the implementation of the program, emphasis would be placed on
flexibility. The phasing described above is therefore intended as a guide to
development and a projection of its likely course on the basis of the best
estimate possible at the present time. It would be modified in the light
of experience and information supplied by the monitoring unit. The Project
would be reviewed at the end of Year 4; at this time decisions would be made
on whether to implement Phase II of the development program, on the rate of
implementation, and on what changes need to be made.
Land Allocation
22.       Demarcation.  Land settlement and development in Eastern District
would be the responsibility of the Land Management Unit stationed at Ghazala
Gawazat. An aerial survey of the development area would be made during the
first year of implementation and settlement locations marked on the photographs.
Field teams carrying out ground survey and allocation work would consist of
land allocation officers, survey assistants and laborers who would be needed
to cut traces. Their equipment would include survey instruments, tents,
tools, chainsaws and tractor trailers for moving materials. Field superivision
would be provided by Senior Land Allocation Officers who would also be respon-
sible for arranging logistic support. Trucks would be provided under the
Project for moving supplies and materials. The team would designate village
sites, cropping and grazing areas. A model tentative settlement plan is given



ANNEX 6
Page 7
in the attached Map (IBRD 12473). However, it should be emphasized that each
settlement would be specifically designed so as to accommodate the specific
requirements of the individual community concerned.
23.       Selection of Settlers.  As far as possible, settlements would be
linked with specific communities in the homeland areas; that is, a settlement
unit would be occupied by settlers from a single community. This would not
pose a problem for those already opened wateryards where in-migration is
already taking place, as the natural inclination of the new settlers has been
to settle alongside their former neighbors. The selection of settlers for
the as yet unopened wateryards would be the responsibility of the traditional
tribal authorities - in this case the Land/Village Sheikhs - and the local
councils. This selection process would encourage a genuine community spirit
and would minimize the possibility of disputes. Further uncontrolled (i.e.,
unsupervised) settlement in these areas would be strictly prohibited, and this
regulation would be enforced by the local Government authorities (Annex 4).
24.       The Settlement Unit.  Settlements in the Qoz land system would each
center around wateryards spaced at 15 km intervals; each would serve an area
within a radius of 7.5 km or a total area of 17,000 to 18,000 hectares. This
radius would provide a reasonable maximum limit for regular access to the
wateryards. Farmers would be grouped in five villages close to the wateryard.
Where farms are close to the villages, families would probably continue to
live in the villages during the crop season. Where farms are more than 3
kilometers from the villages it is likely that families would move to tem-
porary accommodations on their farms during the rains when surface water would
be available. In addition, to the cropping areas, each settlement unit would
contain 2,500 hectares of communal grazing.
25.       The hinterlands around the wateryards would be surveyed and divided
into farms averaging 25 ha. Provision would be made for households of
different sizes, and actual farm sizes would range between 20 and 30 ha. It
is assumed that an average family of 6 persons would cultivate 6 hectares,
although ox-drawn implements may be introduced in Phase II. A farmer who
has ox-drawn implements would be able to expand his cultivation area to
7.5 hectares.
26.       Farms would be too small for tractor equipment to be used, and it
is unlikely that an average family of 2.6 labor equivalents, with limited
opportunities for hiring labor, would be able to cultivate more than 7.5
hectares using ox-drawn equipment. However, a technical breakthrough could
result in a reduced fallow period and reduce the total land required by a
family by half. Therefore, a settler would initially be given full rights to
the use of only 12.5 hectares and the total land allotment per family reviewed
at the end of the Project.
27.       Assuming that 15% of land would be lost to access roads, dwellings
and uncultivable soils, each settlement would accommodate about 500 farm
families. It is assumed that it would be possible to accommodate only 250



ANNEX 6
Page 8
new settlers in settlements around the wateryards which have already been
opened. Under these assumptions, it is estimated that at the end of the
Project (year 5 to 6) 2,500 farmers would be settled, a total of 50,000 to
75,000 ha of land allocated, 1/ and 3,200 cropped. If a second phase should
follow, by year 11, about 10,000 farmers would be settled, a total of 250,000
ha of land allocated, and 50,000 ha cropped. At full development (year 15),
10,250 farmers would be settled, a total of about 250,000 - 300,000 ha, alloca-
ted and 62,000 ha cropped.
28.       Land Tenure.  The Land Management Unit located at Ghazala Gawazat
would have overall responsibility for the settlement program. The settlers
would receive long term land use rights containing covenants for a suitable
cropping system. Under the Unregistered Land Act of 1970, all land not
previously registered was designated Government property. With insignificant
exceptions, all land within the Project area falls into this category, and
legal facility therefore exists for providing land leaseholds backed by civil
law. Terms, conditions and duration of land use rights would require specifi-
cation during the early stages of the Project. 2/
29.       Land boundaries would need to be beaconed on the ground and re-
corded on maps and plans. A land registry would be established within the
provincial government at Nyala for this purpose. Boundaries of large units
(grazing areas and settlements) would be marked and filed on air photographs.
Boundaries of smaller areas would require detailed survey.
30.       Land vacated in the homeland area by departing settlers would also
be surveyed and, in association with local village committees, decisions
reached on its future. When necessary it would be incorporated into stock
routes in order to provide easier passage for nomadic herds, otherwise it
would be available to create larger and viable units for remaining settlers.
31.       The rate at which the above measures could be introduced would
depend on the extent to which local and traditional institutions could be
mobilized. Within the settlements, local councils and Land Sheikhs would be
expected to undertake allocation with guidance from Project staff. A Sudanese
sociologist, who is well informed about the local conditions and habits of
social groups to be involved in the settlement program would be employed
in the Land Management Unit. His task would be to discuss with prospective
settlers alternatives for settlement patterns and maintain permanent dialogue
between elders, the local authority, the Western Savannah Development Corporation
and the professional field teams implementing the settlement program. His
findings and recommendations would be of particular importance for the program's
1/   Settlers would initially have full rights to only half of the farming
area (i.e. around 30,000 ha).
2/   Payment for land use on a leasehold basis was considered to be premature
at this stage. This would, however, be explored in a possible Phase II
project (Annex 14).



ANNEX 6
Page 9
success and would be closely monitored and evaluated by the Land Management
Unit. Initial arbitration in the case of conflicting claims would be handled
by village councils but right of appeal to a higher authority would be
reserved. It is expected that the response of the prospective settlers to move
into the proposed areas will be favorable. However, Government realize that
the settlement process would have to be implemented voluntarily and that the
participants should have full power of free choice to participate or not.
Food for New Settlers
32.       Most families would not be able to grow crops during the season
they are moved, and crop production in the second year of settlement may be
insufficient to meet the family needs. Supplementary food rations would
therefore need to be provided for at least two years. The standard daily
requirement per person would consist of 500 grams of grain, 30 grams of
vegetable oil, 30 grams of dried skim milk and 20-30 grams of pulses. The
World Food Program (WFP) has indicated that it would look favorably upon a
request for assistance, and under a standard WFP agreement would supply food
free to the port of entry. The Government would pay 50% of the market cost of
transport and storage. It is likely that wheat would be imported, sold at
Port Sudan, and used to finance local purchase of millet. Government would
also be expected to provide a WFP Project Manager, Accountant,and Storekeeper.
Government would contact the WFP in order to finalize arrangements on food
assistance. Action should be coordinated with the ODM/Abu Dhabi project staff.
Crop Production
33.       Because of the lack of adequate data and the absence of research
findings, no package of new techniques would initially be introduced in the
settlement areas. Farmers would follow their traditional cropping pattern
of about two-third millet and one-third groundnuts. However, once the
results of the adaptive research program (paras 36-47) are available, and
the extension service is fully mobilized, it is expected that substantial
variations on these practices would be introduced.  One easily foreseeable
change would be the encouragement of farmers to plant hashab during the third
year of cultivation. These trees would require very little care and would
yield a substantial cash return to their owners. However, in the early years
the main emphasis of the program would be to arrest the decline in the environ-
ment in the homeland areas where population pressure is leading to rapid
depletion of the soils, and to stabilize crop production in the new settle-
ments under carefully supervised conditions.
34.       Projected crop production in the program area under both a "With
Program" and a "Without Program" situation has been estimated. In the "Without
Program" case, in estimating the production of the new settlers, it was
calculated that at the present population growth of 6.25% per annum (see Basic
Assumptions), reserves of virgin land in the Baggara Land System would be
exhausted by 1985. Therefore, farmers would have no alternative but to
progressively reduce the area of fallow. Pressure on land in the Northern Qoz
areas has already resulted in movement out of the area into open wateryards to
the South.



ANNEX 6
Page 10
35.       Ownership of Animals.  Farmers would be able to keep 3 animal units
(milk cows plus calves or their equivalent). 1/ It is envisaged that each
farm would support two animal units (two milk cows or oxen) on natural graz-
ing and crop residues and that a flock of 5 to 10 sheep and goats would be
kept on communal grazing within the settlement. The location of communal
grazing areas would be decided at the time units are demarcated and in
consultation with representatives of the incoming settlers. They may consist
of 5 areas, each of 500 hectares, or of smaller units, not less than 100
hectares each and shared by 20 or more families. Provision of small grazing
units would make it possible to differentiate between land more suited for
cultivation and land more suited for grazing and would make it easier for the
extension staff to supervise livestock. Furthermore, it is assumed that
families would continue to maintain the number of livestock they held before
settlement and be permitted to run livestock in excess of those permitted in
the settlement area, with the nomadic herds. No incremental income from
livestock has been assumed at this stage.
III. Adaptive Crop Research
36.       Very little crop research has been carried out in the Western
Savannas. The limited resources available for agricultural research have
been largely directed to areas and crops most likely to make a large and
rapid contribution to the country's economy. As a result, research efforts
have concentrated on the problems of irrigated production (mostly of cotton)
and of large-scale mechanized farming. Proposed development in South Darfur
gives a new urgency to the need for a supporting research program in these
dry savanna areas.
37.       A sound program of applied research is essential for the ultimate
success of the program. Perennial water supplies, virgin land, security of
land tenure and fair market prices should be adequate incentives to attract
settlers from the areas with depleted soils due to land pressure to the new
settlements and to secure the settlers' adherence to the conditions of their
rights of usufruct. Until such time as there are proven technical innovations
for the extension services to offer farmers, they will continue to grow their
own crop varieties and use customary cultivation practices.
38.       By itself, settlement will provide only a temporary solution to
the problems of South Darfur. It will reduce the pressure on land in the
Rizeiqat and Ma'aliya homelands and guarantee stable crop production in the
1/   No real problems are envisaged in obliging the settlers to limit the
number of their livestock; informal restrictions are already being
enforced in existing settlements without outside guidance or request.



ANNEX 6
Page 11
newly opened Qoz areas, but a long term solution will depend on the estab-
lishment of more productive farming systems of the qoz soils and extension of
cultivation into areas than cannot now be cultivated with hand implements.
Farming Problems
39.       The main agricultural problems are:
(1) Poor Millet Varieties. Local varieties are inefficient
grain producers and a high proportion of the products of
photosynthesis is used in the production of leaves and
stems. Varieties have too long a growing season which
make them very susceptible to reduction in the length of
the rainy season.
(2) Buda. The high proportion of land under millet leads
to a rapid build-up of buda in cultivated land and de-
creasing millet yields.
(3) Weeds. Effective weed control is rarely achieved be-
cause high seasonal crop labor requirements cannot be
met. Labor is required for weeding millet at the same
time it is required for establishing groundnuts, the
latter is given priority.
(4) Cultural Practices. All cultivations are carried out
with hand implements. This restricts cropping to the
sandy qoz soils and makes it difficult to achieve timely
weeding.
(5) The Natural Fallow System. The traditional cropping sys-
tem in which a few years under cultivation is followed by
a longer period of natural fallow is an inefficient use of
land and creates a heavy demand on labor for land clearing.
(6) Pests. Millet suffers considerable pest damage. Seed
is untreated and, because of loss of seedlings to insect
pests, a very high seed rate has to be used. Subsequent
loss of crop occurs to stem borers and birds.
The Research Program
40.       An adaptive research program would be initiated under the Project.
This program would concentrate on the solution of the immediate problems of
3mproving production and evolving more efficient farming systems. The main
lines of research would be:



ANNEX 6
Page 12
(1) The introduction, testing and screening of millet varieties.
Work has already begun on screening the major collection
of millet material held at Wad Medani.  For South Darfur,
emphasis should be put on identifying varieties with high
yield potential and a higher proportion of dry matter as
grain. Suitable varieties should have a shorter growing
period than those now grown and be insensitive to day
length. Selected varieties would be tested for local
acceptability before being bulked for distribution to
farmers. The present low yields make it highly probable
that substantially better varieties can be found among
introductions from other African countries and India where
millet breeding has been underway for a considerable time.
A breeding program would not be necessary in the early
stages of millet improvement, but improved material from
the screening program might later be used in breeding
work. Assistance in the millet improvement program would
be sought from the International Crop Research Institute
for the semi-Arid Tropics (ICRISAT) on a consultancy basis.
(2) The evaluation and screening of other crop varieties,
including groundnuts, sesame, sorghum, pulses and forage
legume. Special emphasis would be put on forage legume
work and include testing of Dalichos, Lablab, Clitoria,
Stylosanthes, Phillipesara and Medicago spp.
(3) Work on control of buda and weeds with trials of low cost
pre and post emergence herbicides using simple applicators.
Chemical weed control in millet would relieve labor for
better hand weed control in groundnuts. During screening
of millet varieties, evidence of resistance to buda would
be sought.
(4) The introduction and testing of a range of ox-drawn imple-
ments from other parts of Africa. The use of ox-drawn
implements would reduce farm labor requirements and permit
larger areas of crops to be cultivated. Suitable imple-
ments have been developed elsewhere and the object of the
screening program would be to identify suitable equipment
that could be manufactured locally.
(5) The development and testing of improved farming systems
incorporating new crops and techniques. The objective
would be to reduce and possibly eliminate the present long
fallow period. The introduction of higher yielding crop
varieties would result in a smaller proportion of the
cropped area being sown annually to millet, with a result-
ing reduction in the buildup of buda, and permit an
extension of the cropping period. Research could lead to



ANNEX 6
Page 13
a short fallow, possibly replaced by forage legumes, and
with gum arabic trees restricted to farm boundaries and to
plantations on what initially would be communal grazing.
Because of the possibilities for intensifying production,
the final decision of total farm size for successive
groups of settlers should be deferred until the end of
the Project to give research the opportunity to provide
the answers (para 26).
(6) Work on the management of Naga'a soils, including methods
of mechanical land preparation (e.g., chisel and disc
plowing) needed to bring them into cultivation, suitable
crops and production practices and their potential for
development as additional grazing areas. The development
of suitable techniques would enable substantial areas of
these soils to be brought into cultivation to relieve land
pressure on the Baggara Land System in a possible Phase II
of the development program.
41.       Advice on pest and disease control would be provided by short-term
consultancies. Measures already practiced elsewhere would be suitable for
the control of the major pests, such as stem borer and American bollworm.
Staff                                                             1
42.       In order to carry out this research program, an Expermimental Sec-
tion would be established in the Crop Division at Ghazala Gawazat. Senior
research staff financed by the Project would consist of a Senior Agronomist,
in charge of the Experimental Section; a Senior Weed Specialist; and two
Agronomists responsible for crop introduction and Naga'a soils work. Their
duties and responsibilities would be as follows, and terms of reference
for the Senior Agronomist and the Senior Weed Control Specialist are found in
Annex 15, Appendix 2.
(a) The Senior Agronomist would be responsible for administra-
tion of the Crop Experimental Section at Ghazala Gawazat.
He would be in charge of all crop experimental work in
Eastern and Southern Districts and would agree to all
research programs before they are submitted to the Deputy
Director General of WSDC posted at Ghazala Gawazat for
approval. He would be personally responsible for the
development of more productive and viable farming systems.
(b) The Agronomist (Crop Introduction) would be responsible
for all screening of new varieties, comparison of promis-
ing new varieties with those presently grown and bulking
of seed of those chosen for introduction. Emphasis would
be placed on the identification of improved millet and
forage legume species.



ANNEX 6
Page 14
(c) The Senior Weed Control Specialist would be responsible
for developing improved and low cost methods of control-
ling crop weeds and buda (Striga hermonthica) suitable
for small scale farmers. He would examine the feasibility
of using pre and post emergent herbicides, applied with
simple applicators.
(d) The Agronomist (Naga'a soils) would be responsible for
developing methods for bringing the heavy non-cracking
Naga'a soils into productive use. He would study alter-
native cultivation methods, crops and cropping patterns
and the possibility for utilizing these soils for animal
production.
43.       An Agricultural Engineer financed by the ODM/Abu Dhabi project
would be attached to the Crop Experimental Unit and would be responsible for
the introduction and testing of ox-drawn implements and the choice of suit-
able equipment for local manufacture. He would cooperate with the Agronomist
(Naga'a soils) in trials on cultivation methods suitable for Naga'a soils.
Facilities
44.       Land would be made available for program research at Ghazala
Gawazat, which is sited on a cross section of the main soil types--qoz,
naga'a and buta. Equipment would be provided and funds would be made avail-
able under the Project for consultancy fees for visiting specialists, who may
be required to participate in specific projects such as millet improvement and
the development of improved farming systems. Provision would also be included
for the importation of seed and of ox-drawn equipment for testing. Provision
would be made for a laboratory unit with basic entomological, phytopathological
and soils equipment for the identification of pests and diseases and soil
moisture testing. The unit would have its own tractor and cultivation equip-
ment.
45.       Facilities would be provided for trials at the Nucleus Development
Centers, which are located at sites representative of the main environmental
conditions (para 48). A Technical Assistant, aided by a Research Assistant
would supervise field trials at each NDC in Eastern District. Demonstrations
for farmers and Unit Farms at the NDCs would be supervised by each Center's
Farm Manager. The NDC at Abu Matariq would be supplied with tractor equipment
for trials on Naga'a soils.
46.       Provision would be made for transport to research staff and for a
truck for moving materials and equipment. The ODM/Abu Dhabi estimates include
provision for housing, transport and a driver for the Agricultural Engineer.



ANNEX 6
Page 15
IV. Extension Services
47.       Extension advice would be disseminated to farmers through 3 Nucleus
Development Centers (NDC) to be established in the Project area. These
centers would be located at El Heid (to serve the Rizeiquat Qoz), Mazroub (to
serve the Ma'aliya Qoz), and Abu Matariq (to serve the Baggara Land System. 1/
Each NDC would cover some 60 ha and would provide extension services to
farmers within a radius of 40-50 km. They would also provide field trial
facilities for Ghazala Gawazat; this function would be particularly important
as the NDC's are expected to facilitate a two-way flow of information between
farmers and research workers.
48.       The NDC's would be under the direct control and supervision of
a Senior Agronomist (Extension) stationed at Ghazala Gawazat; the Senior
Agronomist would be assisted in these duties by an Agronomist (Extension).
(Both of these staff would be provided for under the ODM/Abu Dhabi Project).
Each NDC would be supervised by a Senior Extension Officer. As each NDC
would service 10 settlements, provision would be made for an Extension
Officer for every three new or rationalized settlements and for one Extension
Assistant per settlement. An additional 10 Extension Assistants would be
responsible for areas vacated by the new settlers.  By the end of the Project
period, the extension staff would therefore include 3 Senior Extension
Officers, 10 Extension Officers and 40 Extension Assistants; provision would
also be made for itinerant ox trainers from year 5. These extension staff
would be located in the Extension Section of the Crop Division at Ghazala
Gawazat.
49.       Before undertaking their respective assignments, the extension
staff would receive initial training at Ghazala Gawazat. On-the-job train-
ing would also be given at regular intervals, and at two week intervals
during the crop season.
50.       Work programs would be drawn up at the beginning of the season by
the Senior Agronomist (Extension) in consultation with the Senior Extension
Officers; the latter would in turn prepare detailed programs for their staff.
The work required of each extension worker would be clearly defined. Exten-
sion advice would be freely available to all, but emphasis would be put
on work with selected farmers who agree to try out recommendations and so
demonstrate their practicability and effectiveness. Only proven technology
would be disseminated to farmers.
1/   Three similar NDC's would be established in the Southern District to
serve the ODM/Abu Dhabi Project; these would be located at Dimsu (Qoz
soils), Jimerza (Alluvial soils), and Serragh (Basement soils).



ANNEX 6
Page 16
51.       A small training school for farmers with facilities for overnight
accommodation would be provided at each NDC. Training would be undertaken by
extension staff, who would also provide follow-up assistance and supervision
to farmers in the field.
52.       As far as possible, extension staff would be housed at the NDC's
but, in deciding the sites of houses, job needs would be taken into account.
Extension Assistants would live in ancillary housing in the settlements they
service. Provision has been made in the ODM/Abu Dhabi project proposed for
houses, transport, drivers and a typist for the Senior Agronomist (Extension)
and Agronomist (Extension). Vehicles would be provided to ensure adequate
mobility for extension staff and each NDC would be equipped with a lorry for
securing supplies and transporting farmers for training.

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Ghazala Gawazat Center for
Investigation, Research and Extension
A. General Description
1.        Ghazala Gawazat research station, established in 1957, is situated
about 21 kilometers northwest of Ed Da'ein on the main road to Nyala and 20 km
from the Ed Da'einrailroad station. Average rainfall enjoyed by the station
in the 14 year period prior to 1970 was 474 millimeters. The altitude of the
station is about 485 meters above sea level. The station extends either side
of the Khartoum-Nyala railways, in the dar of the Rizeiqat, and covers an area
of 5,310 hectares. The area is divided into 20 paddocks, nominally square, of
256 hectares each and one triangular paddock of 190 hectares. Due to original
mapping errors few of the paddocks are square and they vary in size from 378
hectares (Paddock 1) to 182 hectares (Paddock 7) (Figure 1).
Center Facilities (Figure 2)
2.        The station now has 3 stores (buildings A, B and C) and 6 adminis-
trative office buildings (buildings D, E, F, G, H and I). Building A houses
three separate stores: 1 for petrol, 1 for diesel oil and lubricants; and a
third for spare parts. Building B is used for storing drugs and some veter-
inary and miscellaneous animal production laboratory equipment. Building C
is used for storing feeding stuffs and miscellaneous materials. In addition,
diesel oil is stored in a tank in the middle of the compound in a large
number of 40-gallon drums, and there is also a large stock of barbed wire.
Building D includes an Office for Range Management Technicians, together with
a large almost empty room and a small seed store; there are a number of steel
cabinets in the large room, in one of which the entire station library is
housed. Building E is the Office of the Range Management Officer. Building
F houses the Meterological Office; there is some equipment, including a radio
telephone that is not yet operational and office space for the technical
assistants. Building G is used by the clerical staff. Building H contains
the Office of the Officer in Charge. There is office space in this block for
three other officers. All administrative buildings are poorly maintained and
require urgent repairs, repainting and new fittings.
3.        There are 13 staff houses; one senior staff house, two of medium
size and 10 one-bedroom junior-type houses. All are good houses but they are



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 2
in urgent need of proper maintenance. There is a four-bedroom guest house,
also in need of maintenance, that includes two bathrooms, two lavatories, a
dining-sitting room and a kitchen. In the same building as the kitchen there
is a small medical clinic.
4.        Laborers are housed in a village than has not been well planned and
is not properly maintained, the total fund for maintenance and new huts being
approximately hSd 200 per annum. There are no proper toilet facilities. It
is a health hazard to both labor and the cattle and it is bound to increase
the incidence of tapeworms in human beings and "measles" in the cattle.
5.        Other facilities comprise:
-  An old horse stable that is not used at present, but is
being altered so that it can be used as a feedlot.
-  A small dairy complex where the standings were never completed.
At the back of the cow shed there are three rooms: one is
used as a milk reception room, in the second milk is hand-
filled into bottles, and cheese is made in the third during
the wet season when there is a surplus of milk.
-  Yards for milking cows and calves and a series of night
enclosures for bulls and older animals.
-  A yard with a spray race.
-  A small veterinary clinic.  There is no post mortem room.
-  An upright concrete silo; presently unused.
-  A pigeon loft.
-  A new poultry building with a capacity for 3,000-4,000
laying hens.
-  Approximately 1.3 ha of irrigated garden in which
fruit and vegetables are grown.
6.        The perimeter fence and the internal fences either side of the
railway are badly in need of repair almost throughout their total length.
These fences are five-wire with sahab (Anogeissus leiocarpus) or durote
(Terminalia laxiflora) posts. There are no cattle grids at the various
entrances. The area is divided into 2.6 square kilometers (one square
mile) blocks by fire lines.



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 3
7.        Farm machinery available at the center includes:
- One Russian-built bulldozer.
- Two Russian-built "Supa" wheel tractors (neither are in
working order).
- One tractor- Massey-Ferguson "165".
- Assorted implements (two scraper blades, a seed drill, two
heavy-duty disc harrows, a ripper, a tyne harrow, a 3-disc
plough, a posthole digger, a sprayer and a buckrake),
almost all of which need repair.
8.        Services.  There is only one telephone; this is in the office of
the Officer in Charge and it is not working. There are two generators,
each rated at 22 KVA, but both inoperative. There are five boreholes; two
are in the south of the farm (in subdivisions 1 and 2 respectively), while
three are in subdivision 20. Undoubtedly, these five boreholes possess
sufficient total capacity for all the water requirements of the station; they
are, however, badly sited, two being at one end of the farm and three at the
other. There are also insufficient drinking places at the existing troughs.
One male nurse, who uses a small clinic attached to the kitchen of the guest
house, caters for the medical needs of everyone at the station. There is a
hospital in Ed Da'ein. There is an elementary school within the station
where children are taught until the sixth grade; the total number of students
is approximately 300 are there are six teachers. Additional permanent
classrooms are urgently required. Inadequate facilities undoubtedly explain
the dissatisfaction among staff and labor. The only recreation facilities
are a football pitch and an abandoned tennis court.
Staff

In addition, there are seven technical assistants working in the meteorolog-
ical office, which is administratively separate but located on the station.
The present labor force numbers 164, with 122 working in animal production
and 42 in range management.
10.       The budget of the station is estimated at hSd 100,000 (US$250,000)
annually. In 1974/75, funds directly at the disposal of the Officer in Charge
apparently totalled LSd 11,400 (US$28,500). The total budget is insufficient
to maintain the station properly and finance a relevant investigational
program. There is no revolving fund into which revenue could be paid in
order to assist in the financing of the station, so that there is no direct
financial incentive for managerial improvement.
11.       Some useful records of animal performance have been accumulated,
but they have not yet been properly analyzed. There have also been some
useful range menagement investigations, including a burning trial and trials
on the establishment and propagation of Andropogon gayanua. Unfortunately,
these trials have not been related to subsequent animal production.
Soils and Vegetation
12.       Soils.  The northeastern and eastern sectors of the station lie
within the Qoz Land System, and the other sectors within the Baggara Land
System. The two land systems merge gradually through a transition zone.
The land systems of the station are represented as follows:
(a) The Qoz Land System occupies approximately 30 percent of
the Research Station. It consists of a gently undulating
aeolian sand sheet and dune complex, with a variable cover
of aeolian sand and alluvial deposits towards the lower
lying margins. The system is divided into two subsystems
on the basis of this variable cover: the sand sheet and
dune complex, and the Qoz Alluvial Transition Zone.
(b) The Qoz Alluvial Transition Zone occupies 12 percent of
the Research Station and occurs as a broad transition zone
between the sand sheet and dune complex and the Baggara
Land System. The subsystem is divided into five recurring
landform units, each with varying depths of aeolian deposits.
The landform units are the same as those recognized in the
Sandy Naga'a subsystems, but the proportion of each within
the subsystem varies.
(c) The Baggara Land System is the major land system in the
Research Station and occupies 70 percent of the total
area. The Land System consists of a level to very gently
undulating alluvial plain with a complex system of very



ANNEX 6
Appendix I
Page 5
shallow, meandering, connected and disconnected channels
separated by long rectilinear sheet wash slopes, gentle
depressions (buta) and aeolian sand deposits.
(d)  The Sheet Wash Plain is a level to very undulating old
alluvial plain (locally caleed Naga'a) with slopes rearely
exceeding two percent. A thin cover of aeolian sand up to
10 centimeters deep may occur where rain splash and sheet
erosion have not exposed the heavier textured alluvium
beneath.
(e)  Buta are gentle depressions that occur throughout the Qoz
Alluvial Transition Zone and the Baggara Land System. The
soils are recent, cracked alluvial clays or sandy clays
and are frequently underlain by some sandy micaceious
alluvium.
This wide representation of the program area land systems makes the Ghazala
Gawazat especially useful in that it allows the Project to carry out research
on crop production and range management and to establish a model settlement
unit on which to experiment with forage production and livestock production;
eventually this research would result in the development of a mixed farming
system for the settlement areas.
13.       Vegetation.  Much of the range vegetation on the station is over-
grazed and thus provides a research base for various types of range manage-
ment interventions.  Four classes of range conditions can be identified; the
determinants of condition and the areas in each class are shown below:
Condition     Percentage Pasture Cover       % Total Area
Good                       80                       1
Fair                  65 - 80                     41
Poor                  20 - 65                     28
Very Poor                  25                     30
B.  Project Investments
14.       Given its existing facilities and its excellent location, the
Ghazala Gawazat Research Station would provide an excellent base from which
program specific adaptive research, extension and training facilities could be
carried out. This central location would permit the integration of these
efforts, thus facilitating a constant exchange of information both between
Project staff and between the staff and the ultimate beneficiaries.

Page 6
15.       Under the Project, the entire operation of Ghazala Gawazat would
be put under the direction of the Western Savannah Development Corporation
(WSDC). The Deputy Director General of WSDC would be given specific respon-
sibility for the operation of the station; he would be located at Ghazala
Gawazat and would report directly to the Director General of WSDC. For
purposes of Project implementation, six administrative units or divisions
would be established at Ghazala Gawazat, the heads of which would report to
the Deputy Director General. These would include:
(a) The Administrative Unit
(b) The Range Division
(c) The Livestock Division
(d) The Training Center
(e) The Crop Division
(f) The Land Management Unit
Staff for these units would be provided under the proposed IDA Project.
16.       The Administrative Unit.  This unit would be responsible for:
(a) all financial matters for the station such as budgets,
accounts and disbursement of funds;
(b) spending procurement requests based on requisitions from
other units to the WSDC Headquarter at Nyala:
(c) supervision and maintenance of building and structures
and issuing of requests for maintenance and repair to the
Civil Works Division at Nyala;
(d) management of catering and food supply for the station,
the Training Center and guest house;
(e)  recruitment of lower level staff;
(f) organization of accommodation of all staff; and
(g) provision of personnel services.
17.       Range Division, Livestock Division and Training Center.  These are
described in Annex 7.
18.       Crop Division.  This division is described in Annex 6.



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 7
19.       Farm Management Unit.  This unit would be responsible for the gen-
eral management of range, crops and livestock at the Ghazala Gawazat Research
Station itself. The unit would be headed by an internationally recruited
Farm Manager who would be provided under the ODM/Abu Dhabi Project.
20.       A Land Management Unit would also be stationed at Ghazala Gawazat,
although this unit would not report to the Deputy Director, but would be part
of the Land Management Division under the direct authority of the Director
General of the WSDC. This Division would be responsible for the opening of
new settlements and all aspects of land development in the program area. The
unit's work program and facilities are discussed in Annex 6. Provision has
been made in the estimates of the headquarter's unit costs for aerial surveys,
and a survey would be carried out of the whole of the settlement area in the
first year of the Project. Groundwork would be carried out by field teams,
each under a Land Allocation officer responsible for settlement and range
development. The unit would plan and supervise all aspects of land alloca-
tion and settlement and be responsible for the mapping of settlement units,
the registration of leases, and for the mapping and demarcation of range
areas. A surveyor, a cartographer and a draftsman would be provided for
the unit, which would have its own drawing office. Additional mapping and
designing facilities would be available at the headquarters unit at Nyala.
The unit would work closely with the Crop and Range Divisions and would
provide them with maps and with the required support in the field.

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Livestock Development Activities
General
1.        The organization of the pastoral community in the area represents
a successful adaptation to the environment. The security enjoyed by the
pastoralists is unparalleled among sedentary cultivators. Cattle represent a
store of wealth, and the single most attractive prospect for agricultural
investment, as land is not negotiable and knowledge for improving land pro-
ductivity is presently severely limited. However, because pasture is a com-
munal resource, an individual cannot alone strike a balane between pasture
resources and animal numbers, since to reduce his herd size carries no
guarantee that the slack created will not be taken up by others. Analysis of
herd performance has shown conclusively the superior performance of migratory
herds. However, the system is being increasingly threatened by expanding
cultivation and by range pressure, as well as by competition from sedentary
herds.
2.        The Southern and Eastern Districts of South Darfur have important
livestock resources. Stocking density varies with season, the highest con-
centrations occuring during the winter, at which time migratory herds are
grazing along Bahr el Arab. Recent aerial and ground surveys have indicated
the following (rounded) numbers:
Cattle         Sheep           Goats
('000)   %   ('000)    %    ('000)    %
Southern District /1
(ODM/Abu Dhabi Project Area)       600     4      100    1.0     100    1.0
Eastern District /2                 1,300   10      600    4.0      100    1.0
(Project Area)
SUB TOTAL                      1,900   14      700    5.0     200    2.0
Whole Country /3                   14,000  100  13,500  100.0  10,500  100.0
Source:
/1  S. Darfur Land Use Planning Survey (HTS 1974); from other evidence, these
figures appear to be a 30% under-estimate. The area will be resurveyed
in 1976 as part of the National Livestock Census.
/2   Aerial livestock census and ground survey, 1975.
/3   There are in addition, an estimated 35,000 camels, 40,000 donkeys and
8,000 horses.
Source: National Planning Commission - July 1975.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
Present Situation
3.        The present situation concerning livestock production in the
Project Area is characterized by:
(i)  heavy pressure on the available grazing areas and the
absence of range management; 1/
(ii)  approximately three months of semi-starvation, especially
of cattle (late dry season) which coincides with the
calving period;
(iii)  a low offtake rate due to the influence of poor nutrition
and disease;
(iv)  an unstable equilibrium between transhumant and sedentary
livestock production and crop production which with the
increasing transformation of pastoralists into crop producers
and the resulting encroachment on grazing land might lead
to a breakdown of pastoral livestock production;
(v)  inadequate animal husbandry practices, especially in
the rearing of young stock, leading to stunted growth,
low fertility and lack of resistance to disease; and
(vi)  the fact that transhumant livestock production represents
an efficient utilization of the major resources of the area
and that the Baggara are already partly integrated into the
cash economy.
Preconditions for Livestock Development
4.        Numerous steps would have to be taken if sustained development of
the livestock industry in the Project area should occur. These are as follows:
(i)  broaden the knowledge about livestock movements in the
area and related constraints which are met by the pastoralists
and sedentary groups during the dry and rainy seasons;
(ii)  accelerate non-formal education and training of the decision
makers and the young stockmen in order to increase the aware-
ness of the problems facing the community as a whole and
to popularize the technology which is already available
and would improve the income and standard of living of
the pastoralists;
(iii)  provide better human health and veterinary services and stock
water supply, in order to gain the confidence and cooperation
of the livestock producers who would carry out the proposed
interventions;
1/   The Savannah Development Project - Phase II HTS Report estimates that on
the basis of herbivore biomass calculations that stock in excess of
carrying capacity by land system varies from 25-400%.



ANNEX 7
Page 3
(iv) modernize livestock production in the area bringing about
gradually:
- polarization of livestock and crop production allowing
for uninhibited movement of migratory herds;
- introduction of forage production both as part of mixed
agricultural production farming systems in the agricultural
settlements and in the Bahr and Regeba land systems, in
order to alleviate the shortage of forage during the last
three months of the dry season;
- introduction of better range management in the area including
deferred and rotational grazing, provision of additional
stock water and pasture emprovement;
- better animal husbandry and calf rearing methods;
- more efficient veterinary services;
- improved livestock marketing which would increase the
producer's share in the consumer's price; and
- testing the possibilities for initiating the establishing
of a saving's scheme for pastoralists, which would
satisfy expectations for investing their cash surpluses
according to their needs and traditional customs.
The implementation of the proposed measures requires different services
coordinated within the framework of the Project's management and organization.
These services would be carried out by the Livestock Division which would be
located at Ghazala Gawazat and would include the following activities:
- gather data, and conduct non-formal education and planning;
- initiate the establishment of the Baggara Livestock
Development Committee;
- supervise veterinary services;
- establish two small breeding herds; and
- prepare for the Baggara Pilot Savings Scheme, and the
Baggara backgrounding and sales ranch for implementation
during a possible Second Phase Project of the Development
Program (Second Phase Project).
Data Gathering, Non-Formal Education and Planning in the Camps
5.        The Head of the Livestock Division would, together with the Heads
of the Range Division and Training Center and the District Veterinary Officer,



ANNEX 7
Page 4
prepare and carry out during the first two dry seasons (January-May) visits
to Baggara camps in the Bahr-El-Arab for the purpose of establishing a dialo-
gue with the pastoralists at the camp (farig) level, gathering detailed data
on movements and constraints and working out with the elders schedules for
the provision of veterinary services as well as individual projects related
to the provision of stock water in the context of organized grazing in
certain critical areas. It would be particularly important that the group
spend enough time in each camp to enable them to establish good relations,
explain fully the Project concept and to allay any apprehensions that solu-
tions would be imposed on the community. All information and data would be
recorded and in cooperation with the Land Management Unit put on maps to
serve as a base for developing a master plan for range management in the
Project Area.
The Formation of a Livestock Development Committee
6.        The key to the success of the Project is to gain the confidence
and cooperation of the pastoralists because on that hinges the eventual
acceptance and implementation of the innovations in grazing management which
will arise out of the data gathering. It is essential that at every step
of the Project the pastoralists get increasingly involved to the point of
feeling that it is their project and that the local authorities are closely
associated with all aspects of Project implementation.
7.        To achieve this a Livestock Development Committee would be formed
under the chairmanship of the Assistant Commissioner in Ed Da'ein comprising
two representatives from each morhal (stock route) elected by the pastoralists
using that morhal under the proviso that they be themselves transhumant
livestock owners. Additional members would be the Assistant Commissioner of
Abu Matariq, the Deputy Director General posted at Ghazala Gawazat and the
heads of the Livestock and Range Divisions and the Training Center. The
Committee would meet twice yearly at Ghazala Gawazat at the beginning and at
the end of the rainy season and once yearly in the Bahr El Arab. The pastora-
list members of the Committee would act as links to the camps which they
represented and would be the contacts for all Project staff during work in
their area. The meetings would discuss all related aspects of Project imple-
mentation and thus continue the dialogue established during the data gathering
visits to the camps. Dates and location of the annual vaccination campaign
would be agreed and proposals for special Project related development such as:
stock water, grazing management and forage production, would be studied.
Adaptive Livestock Research
8.        The Division would carry out the following adaptive research:
(i) monitoring of a number of transhumant (long-term and
short-term) and of sedentary herds in order to
establish vital statistics, husbandry practices and
nutrition parameters through periodic visits;



ANNEX 7
Page 5
(ii) monitoring a similar number of herds where owners
have agreed to the introduction of certain breeding,
calf-rearing and feeding innovations;
(iii) investigating the possibility of relieving the pressure
on the grazing areas by buying 14-16 month calves from
transhumant herds, backgrounding them for 12 months at
Ghazala Gawazat, sending them to feedlots in the eastern
provinces for finishing and sale, and comparing the
economics of this procedure with the present practice
of retaining bulls to the age of 5-6 years;
(iv) conducting at Ghazala Gawazat fattening on an experi-
mental scale of two age groups of bulls from sedentary
and transhumant herds, utilizing available industrial
by-products such as groundnut and sesame cake;
(v) investigating in cooperation with the Range Division
and the Crop Division, the possibilities of improving
existing smallholder sedentary cropping systems by inte-
gration of crop and livestock husbandry by:
(a)  establishing of permanent crop/fallow rotation to
better crop production and that of by-product
utilization for livestock feeding;
(b) introducing suitable grasses and legumes, which could be
grazed by livestock on the fallow area of the rotation;
(c) increasing the utilization of browse on the area of
range allocated to sedentary smallholders during the
dry season;
(d) introducing a deferred grazing system on the area of
range allocated to the sedentary smallholders;
(e) utilizing Acacia senegal or a suitable legume, browse
bush, and/or a small tree as live fencing to demarcate
fields. This would enable the smallholder to collect
a crop of gum arabic or add an additional supply of
browse for his livestock;
(f) testing the possibility of introducing some degree
of specialized milk production or the growing-out
of beef animals on the sedentary holdings and in the
settlements. Milk production could be encouraged also
by the use of improved Kenana bulls as sires, the use
of mineral supplements and by the feeding of legume
crop by-products such as groundnut haulm and cake
to the milking cows.



ANNEX 7
Page 6
A total of 11 man months of consultant services would be provided to allow
for the services of an internationally recognized livestock research scientist
to assist in planning and supervising the research and breeding program.
Veterinary Services
9.        The Project would provide the necessary investment and operating
costs for the veterinary services in the Project area to allow for their
expansion so that they may render a more efficient service to the livestock
owners. Six veterinary dispensaries and adequate housing for staff would
be constructed during the first two years of the Project and located according
to density of livestock population and migration patterns. The District
veterinary headquarters in Ed Da'ein would be expanded by a diagnostic unit
and staff housing. Vehicles would be provided for professional staff including
three trucks to be equipped as mobile units in order to enable a stepped up
program of field vaccinations. An initial stock of drenches and acaricides
and equipment for their dispensing would be provided for sale to the livestock
owners who would be instructed in their proper utilization and thus be able to
treat their own animals. In conjunction with the animal health program
extension work on Hides and Skin improvement would be strengthened by the
addition of field facilities and staff to train the livestock owners in
proper methods of flaying and drying. The day-to-day implementation of this
component would be the responsibility of the District Veterinary Services
Division of the Animal Resources Department in Khartoum through the Assistant
Commissioner for Animal Resources in Nyala. Supervision of activities would
be the responsibility of the Livestock Division of the Project which would
further assist in procurement through the Western Savannah Development Corpora-
tion (WSDC) and the coordination of field activities. The veterinary services
presently provided are much appreciated by the livestock owners and the
strengthening of these services in the Project area is therefore part of the
effort to gain the confidence and cooperation and to pave the way for range
management interventions and increased sale of surplus stock by limiting the
risks of heavy losses through outbreaks of disease.
Establishment of a Breeding Herd
10.       The Livestock Division would assume responsibility for the
existing herd of cattle at Ghazala Gawazat and would initially cull heavily
to relieve the present pressure on the grazing blocks. Two small herds of
Kenana and Butana cattle would be established to breed bull material for
sale to interested livestock producers in the area. Surplus female stock
would be disposed of through yearly sales in order to keep the herds to an
agreed limit. The present "nomadic cattle" herd would be culled except for
a selected group of breeding stock. As soon as range conditions on the
station would allow it a gradual addition of outstanding individuals would
be made by direct purchase from herds in the Project Area, out of the funds



ANNEX 7
Page 7
received from culling. Thus, towards the end of the Project two breeding
herds of about 100 cows each would be established from which in due course
good bull material would be sold to the producers.
B. The Range Division
11.       The Range Division would be located at Ghazala Gawazat with three
experimental range stations located at Nyamis, Abu Matariq and one in the
northern grazing area (North Darfur Province) yet to be sited.
The Range Division would carry out the following:
- participate in data gathering, non-formal education and
planning, and the establishment of the Livestock Develop-
ment Committee;
- implement range research at Ghazala Gawazat and range improve-
ment experiments at the three range stations;
- work out, on the basis of information gathered during dry
season meeting in the camps, a range management master plan
for the Project Area;
- participate in the training of the range school;
- assist the Crop Division in the establishment of grazing
areas in the settlements; and
- cooperate with the Crop Division in the introduction of
forage production on the Settlement Farm Unit.
The Division's role in the data gathering, non-formal education and planning
as well as in the Development Committee is described under the Livestock
Division (para 5).
12.       Range Research objectives would include:
(i)  observations in the field and at Ghazala Gawazat in order
to establish practical values for carrying capacities of
the various types of grazing areas;
(ii)  The conduct on Ghazala Gawazat and in the areas of the three
range stations of experiments with range management procedures
and forage production innovations with particular emphasis on
examining costs and feasibility of large scale application,
The experiments would include:



ANNEX 7
Page 8
- fire line establishment; grazing area demarcation; controlled
burning; breaking of naga soils and their possible seeding;
selective bush clearing; forage production under dry-land
flash flood and receding flood; cultivation with methods
which could be applied by the pastoralists; improvement of
range land by over-sowing; surface water retention partly
as a means of extending the forage growth period in the Bahr
and Regeba Land Systems and partly for erosion prevention
and vegetation improvement in the northern grazing areas.
Range Management Master Plan
13.       The Division would tabulate and map all information gathered during
the dry season meetings in the camps, and taking into consideration rainfall
and vegetation establish a master plan for range management in the various
land systems of the Project Area. Based on this master plan the Division
would work out a proposal for a simple practical first approach to range
management procedures with emphasis more on its acceptability to the pastora-
list and the ease of implementation than on professional completeness. The
acceptance of such interventions by the Livestock Development Committee would
not necessarily mean acceptance by all pastoralists and a continuous dialogue
and non-formal education would be needed to get implementation under way in
some areas.
Northern Grazing Areas
14.       A particular problem exists in the northern grazing areas where
the grazing of animals of pastoralists from the Project Area during wet
years is followed by grazing of several other pastoralist groups. A scheme
of deferred grazing in this area is particularly needed as this is the area
already showing signs of severe deterioration. Funds for aerial mapping
of this area are included under the Land Management Division of the WSDC.
This should be given highest priority and take place in Year One of Project
implementation. In view of the many pastoralist groups involved, negotiations
preceding the introduction of Range Management may require joint negotiations
between the authorities and the traditional leaders and quite possibly the
introduction and enforcement of provincial regulations.
Grazing Schemes and Stock Water Development
15.       Certain areas of the Southern Qoz and the Regeba Land Systems could
be grazed more intensively during the early part of the dry season provided
stock water points were available. This would delay the arrival of stock in
the Bahr El Arab area, thus alleviating the grazing pressure on that area.
Boreholes and some hafirs are already present in the area, but some are not
yet operational and some have fallen into disrepair. There is considerable
interest on the part of the pastoralists to expand the number of watering
points. It would be extremely important for the Project's relationships
with the pastoralists that action would be taken at an early stage to comply



ANNEX 7
Page 9
with this request. It would, however, be equally important that no stock
water point should be established except in the framework of a system of range
management agreed to by all involved in the area. Such schemes
offer a good opportunity of close cooperation between the Project staff and
the pastoralists and an equal opprtunity to introduce the principle of self-
help by having a reasonable part of development and maintenance cost paid
for by the pastoralists themselves.
The Settlement Areas
16.       The Range Division would be called upon to assist the Crop Division
in the laying out of grazing lands in the settlements, to assess the number
of stock they should carry and to devise grazing systems to be adopted, (Map,
IBRD 12473).
Forage Production
17.       It is of utmost importance to introduce at the earliest possible
forage crops in the production pattern which could be grown in rotation on
the settlement units and which are needed as a prerequisite for developing
mixed farming systems. Funds for adaptive research in this field have been
included under the Crop Division (Annex 6). The Division would render such
assistance as would be required during the field trial stage of this adaptive
research. Forage production trials would also be undertaken by the Mechan-
ized Farming Corporation at the Umm Agaga Farm and the Forage Production
Officer of the Division would be closely involved and provide such profes-
sional assistance as would be required.
The Training Center
18.       Based on the premise that the introduction of improved range manage-
ment would entirely depend on the acceptance of the proposed measures by
the pastoralists within the framework of their traditional social structure
a training component, aimed at motivating the pastoralists for these becomes
indispensable. The long term success of the Project hinges on the gradual
introduction of relevant range management systems, both for the start-up
period of research and experimentation, as well as for the initial imple-
mentation and further development and a considerable number of professional
and intermediate level range staff would be needed. There is at the moment
no training facility for such staff in the Sudan and the Project would,
therefore, need to train this staff itself. It would be essential that
all Project staff were familiar not only with the overall concept and ob-
jectives of the Project, but also with the social structure and value systems
of the people with whom they would come in contact during their work. There-
fore, organized staff training for all staff during the first year of the
Project and for new staff members thereafter would be a necessary aspect.
19.       During the Project's implementation the need will arise, from time
to time, to gather groups of cultivators or pastoralists at Ghazala Gawazat
to demonstrate various aspects of the work being carried out by the Project.



ANNEX 7
Page 10
Similarly, there would be a need to inform and train Government officials
working with or in relation to the Project. To meet these needs a Training
Center would be established at Ghazala Gawazat comprising of three classrooms,
a dormitory, kitchen, dining room, and offices for teachers and equipped with
student transport, a mobile unit for non-formal training in the field, and
facilities for the production of audio visual material, lecture notes and
simple pamphlets and photographs. A small reference library would also be
included. Instructors would come from Project staff, most of whom would be
field staff.
Non-Formal Education
20.       Non-formal education would aim at two categories of pastoralists:
the decision makers in the camp and young stockmen. Non-formal education
of elders would mostly be undertaken in connection with data gathering in
the camps during the dry season and would aim at explaining the Project
concept, the principles of range management, animal husbandry, animal health,
marketing of livestock and the advantage of cash saving against future losses
as opposed to the carrying of surplus stock. Slide shows and films preferably
from the Project Area would be used as training aids. Non-formal education of
young stockmen would be in the form of annual courses of 90 days duration
during the months of August, September and October at the Training Center.
The course would consist of classroom and outdoor training, with demonstra-
tions in grazing management, calf rearing, general husbandry and health,
livestock marketing and simple processing techniques for hides and skins and
milk as well as a thorough explanation of the Project objectives. The course
would cater for 60 young stockmen each year and their participation would be
solicited during visits to the camps. Each student would receive board and
lodging and be issued two sets of clothes.
The Range School
21.       This school would conduct 12 month courses for 15 agricultural
graduates (Shambat). The two best students of the first 5 courses would be
given fellowships to continue training overseas for a degree in Range Manage-
ment. The Range Division would take part in this training partly by officers
of the Division assisting in the planning of the curriculum and giving lectures
and demonstration and partly by arranging for student visits to various field
activities. The course would produce more staff than the Project itself would
need; but they would find ready employment with the Range Services of the
Department of Natural Resources. Courses for the range school and the non-
formal education would be conducted at Ghazala Gawazat in the same facilities.
22.       In order to continue investigation into the sociology of livestock
producers in the Project Area and especially to monitor their reaction to
the Project activities, funds have been included for 5 man/years of socio-
logical studies for a team of sociologists specializing in this field to



ANNEX 7
Page 11
live in the camps and with sedentary livestock owners. This study would be
carried out under the auspices of the Economic and Social Research Council
of the National Council for Research in Khartoum. A special feature of the
Training Center would be the employment of an Information and Publications
Officer whose main function would be to prepare broadcast and publication
material partly of a general information nature and partly about the Project
activities directed at the population in the Project Area and at the public in
general. As transistor radios are becoming more and more common in the camps
the activity would play a major role in gaining the cooperation of the population
which the Project aims to be of benefit to.
Preparation for Project Phase II Activities
23.       During the Project period the heads of the Livestock and Range
Divisions and their staff would prepare and discuss with the Livestock Develop-
ment Committee and individual pastoralists the possible establishment of the
Baggara Pilot Savings Scheme and a Baggara Backgrounding and Sales Ranch for
implementation during a Second Phase Project.
24.       The Baggara Pilot Savings Scheme.  During the data gathering visits
to the camps, the team would explore with the elders the feasibility of
establishing in the Dar area a savings scheme which initially would act as a
safe depository for surplus cash deposited as a safeguard against future
losses (paras. 4 and 20). It is anticipated that such a scheme would be
located in the Dar (homeland) area, and would carry out transactions at the
local markets during the rainy season, and in the Bahr El Arab area during the
dry season by means of a mobile unit. However, the actual operation of such a
scheme is not expected earlier than in a Second Phase Project. Before
the scheme could be established the principle would have to be endorsed by the
Livestock Development Committee; also, a supervisory committee would have to
be formed, and the possibilities of linking activities to the Agricultural
Develoment Bank or another established banking institution investigated. A
similar saving system among the rural population in Swaziland was successful
and might serve as a model.
25.       The Baggara Backgrounding and Sales Ranch.  It was felt that live-
stock producers at present receives less than their share of the consumer
price for livestock (Annex 16). The WSDC, by monitoring markets and proposing
relevant changes to local authorities could contribute to rectify this situa-
tion. The recent establishment of the Livestock and Meat Marketing Commission
will further aid in this direction. It would, however, seem that during a
possible Phase II project, there is a basis for the establishment of a back-
grounding and sales ranch (Ranch) which would be the collective property of
the Baggara and eventually develop into a cooperative. The Ranch would buy
livestock from the Project Area and sell to the East or for export, either
directly or after a period of backgrounding. Any profits realized after



ANNEX 7
Page 12
deduction of cost would be channeled to the producer.  The area which has
been identified by a previous Mission 1/ totals some 360 km (36,000 ha) and
is located on the south side of the railway line 60 kilometers east of Ed
Da'ein, 240 kilometers west of Nyala, and 3 kilometers west of Abu Gabra
railway station. The site is strategically located for the livestock markets
at Ed Da'ein, Gimelaya, Abu Gabra and Abu Matariq. Recorded data are not
available for each of these markets, but the total annual throughput is
estimated to be in the vicinity of 18,000 cattle and 40,000 sheep annually.
Most animals are purchased in the rainy season. The whole area is composed of
sand and sand dunes, and the rainfall averages 450 millimeters annually. The
site contains no cultivation or settlement and it is part of a vast range
extending to the North, South and East. Areas of cultivation surround most
settlements in the locality. Those closest to the proposed site are Abu Gabra
to the South, Dar est Salaam to the West, and Mical immediately to the North
of the railway line. From Dar es Salaam cultivation continues for several
kilometers to the West, but the other cultivated areas are merely surrounded
by rangeland similar to those of the site. To the West some land has been
demarcated by the Rural Development Department for ranching and water bore-
holes have been installed, but little other development has taken place to
date. The main migration routes of the Maaliya and Rizeiquat tribes pass to
the east of the area, following a line of Government wateryards, while some of
the Maaliya followed by Ha,ar. Barti and Koahla pass to the west through Taha.,
The Maaliya and Rizeiquat are mostly cattle owners but the other three groups
who enter the area considerably later are traditionally sheep and camel
herders. During the wet season, there is some surface water in the south of
the area but the absence of permanent water or Government wateryards results
in considerable underutilization of grasses which are then often burned.
Prospects for developing groundwater on the site are good.
26.       This component has been foreseen for a Second Phase Project because
considerable preparation would be needed in order to ensure the full partici-
pation of the Baggara themselves. The issues which must be addressed are:
ownership of the Ranch; organization and management; the degree of financial
participation of the Baggara in the running cost and working capital of
the farm; and credit arrangements, if any, in connection with the provision-
of working capital. The establishment of the Ranch would offer a viable
alternative to the present markets which are dominated entirely by the mer-
chants and their agents, thus forcing buyers to adopt more producer oriented
price policies and trade procedures. It would also offer an opportunity to
introduce purchasing on the basis of weight and a system of grades. It is
believed that the Ranch would gradually develop into a cooperative offering a
new possibility for local investment by the Baggara, and that it would offer
a model for similar ranches to be established in the Bahr El Arab area and
elsewhere in the country.
