June 1975).
April 11, 1977



ANNEX 8
Page 1
SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Water Facilities and Buildings
A. Background
Rural Water Supplies
1.        In the Savanna Belt, all settlements are located close to or
within easy reach of natural watering points or artificial water supplies.
Even the stock routes are carefully selected by nomadic pastoralists along
dependable water points, either natural or artificial. In the rural areas,
the perennial and seasonal streams and natural lakes and ponds supplemented
by shallow wells are the principal sources of water. The latter are usually
located in the dry beds of wadis. Since 1947, the Rural Water Corporation
(RWC) has constructed a network of boreholes reaching into the Nubian and
Umm Rwaba Sandstone acquifers under the sandy qoz areas. RWC also construct
hafirs 1/ which are artificially excavated and constructed reservoirs into
which surface run-off is diverted for storage. The hafirs are usually
found in the clay areas. As a result of this program, sedentary agricultural
activities have developed around these sources of water. The towns and
population and developed centers also have their own water supply in the
form of wateryards (as the boreholes fully developed to cater for
domestic and stock needs are called) or hafirs, depending on the geological
conditions of the locality.
2.        Shallow wells will continue to be important sources of water in
the basement complex, and especially in the alluvial sandy and loamy clay
regions. Shallow wells provide water for domestic and livestock use, and
in some cases support irrigated vegetable farms; the wells are usually 2 - 4 m
deep and have quadrangular timber-shored walls. Each farmer draws water by a
gourd on the end of a rope and distributes it to each plot through a network
of open earth ditches. During the wet season, the water table may rise to the
surface, and in drought years the wells sometimes have to be deepened.
3.        Wateryards, built around boreholes by the RWC, are common focal
settlement points in the qoz regions. Besides supplying vital water for do-
mestic and stock use, they are social centers where trading posts may develop.
A variety of pumps driven by diesel engines are used to raise the water from
1/   The plural of hafir is hafira, but in this report the English plural
form will be used.



ANNEX 8
Page 2
cased boreholes extending into the sandstone acquifer to an elevated tank,
usually of about 50,000 liters (11,000 Ig) capacity. The water is
then distributed to taps, from which people draw water at the official rate
of Sd. piaster 0.2 per 4 gallon tin (18 liter) or (ESd 0.11 or US$0.28 per
m ). A metal trough is used to water livestock, which are assessed per head
according to the local rate of consumption. The pumps and engines are of
various makes: British, German, Italian, Yugoslav and Egyptian. The most
predominant is the combination of the Lister Diesel 6 hp Engine and EDECO
reciprocating "donkey" pumps. Due to the various makes of engines and pumps
used, poor administration and lack of funds, maintenance and up-keep of the
wateryards is generally inadequate.
4.        Rural Councils are responsible for the maintenance of installa-
tions as well as for collection of water charge. There are some reports
of misappropriation of revenues as the water meters are often out of order
and are rarely repaired, so there is no means of cross-checking collections.
The mechanical installations need to be standardized and regular preventive
maintenance and servicing procedures should be set up to ensure the contin-
uous functioning of these vital facilities. The RWC is planning to estab-
lish maintenance units for every 40 wateryards. Spare parts are procured
for all water yards by RWC at the national level and distributed to the
various Rural Councils at cost. The Councils have presently no facili-
ties for the maintenance of the wateryards, although the breakdown of a
pump or engine can cause very serious hardships to the farmers in the
vicinity. Therefore, the maintenance of the wateryards is carried out
by RWC on a contract basis. In cases of shortage of water profiteers
cbarge as much as 6 piasters for an 18 1 tin of water (hSd 3.5 or US$8.40 per
m ) or 30 times as much as the official rate. Twinning 1/ of boreholes is
being considered as a solution in some critical locations, but in view of the
high cost this is not a viable substitute for a well-orgnized maintenance
organization. With the proliferation of boreholes the underground water
supply system is another area where careful monitoring and study is needed.
5.        The hafirs ire rectangular earth structures.  Their capacities range
from 3,000 - 85,000 m . Over 350 hafirs have been constructed since 1947 in
Northern and Southern Kordofan, and over 150 in Northern and Southern Darfur
Provinces. There are in addition 12 - 15 dams and weirs. The latter have
capacities of from 60,000 to over 1,500,000 m .  (Hillalya Dam in Northern
Darfur built in 1960). Hafirs accumulate surface run-off from the natural
catchment of the nearby hills which is diverted through strategically-located
ditches. Some hafirs are fed by artifically-prepared catchment basins. The
sediment is trapped in a stilling-bowl and the flow into the reservoir is con-
trolled by a reverse flow sluice valve. The outlet starts from a galvanized-
1/   Twinning means the availability of a stand-by fully equipped borehole.



ANNEX 8
Page 3
iron strainer then through a 4 in. asbestos/cement pipe to a masonry or
concrete-lined well from which water is pumped for distribution by means of
stand-pipes with funnels and/or to distribution troughs.
6.        The location of hafirs is limited to the clay formations.  The
bottom of the hafir is usually at least 1 m above the bed rock, and there is
a 2 m high embankment built from the excavation aro nd the reservoir. Some
hafirs are twinned, such as at Habila (2 x 40,000 m ) and El Fasher. This
is done to increase capacity when the thickness of the clay stratum limits
the depth of the hafir. In most cases, it is necessary to provide filtration
and treatment plants to render surface water fit for human and stock consump-
tion. Various methods are used for this purpose. These are again not prop-
erly serviced and maintained. In some cases, arrangements similar to those
described under water yards are used in conjunction with hafirs for distri-
bution purposes.
7.        Hafirs, being dependent on surface run-off, are liable to dry up
in drought years. They also suffer from evaporation losses as well as
seepage due to sub-surface intrusions of pervious formations. Silting is
also a problem, although in well laid out and designed hafirs most of the
silt is trapped in the collecting ditches and stilling bowls.
B. Proposals for Savannah Development Project
8.        The RWC has the capability and the technical expertise to drill
and equip new boreholes as well as to construct the ancillary works connected
with a water yard. However, it is not furnished sufficient funds from the
central budget and the Rural Councils to effectively maintain the ones it
has constructed. Furthermore, no maintenance work has recently been carried
out on hafirs. Therefore, Government has requested that, at least during the
Project investment period, the maintenance responsibility for wateryards be
assumed by the Western Savannah Development Corporation (WSDC).
9.        A well managed water organization should be self-supporting..
With maintenance and operation responsibilities resting with the Rural
Councils, who must rely on an underfunded RWC for maintenance capability, the
wateryard operation evidently does not break even and an adequate job of
maintenance is not provided. It would thus be necessary to establish within
the WSPA an organization capable of construction and maintenance of civil
works. This Civil Works Division would be provided with suitable plant and
resources. It would be responsible for hafir construction and maintenance
and, since a major component of the Project would be buildings, it would have
the capability of supervising and execution of building construction, if
necessary. The Civil Works Division would be headquartered in Nyala under
the WSDC.



ANNEX 8
Page 4
10.       Since the Project area is relatively remote it may be difficult
to attract qualified personnel for successful implementation. One of the
requisites for attracting personnel is adequate housing. It is proposed
to construct housing, workshops, offices, laboratories and other necessary
Project related buildings.
11.       A Wateryard Division would also be created in the WSDC.  This
Division would be responsible for the maintenance and operation of wateryards;
it would headquartered in Ed Da'ein, where the provincial wateryard mainten-
ance center is located.
The Civil Works Division
12.       A Civil Works Division would be created within the WSDC. It would be
headed by a Chief Engineer with sufficient staff to direct the operations of
the Construction and Maintenance Sections. (See Organization Chart of the
WSDC in Annex 15, Organization and Management). The Civil Works Division
would:
(a) contruct and rehabilitate hafirs in those areas in the
two districts where ground water in unavailable;
(b) contract with the RWC to open existing boreholes as
required for the resettlement of farmers;
(c) construct on force account to the approved designs
and standards of the Public Works Department, those
buildings for which local contractors are not prepared
to tender;
(d) provide engineering supervision for those buildings
constructed under contract.
Hafir Construction and Rehabilitation
13.       The Civil Works Division would have the responsiblity for the
construction and rehabilitation of hafirs in the Project area. It is
planned to construct about 20 new hafirs and rehabilitate about 20
existing hafirs. Hafirs would be constructed in those areas where ground-
water is not available and where soil conditions are favorable (i.e.,
where there are impervious soils).  They would most generally be located
near wadis, so that diversions could be made from the wadis into the
hafirs, or the flood water spilling over the wadi banks could be collected
and stored in the hafirs. The hafirs would be located by Civil Works
Division Engineers with the assistance of professionals from the Range
Division so that they would have the best locations from an engineering



ANNEX 8
Page 5
point of view and at the same time be located in accordance with good
range management practices. Hafirs should be located so that the most
water would be stored for the least excavation. For instance, topography
permitting, excavation should equal the dike embankment plus the embank-
ment shrinkage. However, most of the time topography would probably not
permit such an ideal solution, and in order to store the maximum amount
of water without excessive evaporation losses, the excavated section
would exceed the embankment quantity in the surrounding dikes. From a
range management viewpoint hafirs should be located so that over-grazing
would not occur around them; ideally the water in the hafir would be ex-
hausted at the same time as the range.
14.       Investment costs for the hafirs to be constructed under the Project
are based upon a 6.5 m depth, base dimensions of 40 m x 20 m, and top    3
dimensions of 91 m x 46 m. This depth would provide a capacity of 1500 m
with all the water stored in the excavated area and a minimum of loss due to
evaporation. The side slopes were assumed to be two horizontal to one vertical
and the slopes would be four horizontal to one vertical. The spoil would be
placed 10 m from the top of the excavation at the ends and 5 m at the sides.
15.       Hafirs would be constructed with inlet works in accordance with the
Sudan Government standards and practice. This normally consists of a canal
discharging into a stilling bowl which in turn is drained by an inlet well and
pipe with a sluice valve and an energy dissipator in the storage area.
16.       The hafir outlet works consist of a strainer in the storage area
on the end of a pipe leading to an outlet well structure. From the well
the water is pumped into pipes leading to drinking troughs. If the water is
to be for human consumption, a filter would be installed which would be
maintained by the Civil Works Division.
17.       A seven barbed wire fence with steel posts set in concrete would
be constructed around the hafir to keep animals from entering and to discour-
age trespassing by the people. A caretaker/guard would be necessary to oper-
ate the pump and protect the property from vandalism.
18.       As with new construction, hafirs would not be rehabilitated where
adequate ground water exists. However, in some areas old hafirs need new
inlet and outlet works and valves, and the fences need repair. Where these
are necessary to the Project, Civil Works Division crews would perform the
work necessary to place the hafirs back into operation. Rehabilitation does
not mean desiltation, because it would take as long as three years to dry up
a hafir to the point that it would be possible to remove any silt with machin-
ery, and in the meantime the facility would no doubt be needed to supply water
to the animals. Considering the time involved, it would be more advantageous
to construct a new hafir.
19.       Schistosomiasis (Bilharzia).  All hafirs in the Sudan provide a
home for the fresh water snails that host the parasites which cause this



ANNEX 8
Page 6
disease. Since there are a total of 500 hafirs in the province, the forty-one
in this Project would have no appreciable effect on the incidence of the
disease. Although fencing would be used to discourage people from entering
the hafir areas, it probably would not be 100% effective. Accordingly, an
annual cost for the purchase and application of molluscicides has been in-
cluded in the tentative cost estimate for a possible Second Phase Project of
the Development Program (Second Phase Project) after the Project hafirs
are placed in service. If the application of the molluscicides proves to be
successful, Government, hopefully, would follow suit for the hafirs and
natural ponds.
20.       Hafir Construction Schedule.  It is expected that the first year
of the Project would be spent in procurement of vehicles and equipment as
well as in the recruitment of personnel. Actual construction and rehabilitation
of hafirs would not begin until year four, and with the exception of the rainy
season, would continue for the next three years; at that time twenty new
hafirs would have been completed and twenty-one old ones rehabilitated.
Building Construction
21.       During the first four years of the Project, it would be necessary to
construct a total of 295 buildings at an estimated cost of hS 2,010,000
(US$5,025,000). Of these, 170 would be houses for the Project staff. The
rest of the buildings would consist of offices, workshops stores, and specialty
buildings.
22.       Since Nyala would be the WSDC headquarters, it would be necessary to
provide twenty-six residences for staff along with offices, stores and other
support buildings, bringing the total number of buildings to be constructed to
fifty three. At Ed-Da"ein (the Veterinary Services headquarters and the Water
Yard Maintenance Center) eighteen houses along with stores and other ancillary
buildings would be constructed. The greatest number of buildings, 112, would
be constructed at Ghazala Gawazat; fifty-one of these would be residences and
the rest offices, stores, a training center and other supporting buildings.
The remaining buildings would be constructed at the three nucleus development
centers at Ed Da'ein, Dimsu, and Abu Gabra.
23.       Construction Materials.  Presently with only a small amount of
construction underway in the area, construction materials are sometimes
difficult to get. There are now two cement factories in the Sudan, and a
third factory is under construction; however, if their output is not suf-
ficient, there is a possibility that an undetermined amount of cement may have
to be imported. Unless cement is reserved from the local production, much of
the work would have to use imported cement. Bricks are locally manufactured
in the Project Area but production would have to be accelerated in order to



ANNEX 8
Page 7
produce enough for the buildings contemplated. Some of the buildings would
probably have to be constructed with locally made concrete or sand blocks.
Concrete sand would be found at or near building sites, but the coarse
aggregates would have to be hauled by truck or shipped by rail from the
basement complex area. Coarse aggregates for concrete or block work at Ed
Da'ein and Ghazala Gawazat would go by rail from Nyala to Ed Da'ein. All
other building materials would have to be imported and shipped from Port
Sudan. Prefabricated buildings were considered, but finally judged to be
too costly.
24.       Although some building materials are transported by truck from
Khartoum, most of them should come by rail, which would involve a minimum
of trucking since the major building sites are in the vicinity of rail points.
In fact, if a siting were available, materials could be unloaded from the
railroad at a point some fifteen kilometers from Ghazala Gawazat, the largest
of the building sites. Other sites on the railroad are Nyala and Ed Da'ein.
Seventy per cent of the buildings would be located at these three towns.
Materials would have to be trucked to the other locations from the closest
rail point.
25.       Building Designs and Standards.  The buildings for the most part
would be constructed to standards and designs in common use by all the agen-
cies of the Sudanese Government. The residences would be the regular houses
furnished to Senior Grade, Middle Grade and Junior Grade Civil Servants. The
ancillary houses would be of thatch, commonly used by the people of the area.
Other designs for workshops, stores and the like would be in accordance with
Government standards. If standards for some would not be available, engineers
of the Civil Works Division would produce them.
26.       Construction Method.  The building construction should be performed
by building contractors or the Corporation for Public Buildings and Works.
In any event, the work should be put out for local tender and awarded to the
lowest competent bidder. There are several private contractors in the Sudan,
both local and foreign, who would be interested in bidding for the contract,
and the value of the work is such that it should attract capable building
concerns. Building construction should be scheduled so that completion of the
houses is the first priority. Construction of the other buildings could
proceed at the same time as that of the houses, but it should not interfere
with the timely completion of the residences, since they are the key to
obtaining competent professional personnel on a timely basis. Building
construction contracts should stipulate that the houses have first priority.
Chart number 16942 shows the relation between the staff house construction
and the presence of profession personnel in post.   However, due to the tight
schedule and the remoteness of some of the buildings, it may be necessary for
the Civil Works Division to perform some of the work by its own forces. The
practice of building by force account should be used as a last resort,
however, and should not become a regular activity of this organization.



ANNEX 8
Page 8
Contruction Plant and Equipment
27.       The Project.  Equipment required for this phase would be
used primarily for hafir construction. The production unit of equipment is
presently a carry-all scraper pulled by a track type tractor. This type unit
would be used for the hafir construction envisaged in the Project along with
adequate support equipment. Compaction equipment would not be needed since the
water level in most of the hafirs would not be above the excavated area.  If
this would not be the case, compaction of the embankment section could be
achieved by the earth moving equipment itself moving over the fill after
spreading in layers not exceeding twenty centimeters.  Towed scrapers are the
best for this type earthwork because the haul distances are short, and as the
hafir is deepened the ascending grade coming out of the excavated area is very
steep. This condition would make it difficult to use self-propelled earth
movers.
28.       For the purpose of this type construction the basic equipment group
needed would consist of:
(a) Two medium track type tractors with angle dozer (140 hp).
Operations: pulling scrapers and dozing as needed.
(b) One track type tractor with angle dozer (105 hp).
Operations: cleaning and grubbing, dozing, and ripping.
(c)  Two towed scrapers (9.2 m ).  Operations:  Loading, trans-
portation, spreading, rough grading and compaction of
excavated material.
(d) One ripper to be pulled by the 105 hp tractor to loosen
compacted earth so the scrapers can easily be loaded.
(e) One fuel trailer.
(f) One grease truck for lubrication and servicing of other
equipment.
(g) One water tanker and pump to haul water for the camp,
equipment and compaction, if necessary.
(h) One utility truck for routine hauling of supplies and
materials.
(i) One 4-wheel drive pick-up to transport supervisory
personnel.
29.       A first class workshop would be constructed at Nyala, to service
all Project equipment and vehicles. A mobile workshop, transport truck and
a fuel tanker would be made available to the Construction Section by the
Administration Section to perform repairs in the field and transport equip-
ment and supplies. These units would not be permanently located in the



ANNEX 8
Page 9
Construction Section, as they should be available to service all com-
ponents of the Project.
30.       Second Phase Project.  Since operations of the Civil Works Division
during a Second Phase Project would mainly consist of maintenance of hafirs
and buildings, its equipment needs would be minimal and consist of the following:
(a) One wheel loader (125 hp) to load maintenance materials
when repairs to embankments and piping would be required.
(b) One dozer (105 hp) to keep embankments maintained and re-
pair dikes and diversion canals.
(c) One ripper to loosen compacted earth so it could be
excavated and loaded by the wheel loader and dozer.
(d) Two dump trucks to haul excavated and other materials
for maintenance purposes.
(e) One grease truck to furnish lubricants and service
equipment.
(f) Six utility trucks to haul maintenance materials,
supplies and personnel to the hafir and building
maintenance sites.
(g) Five - Four WD pick-ups to transport supervisory per-
sonnel to the varius maintenance jobs.
As in the first phase, a mobile workshop, transport truck and fuel tanker
would be available for the use of the maintenance section as well as other
components of the Project.
31.       Wateryard Opening.  The Civil Works Division would be responsible
for the opening of wateryards as needed for the resettlement of farmers during
the program. Since there would only be three wateryards opened during the
Project and eight during a Second Phase Project, the work would probably be
contracted by the RWC and supervised by the Civil Works Division. Wateryard
opening consists of the following:
(a) Installation of a pump and engine or two pumps and two
engines if there are two boreholes. 1/
(b) Erection of an elevated water reservoir.
(c) Installation of piping and valves from the pump(s)
to the reservoir and from the reservoir to the
fountains and stock water troughs.
1/   Some wateryards have standby pumps, engines and boreholes.



ANNEX 8
Page 10
(d) Building a pump house.
(e) Erection of a fence around the water yard.
(f) Installation of a watermeter housed in a steel box
which could be locked or situated in a concrete
meter box with lockable trap-door.
(g) Disinfection of the well and piping. Since the wells
are so constructed that surface water can not contam-
inate the underground water, chlorinators should not
be required.
The Wateryard Division
32.       Since the Rural Councils do not have the facilities and as the
Rural Water Corporation neither has the budget nor is paid enough to maintain
the wateryards, a Wateryard Division would be set up under the WSDC with
responsibility for the maintenance of Project wateryards. The Division would
also be responsible for the operation of other wateryards in the Province.
Current maintenance of wateryards is inadequate and it would be necessary in
the start-up period to assist this operation financially until it would become
self supporting. Once the Wateryard Division becomes a viable and efficient
organization it would be transferred with its plant, equipment, vehicles and
staff back to Government. The RWC at its headquarters in Nyala would remain
responsible for those facilities located in the area west of Nyala and which
service only a fraction of the people and animals in the Province as a whole.
33.       It is planned to utilize the existing provincial maintenance center
at Ed Da'ein and four subsidiary wateryard maintenance centers at El Laiet,
Abu Gabra, Buram and Dimsu. The existing plant at Ed Da'ein would be utilized
with the addition of a meter shop specializing in the repair of water meters. 1!
A new warehouse is under construction at Ed Da'ein which will store a major
reserve of spare parts and mobile workshops and service trucks working
from the four wateryard maintenance centers; a systemized method of preventive
maintenance should be achieved. Besides serving as a spare parts depot
for the province, the maintenance center at Ed Da'ein would provide machine
tools and better work shop facilities for the four subsidiary wateryard
maintenance centers.
34.       The Project would include supplemental facilities, vehicles, and
spare parts for the five maintenance centers (para 35). The staffing also is
1/   The establishment of the motor meter repair shop would depend upon the
availability of other meter repair facilities in the country and their
ability to service this area for a reasonable price.

based on the supplemental needs to those already deployed in the region at
existing establishments. The facilities at Ed Da'ein would accommodate the
supplemental equipment, vehicles and installations with improved effectiveness
and efficiency. The wateryard maintenance centers at the four sites would be
fenced, and basic offices and stores/depots would be provided. Government has
begun this construction and the Project provides for its completion.
35.       The Workshop at Ed Da'ein would be equipped with basic machine
tools and equipment including stand-by generators. This would also serve
as the main depot for spare parts for the region. Adequate spare parts
with compatible tools would be kept at the four satellite water yard main-
tenance centers. Major reconditioning and rehabilitation of the boreholes
and related installations would be carried out in the initial year, so
that routine maintenance and servicing only would be required thereafter.
This would be carried out in accordance with a carefully scheduled and
arranged scheme for preventive maintenance to be established.
36.       Wateryard Operation.  The Project proposes that the operation of
wateryards would be the responsibility of the Division within the organiza-
tion of the WSDC. With the strengthening of maintenance, which would make the
wateryard operation more efficient, it is expected that the water charges
would be enough to sustain the enterprise without outside financial assistance.
Funds collected from the sale of water would support the wateryards in the
Province with exception of the depreciation on basic plant such as boreholes,
reservoirs and buildings. There would be sufficient income over and above
regular operation and maintenance expenses to establish a reserve for the
systematic replacement of vehicles, office equipment, shop equipment, pump,
engines and water meters as well as adequate spare parts.
37.       In order to adequately maintain and operate all wateryards in
the Province (including some 30 in the Project area) it would be necessary
to have an annual budget of fSd 864,000 (US$2,160,000). This breaks down as
follows:
(i)  Administration -  ESd 16,000           (US$40,000)
(ii)  Maintenance -    ESd 330,000          (US$825,000)
(iii)  Operation -       ESd 518,000       (US$1,295,000)
ESd 864,000       (US$2,160,000)
The details of the annual budget are shown in Annex 5, Basic Assumptions.

39.       If there are 1,900,000 cattle and 909,000 sheep and goats in the

production of the wateryards (4.1 million m , Annex 5), all water produced

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Monitoring and Evaluation
1.        Under the Project, a Monitoring and Evaluation Unit would be estab-
lished in the Ministry of Agriculture. This Unit would undertake to provide
a continuous flow of information to the Ministry and to the provincial author-
ities with regard to the progress achieved under the proposed Savannah Develop-
ment Project. The Unit would also monitor the progress of the ODM/Abu Dhabi
Project to be implemented in the Southern District of South Darfur Province.
2.        Permanent staff provided for under the Project would include an
experienced Senior Agricultural Economist and a Senior Sociologist. These
staff would be stationed in the Ministry of Agriculture in Khartoum, but
would be expected to spend at least three-quarters of their time in the
Project area. Funds would be included for travel allowances, vehicles and
operating expenditures. Other permanent staff would include enumerators, who
would be responsible for recording of data and for field interviews, as well
as clerks, drivers, etc. Provision would be made for the hire of aircraft
for aerial survey work.
3.        Provision would also be included under the Project for the hiring
of two internationally recruited consultants who would assist the local
staff in setting up the Monitoring and Evaluation Unit. These staff - an
Agricultural Economist and a Sociologist - would each be recruited for a
five-year period; terms of reference for these staff are found in Annex 15,
Appendix 2. It is also evident that much of the monitoring to be under-
taken by the unit would require special skills, and consequently these tasks
would be carried out on contract by internationally recruited consultants,
as would other specific short term investigations, if needed.
4.        The major reponsibilities of the Unit would be to:
(a) Assess changes in the environment as a result of changing
human and livestock numbers and their distribution.
(b) Evaluate the effects of economic, social and technical
constraints on livestock and agricultural programs in-
corporated into the development plans and to assess
and analyze the response of the nomads to the programs.
(c) Test the acceptability of new ideas and techniques for
improving traditional agricultural and livestock systems
and informations received from the WSDC.
(d) Assess the effectiveness of management and the introduc-
tion of new technology.
(e) Measure the progress of construction works.
(f) Train local staff.
(g) Organize special surveys on sample basis.



ANNEX 9
Page 2
5.        Environmental Changes.  The Project would monitor the effect
of changing livestock and human numbers and their redistribution within
the Southern and Eastern Districts of South Darfur. The parameters for
monitoring would be climate, vegetation and the growth in numbers and
distribution of people and livestock. Climatic records are available from
the regional network of the national meteorological service. Vegetation
monitoring would be carried out by an evaluation of aerial photographs and
ground surveys. Livestock numbers and human population would be monitored by
an aerial survey carried out 2 times a year.  Surveys carried out at the
extremes of the seasonal nomadic movements - at the height of the wet season
(August-September) and the height of the dry season (April) - would enable
estimates to be made of non-migratory livestock.
6.        Progress under the IDA and ODM/Abu Dhabi Projects.  The monitoring
process would be initiated at the start of the IDA Project in order to pro-
vide an assessment of the present situation as a benchmark from which to
measure change. Settled farmers and livestock owners would be sampled and
standard interview systems used to provide data on cropping patterns, owner-
ship of livestock, crop and animal production, expenditure and incomes from
farms and other activities, as well as information on aspirations, needs
and response of farmers to the projects. To assess change, sampling would
be carried out at intervals. Data on changes in the cropped area would be
obtained from aerial photographs.
7.        Pilot Schemes.  The response of farmers to new ideas and tech-
niques would be measured through monitoring of pilot schemes and from the
unit farms managed by the Crop Experimental Section. This monitoring
should also provide information on management capability as reflected
in its ability to communicate new ideas to Project beneficiaries,
8.        Project Construction.  The Unit would monitor the rate of con-
struction of Project works and buildings. It would also monitor water-
yard maintenance, the costs of and returns to water supply, and the extent
of seasonal use of tracks.
Work Program
9.        Before any disbursements were made by IDA for the Monitoring and
Evaluation Unit, Government acting through the Minister of Agriculture would
prepare a detailed work plan and reporting procedures for the Unit. This plan
would be submitted to IDA for comment, and would be updated each year during
the course of IDA supervision missions. A detailed report on the progress of
the Project would be submitted to IDA at least once a year, with the first
report due at the end of year 3. However, from the start the Unit would in
intervals as requested by Government and the WSDC inform about the the progress
of the Project and that of the ODM/Abu Dhabi financed Project. At the end of
the Project (year 6), the final report would present recommendations how fast
and along what lines a possible Second Phase Project of the Development
Program should be undertaken.
April 11, 1977

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Technical Assistance for Marketing Companies
Introduction
1.        The major cash crops that would be produced under the Savannah
Development Project are groundnuts and gum arabic. The marketing of these
crops is handled by private traders at traditional markets located throughout
the area. However, all gum arabic is eventually pruchased by the Gum Arabic
Company, which holds a monopoly in the export trade of this commodity; simi-
larly all groundnuts for export are purchased by the Sudan Oilseeds Company.
Both companies represent an amalgamation of former private companies which
operated in the export trade; they operate a floor pricing mechanism by which
they can intervene in the market through agents; however, as these agents are
only located at a few places on the railway line, they are not effective in
guaranteeing the minimum price to farmers at the majority of markets. Both
companies are new and relatively inexperienced. They require assistance
in developing their marketing organization and in exploring potential new
markets in the export trade.
Gum Arabic
2.        Gum arabic is mainly produced from Acacia senegal, commonly known
as 'hashab". Sudan produces about 70% of world consumption of natural gum
arabic. Gum arabic belongs to the water-soluble gum group which has three
sub-groups (a) natural gum, (b) modified natural gums, and (c) synthetic
gums. The total production of water-soluble gums is not known, but in the
United States alone some 1,800,000 tons of both natural and synthetic gum
are marketed annually. Of this 135,000 tons are used in the food industry,
which is the traditional market for natural gum arabic. This compares to an
average annual world total natural gum arabic production of some 60,000 tons,
and bears witness to the scope for market expansion. Over the past years,
Sudans' gum exports have fluctuated between 15,000 and 40,000 tons annually.
The present system of production by which small scale cultivators tap groves
of wild gum is carried out at relatively low cost - although a certain price
has to be offered to attract production, particularly when gum gardens are
some distance from the homestead, and collection may require the hiring of
camel transport and carrying drinking water. It is not surprising that in
years of a good groundnut harvest, gum collection is reduced, and when har-
vests are poor, more effort is made to collect gum. The present stability
of the market has suffered from fluctuations in supply; and when a shortage
has led to high prices, demand has fallen, and buyers have turned to sub-
stitutes.



ANNEX 10
Page 2
The Gum Arabic Company
3.        The export of gum arabic is handled by the Gum Arabic Company
Limited (GAC), which is a public concession company established in 1969;
30% of the stock is held by Government, and the balance is in private
hands. The company employs some 40 staff in Khartoum, and 70 in Port Sudan.
Most of the gum is purchased by local traders who clean and grade it, and
deliver to Port Sudan. The GAC, however, does have agents operating on
2% commission who can intervene as buyers at main markets; at present this
is confined to twelve places in the country and is not fully effective.
4.        Gum arabic is exported in four grades:
(a) Natural: as picked from the tree
(b) Cleaned: the bulk is exported in this grade, which
involves removing impurities such as bark, and bits
of other varieties of gum, together with small frag-
ments of "gum siftings" and "gum dust", which are
sold as separate grades
(c) Hand picked selected: small quantities of larger
uniform pieces are exported in this grade.
(d) Spray-dried: a new grade which is in demand by cer-
tain buyers; this grade involves further processing
but would enable the Sudan to add further value
before export.
At present, about 33% of exports go to the USA, where buyers are all members
of the Water Soluble Gum Association; 30-50% goes to European markets and
15-25% to Asian markets, chiefly Japan.
5.        GAC requires technical assistance to improve its internal market-
ing system; this company ultimately hopes to increase local processing and
to open potential new markets. The technical assistance to be provided
under the Project would assist in this effort; specifically the staff to be
recruited for the company would:
(a) investigate existing and potential markets and establish
demand parameters for the various types/qualities avail-
able;
(b) develop contacts with established buyers and detail their
specific supply and quality requirements;
(c) provide a selected Sudanese national with at least six
months of on-the-job experience with training in an
overseas consumer or technical market, including follow-
ing contracts through from ship to factory;



ANNEX 10
Page 3
(d) explore the economic feasibility and market potential
of investing in further processing of gum in the Sudan;
(e) provide assistance in translating findings from market
investigations into a long term production, pricing
and marketing policy having due regard for the impor-
tance of competing annual cash crops, particularly oil-
seeds; and
(f) set up an effective market network, including staff
training, and streamline the present market system
which appears to involve excessive cost per ton sold.
6.        It is envisaged that this technical assistance would be provided
under a consultancy arrangement and would extend for a period of two years.
Two experts would be required; one on a full-time basis to handle the devel-
opment of internal marketing and the staff training, and a second to explore
the export market and investigate the desirability of investing in further
processing within the Sudan. The second expert would not be required full-
time and could also fulfill a similar function for the Sudan Oilseeds Com-
pany; part of his work would be done outside the Sudan. Terms of reference
for these staff would be drawn up by the Government in consultation with
IDA.
The Sudan Oilseeds Company
7.        The Sudan Oilseeds Company (SOC) was set up in 1974 and is 58%
Government owned, the balance being owned by the private sector. The SOC
was put together by the amalgamation of three previous parastatal corpora-
tions - the Octomay Trading Corporation, the Shuhada Trading Corporation
and the African Trading Corporation. It has an authorised, issued and
paid up capital of bSd 6 million. It is run by a General Manager, who is
responsible to a ten-man Board of Directors; five of the directors are
Government appointees and five are recruited from the private sector.
8.        The SOC has a monopoly in the export of both groundnuts and sesame.
Much of local groundnut production is processed for oil by private traders
for local sale. Groundnut export statistics show that at present about 10%
of exports are HPS (hand picked selected) grades for the confectionary trade
and the balance is lower grade nuts ultimately destined for processing abroad.
Almost all the HPS groundnuts are from the western areas of the Sudan. There
is a limited scope to increase sales of the higher value HPS grades; the com-
pany is at present installing new grading machines at Port Sudan which are
expected to increase the proportion of HPS gradually to about 20% of total
exports. The SOC also plans to expand its oil extraction capacity with the
addition of two solvent extraction plants and one crushing plant.



ANNEX 10
Page 4

Export quantities for the previous five years are shown below: 1/

although it already has agents in the Western Sudan, proposes to open new
branches in Nyala and En Nahud shortly. The SOC requires technical assistance
both to improve and expand its marketing arrangements and for export promotion
- with particular emphasis in each case on manpower training.

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPM.ENT PROJECT
Human Health Survey
National Health Care Program
1.        A National Health Care Program was prepared by the Ministry of
Health with the collaboration of consultants from the World Health Organi-
zation. This program represents the Government's attempt to establish
priority areas of development in the health sector and to construct pro-
grams to overcome constraints to improved health care.
2.        Certain features of the new program represent either departures
from previous practices or a new emphasis for the National Health Service.
For example: more attention would be paid to preventive as well as to
curative medicine; coverage would be aimed at rural and nomadic peoples
as well as the urban population; and community participation and self-
help would be fostered, particularly in conjunction with a Primary Health
Care Program. Agricultural and economic development would be key elements
in this comprehensive health strategy. The overall health program would
be comprised of eight specific programs, the most important of which is
the Primary Health Care Program. Each program has a separate budget and
implementation plan and schedule. These eight programs are described
below.
(1) Nation-Wide Malaria Program
3.        This program is designed to reduce malaria transmission, and
hopefully to eventually eradicate the disease completely. DDT will be
used in rural areas, and cases in the southern region will be treated
with chlorquine. The major constraint to implementing this program is
logistic, i.e., gaining access to scattered rural settlements via a poor
or non-existent transportation system.
(2) "Man-Made" Malaria Program
4.        The aim of this program is to reduce the incidence of malaria
by controlling the conditions which create breeding places for the malaria
mosquito. The program will concentrate on educating the residents and
farmers of the large irrigation schemes in effective malaria prevention
techniques, both at home and on the farm, and on establishing basic treat-
ment centers for those who contract malaria. Benefits from this program
are both social and economic. Included in the latter are the savings from
avoiding future treatment costs and from reducing losses in production
stemming from work-days lost by malaria sufferers. The foreign exchange
cost of the program, covering anti-malarial drugs, Malathion, larvacides
and vehicles, represents 36 percent of the overall recurrent budget.



ANNEX 11
Page 2
(3) Primary Health Care Program
5.        This program represents the Government's attempt to honor its
commitment to provide free health services to the entire population. The
crux of the program would be the establishment by 1984 of 1,247 new Primary
Health Care Units (PHCU), each manned by a single Primary Health Care Worker
(PHC1W). The aim is to establish one PHCU for 4,000 people. The PHCU would
have four functions: (i) it would provide basic curative health care;
(ii) it would refer major medical problems to larger health units (clinics
and hospitals); (iii) it would engage in such preventive health care activ-
ities as identification of malnutrition cases, immunizations, notifications
of epidemics, ante-natal advice and case detections; and (iv) it would
carry out health promotion activities such as advising the community on
sanitation, hygiene, safe water, nutrition. The PHCU will thus become
actively engaged in all comunity development activities. The major con-
straint to development of this program would be the training and retraining
of assistant nurses, or PHCW's. These key staff will have to be retrained
in community health and preventive medicine. In theory the PHCW's should
be recruited from the communities which they will serve.
(4) Control of Bilharzia in Irrigated Areas
C.        The aim of this program is to control bilharzia infection in the
Gezira and Managil irrigated areas by combining snail control with mass
treatment of severely affected age groups, particularly children. An addi-
tional component of this program is research on snail ecology, infection
rates and the economics of bilharzia infections and treatments in irrigated
areas.
(5) Safe Water Supplies Program
7.        The aim of this program is to provide potable drinking water for
human consumption. Shallow wells would be the main source of such water.
An increase of 100 Government dug and maintained wells per year is projected,
which would ad  to the 900 Government wells already in existence.  A major
drawback to thi approach, however, is that it would not substantially
affect the majo 'ty of Sudanese who rely on some 30,000 privately owned
wells, 850 hafir and 30 dams for water consumption.
(6) Environmental Health Program
8.        The aims and details of this program have not as yet been formu-
lated.
(7) Food (Dura) Supply Program
9.        Certain areas of Sudan, notably Darfur, Red Sea and Equatoria,
suffer from chronic shortages of the staple food, dura. These shortages
are due to lack of rainfall, lack of storage facilities, inadequate road
and transport facilities etc. The program is designed to increase agri-
cultural production in these areas in order to overcome these shortages.



ANNEX 11
Page 3
by including this as a major program for the Ministry of Health, the
Government has recognized that food shortages are as much a health as an
agricultural problem.
(8) Onchocerciasis Program
10.       The aim of this program is to reduce blindness and other disabling
complications of onchocerciasis through treatment of victims, while at the
same time instituting limited vector control in areas of high infestation.
Training of field staff and transportation, especially in the southern region,
are major constraints to implementation of this program.
Total Estimated Cost
11.       The development costs of these comprehensive health programs from
1976 to 1984 could surpass US$70 million. The major financial requirements
are the establishment of safe water supplies for human and animal consumption,
the provision of storage facilities for dura, and the supply of agricultural
inputs such as improved seeds, fertilizers, tools and machinery, pesticides
and credit. Major constraints which must be overcome include inadequate
transportation, persuading basically nomadic peoples to adopt a more hygienic
way of life, and persuading the settled population to adopt more advanced
agricultural concepts.
Project Health Component
12.       Endemic tropical diseases, particularly Malaria and Bilharzia,
are a major problem in the Project area. The lack of medical facilities,
available staff and a temporary shortage of drugs constitute serious draw-
backs to the efficient control of diseases in the area. Government has
agreed to include specific requirements for the Project area within its
proposed National Health Care Program. However, the nature and the scope
of the exact human health care needs of the people living in the Project
area have not as yet been determined. Therefore, funds (US$100,000) have
been included under the Project for a health survey of the area, and for
an evaluation of health priorities. In the initial period of Project im-
plementation Government would prepare terms of reference for the study.
April 11, 1977



ANNEX 12
Page 1
SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Cost
1.        Total Project cost is estimated at LSd 15.3 million (US$38.2
million), of which about US$18.5 million or about 48% would represent foreign
exchange requirements. Capital costs would amount to US$15.8 million or 41%
of Project costs and operating costs would total US$22.4 million or 59% of
total Project cost. Project costs are detailed in the following tables by
categories and by Project components. Costs have been estimated at prices
prevailing at the time of appraisal plus expected price escalations to March
1977. A physical contingency of 10% has been included. In addition price
contingencies totalling 33% of base cost have been included to allow for
expected increases for both local and foreign costs; these are equivalent
on average to a cumulative annual price contingency of about 10%.  Price
contingencies are based on estimated levels of price increases for Project
items in the Sudan. In estimating expected price increases it was assumed
that in Project year one no development would take place and that the imple-
mentation would start gradually in year two and be finished in year six.
The specific price escalation factors used in calculating these price con-
tingencies are detailed below:
1980
1976    1977    1978    1979         onwards
Buildings and Construction        13%      12%      12%     12%          10%
Vehicles and Equipment             9%       8%       8%      8%           7%
Other Costs                       10%       8%       8%      8%           7%
2.        Import taxes and duties on Project goods are estimated at 4Sd 2.4
million (US$6.0 million) and have been included.
3.        The costs of the proposed Project represent the costs of the
proposed first phase of the development program. Approximate cost estimates
were also prepared for a possible Second Phase Project of the Development
Program (see Annex 14). These data were needed to determine the rate of
return of the program given an estimated full development period of ten to
twelve years.
April 11, 1977

ANNEX 14
Page 1
SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Preliminary Cost of a Possible Second
Phase Project of the Development Program
1.        Preliminary cost estimates have been made for a possible Second
Phase Project within the Development Program (Second Phase Project). These
estimates were made on the assumption that the first phase (the Project) would
prove successful. Such an assumption was necessary to measure the full impact
of the development program over a thirty year period; initially investments
would be concentrated on infrastructure (mostly buildings), adaptive research,
institution building, veterinary services, and water supply maintenance; their
benefits would materialize only gradually over a long period.
2.        Total Second Phase Project cost is estimated at hSd 12.6 million
(US$31.5 million), of which about US$13.0 million or about 40% would represent
foreign exchange requirements, and US$5.5 million taxes and duties. In the
Second Phase Project capital costs would be negligible; costs would mainly
consist of operating expenses, as the bulk of capital investments would have
been made in the first phase. Although it is inevitable that a Second Phase
Project would expand and/or alter the concept of the Project (first phase), no
additional investments were included, as any such changes would make it
difficult to measure the impact of the Second Phase Project merely as a
continuation of the Project (first phase). As in the Project (first phase),
costs have been estimated at prices prevailing in March 1977. A physical
contingency of 10% has been included. In addition, price contingencies of 90%
have been included to allow for expected increases for local and foreign costs
beyond the date of baseline cost estimates; these are equivalent on average to
a compound price contingency of 7% per annum. Price contingencies are based
on estimated levels of price increases for Project items in the Sudan. In
estimating expected price increases it was assumed that in Project year one no
development would take place, and that the implementation would start gradually
in year two and be finished in year six, or over a period of twelve years both
phases combined.
April 11, 1977

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Organization and Management
A. Background
1.        In light of the difficulty involved in initiating, coordinating
and implementing the proposed Project, it was recognized by Government that
presently none of the existing institutions was capable of undertaking such
a task; alternative strategies for Project implementation were therefore
carefully evaluated. Certain basic requirements immediately became clear;
these include:
(a) The need for broad involvement of participants in the
formulation of local policies and in Project planning and
implementation.
(b) The necessity of strengthening local institutions so that
they would be capable of better planning and control. The
Rural Council (presently the lowest level of government
authority) should be permitted to develop into an author-
ity which could coordinate local services, establish
financial guidelines and controls, and recruit the profes-
sional staff necessary for the effective administration
of broadly based development programs. At present, these
councils are not - even if given sufficient staff - capa-
ble of assuming responsibility for the institution of
either settlement or other types of agricultural develop-
ment programs. On the other hand, the councils are the
accepted channels of local representative Government, and
they are natural points for institutional development; it
was felt that any new programs should give priority to the
strengthening of these councils.
(c) The enlargement of Provincial planning, evaluation and
appraisal capabilities. Present planning mechanisms
exhibit a certain bias towards the modern agricultural
sector; what is needed is a more comprehensive evaluation
of both social and economic goals and a policy focus which
would help to reduce the great disparities which now exist
between the more and the less developed regions. The
Provinces are presently confined by their inability to
prepare and submit any plan beyond isolated departmental
initiatives on capital expenditures.
(d) The need to ensure that, given the Sudan's presently
centralized control of major development expenditures,



ANNEX 15
Page   2
the Project should be fully integrated in the national
development policy and should be given adequate support
at the national level.
B. Western Savannah Development Corporation
2.        It was agreed with Government that a Western Savannah Development
Corporation (WSDC) would be established in South Darfur Province to coordinate
and undertake comprehensive development programs in the area. It was felt
that such a centralized agency would initially prove better able to implement
development projects than would one of the specialized agencies already
established (e.g. the Ministry of Agriculture), particularly when in many
cases responsibility for a certain activity might otherwise be divided between
several agencies. Particular care was taken, however, that the WSDC would not
become another permanent fixture in the Government bureaucracy; it was agreed
that once each functional division of the WSDC had become successfully establi-
shed and a certain expertise gained, this division, together with its staff,
would be transferred to the Provincial institution with authority in that
area.
3.        The WSDC, when established, would initially have responsibility
for the implementation of the two projects expected to be undertaken in
South Darfur Province - the ODM/Abu Dhabi project in the Southern District
and the proposed Project in the Eastern District.
4.        The WSDC would be established as a public corporation under the
Public Sector Corporations Act of 1976 (see Appendix 1). It would be headed
by a Board of Directors, with the Provincial Commissioner acting as Chairman.
The Director General of the WSDC would serve as Secretary to the Board; other
members would include: the appropriate Assistant Commissioners (including
the executives of the Rural Councils in the Project area), local leaders,
and a representative from the Ministry of Agriculture. The WSDC Headquarters
would be established at Nyala, although various staff would be located
elsewhere in the Province according to where they would be utilized most
efficiently. A small office with four persons would be established in
Khartoum to accelerate procurement of goods and services under the Project;
the office would contact ministries, banks, port and railway authorities,
etc. as part of comprehensive effort to streamline Project execution.
5.        The WSDC would have a number of operating divisions under the
Director General at Nyala. The Deputy Director General located at Ghazala
Gawazat, would be technically in charge of operations located at Ghazala
Gawazat but would report to and be responsible to the Director General. The
organization of the WSDC is illustrated on the attached Chart (World Bank
16579). All staff employed by the WSDC would be financed under either the
ODM/Abu Dhabi Project or the IDA Project. Local staff would be seconded to
the WSDC by the Ministry of Agriculture or would be recruited directly.



ANNEX 15
Page   3
6.        The Ministry of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources, would
support the WSDC in several ways in addition to the provision of staff noted
above. As with other public corporations for which the Ministry renders
special services (see attached Chart, World Bank 16490), the Minister would
review the annual budget and work programs prepared by the WSDC; if acceptable
to him, the Minister would then submit the WSDC's budget to the central bud-
getary authorities for final approval.
7.        In the field, the WSDC would work closely with the Provincial Council,
as well as with the other local administrations (District Councils, Rural
Councils, Village Councils, etc.). It is expected that WSDC field officers
(especially those involved in the settlement program) would become ex-officio
members of the Rural Councils, and members of the Village Councils; in this
role the officers would be able to advise the councils with regard to those
of the latter's activities which might affect the programs being administered
by the WSDC. Also, new farming and ranching communities which would be set up
during Project implementation would be encouraged by the WSDC to seek recogni-
tion as Village or Nomad Councils by the Provincial Council. In fact, one
of the most important roles of the WSDC would be to work actively to give the
local councils a sense of purpose and identity so that they could become an
effective and permanent part of the local administrative structure.
DA Staffing and Salaries
8.        The WSDC would require a Staff of some 200 professionals and 700
supporting staff. It is possible that initially there might be some diffi-
culty in recruiting these staff, as the Sudanese civil servants are reluctant
to work in the Project area (or in any part of western Sudan) because of the
difficult working conditions and poor services, and also because they fear
being by-passed professionally if stationed away from Khartoum. It is
estimated that only 10% of the professional and 20% of the supporting staff
would be recruited in the Project area from existing institutions.  This
problem would be partially overcome by paying all staff at the wage levels
prevailing in corporations which operate predominately on commercial prin-
ciples. The salary levels to be paid by the WSDC would be stipulated in its
Act of Establishment. In addition, housing would be built for almost all
professional and half of the support staff, and professionals would be granted
special allowances (furniture and travel) paid for under the Project. This
salary scale was suggested and endorsed by Government and would be effective
immediately upon establishment of the WSDC in order to ensure that the Corpora-
tion would be able to attract the necessary highly qualified staff.  The need
to recruit qualified and dedicated staff for the WSDC requires that conditions
of employment should be attractive; on the other hand, rigorous screening,
both for the staff which would be seconded from other authorities to the WSDC
or employed by it, should be observed. To this end strict criteria in selec-
ting staff for the WSDC would have to be followed.
9.        Provision would be made under both the ODM/Abu Dhabi Project and
the proposed IDA Project for the international recruitment of several key
staff for the WSDC. Seventeen such positions have been identified. Positions
required under the proposed IDA Project would include:



ANNEX 15
Page   4
(a) at Ghazala Gawazat: 2 Senior Research Officers for the
Range and Livestock Divisions respectively; 1 Range
Management Instructor for the Training Center; and 1
Senior Agronomist (in charge of extension) and 1 Weed
Specialist for the Crop Division; and
(b) at Nyala: 1 Senior Cartographer for the Land Management
Division; and 1 Agricultural Economist and 1 Sociologist
for the Monitoring and Evaluation Unit.
Terms of reference for these staff are given in Appendix 2. Certain other
internatinally recruited staff would be provided under the ODM/Abu Dhabi
Project; these would include:
(a) at Ghazala Gawazat: 1 Senior Agronomist (in charge of the
Crop Experimental section); 1 NDC Manager; 1 Agricultural
Engineer for the Crop Division (also in charge of experiments
with ox-drawn equipment).
(b) at Nyala: 1 Senior Civil Works Engineer; 1 Construction
Engineer (Supervision of works); 1 Mechanical Engineer
(Supervisor of the workshop for the WSDC); 1 Financial
Controller; 1 Senior Advisor to WSDC's Director General;
1 Head of Land Management Unit.
These staff would be phased out from in-line executive posts either completely
or into advisory posts during the life of the Project. The development of
local management capability would be an important part of the Project, and
internationally recruited staff would be expected to train local staff to
take over their roles.
Conclusion
10.       The WSDC's main functions would be in the areas of land use planning
and implementation, livestock development and animal health control, range
management, crop production, development of farming systems, technological
improvement, provision and regulation of water supplies, marketing etc.,
areas which are interrelated. The development work in these areas of the
existing departments and ministries, partially or in toto, would come under
the WSDC, at least temporarily. However, there would be certain development
functions which would not be the exclusive concern of the Authority. For ex-
ample, the WSDC would not attempt to direct the development plans of ministries
such as Education and Health in the program area. Obviously, advice would be
offered and close coordination sought, but the Authority would not itself
attempt to control all those aspects of integrated development that it has an
interest in.



ANNEX 15
Page   5
11.       The proposed strategy of the Project rests on the assumption
that it is only by gradual amelioration of conditions within the existing
land use system that major environmental deterioration and human suffering
can be avoided in the long term. It is only such an approach which would
make possible the sustained rise in agricultural and livestock production
in the Project area. The Project organization as proposed is similarly
cautious. The existing administrative structure is likely to remain the
basis of Government activity, and thus it must be part of the framework for
development planning. The ecological problems of South Darfur as part of
the Western Savannah obviously defy territorial administrative boundaries,
but as the Province seems to be the basic effective unit of administration,
any attempt at para-provincial planning which would ignore the amdinistrative
provincial context of implementation is likely to fail. Similarly, it is
unrealistic to expect departmentalism simply to fall away assuming good
planning intentions. Certainly the organization as outlined is predominantly
based on local support; this is inevitable given the character of the Project.
It is also in the nature of the Project that its successful implementation
will depend almost entirely on popular support. Similarly, institutional
success will depend upon creating a framework that allows those cultivators
and pastoralists most affected by the Project to begin to exert influence
over institutions which now appear to them as remote and occasionally hostile.
Moreoever, the Project's strategy should aim to make those institutions
responsive to the need for a sustained rural development servicing primarily
the crop producers and pastoralists in the Project area.
March 9, 1977

Appendix 1
Page 1
SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Public Enterprises 1/
Introduction
I.        Before 1950, there were a few small agricultural schemes which
operated as public enterprises. These were mainly concerned with production
and marketing of cotton under the management of semi-autonomous boards such
as the GASH, the TOKAR DALTA, the While Nile Scheme, the Nuba Mountain Scheme,
etc. Gradually Government gained relevant experience in managing and operat-
ing large scale public enterprises, and around 1950, the Gezira Agricultural
Scheme (Scheme) was nationalized 1/, and operated as a tripartite partnership.
The terms of this arrangement were that:
(a)  Government provided the land and water; this brought for-
ward the construction of the Sennar Dam, and all other
related irrigation works.
(b) The two companies 2/ provided management and the technical
know-how.
(c) The tenants provided labor and all operations as required.
The Scheme presently produces crops on an area of somewhat over 0.8 million
ha.
2.        The successful experience in managing and operating the Scheme
induced Government to establish other public enterprises. It felt that pub-
lic enterprises would be an effective instrument in developing the economy
by utilizing more efficiently the country's resources, introducing modern
technology, and by increasing physical output and income of Government, the
corporation and the participants. In the late fifties and early sixties
several new public corporations were established; these included: the Agri-
cultural Bank; the Industrial Bank; the Agricultural Production Corporation;
the Mechanized Farming Corporations; the Agricultural Research Corporation;
Sudan Railways Corporation; Sudan Airways Corporation; the Rural Water
1/   Public Enterprises are treated as public corporations.
2/   The Gezira Agricultural scheme before nationalization was operated
by two British companies: the Sudan Plantation Syndicate and the
Kassala Cotton Company.



ANNEX 15
Appendix 1
Page 2
Corporation, etc. In addition, other corporate bodies similar to public
corporations were established; these included: the Bank of Sudan, the
Khartoum University and the Omdorman Islamic University.
3.        In May 1970 Government nationalized selected industrial and commer-
cial enterprises; which substantially increased the number of public enter-
prises. At that time Government felt that all public enterprises would have
to be classified into two basic groups depending on the work they were doing.
The outcome of that classification was a division of all public enterprises
into:
(a) Service and Public Utility Corporations; and
(b) Corporations which operate predominantly on commercial
principles.
Examples of the Service and Public Utility Corporations are: Sudan Railways;
Electricity and Water Corporation; Rural Waters Corporation; and the Agricul-
tural Research Corporation. These corporations are subdivided into subsectors
according to their activities; thus there is: the Commercial Sector under
the Minister of Finance, Planning and National Economy; the Industrial Sector
under the Minister of Industry, the Agricultural Sector under the Minister
of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources; the Transport Sector under the
Minister of Transport and Communications; the tourism Sector under the
Minister of State for Special Affairs. The Banking Sector is coordinated by
a Savings and Investment Council under the Minister of Finance, Planning and
National Economy. There are other public corporations and/or corporate
bodies which do not specifically below to one or other of the foregoing
classification such as the Rahad Corporation under the Ministry of Irriga-
tion and Hydro-Electrical Power and the Construction and Building Corpora-
tion under the Minister of State for Construction and Public Works.
Legal Status
4.        Each corporation is established by special law which provides for
its independent legal entity, sets up its organizational structure and
management scheme within which it functions, and stipulates its objectives,
rights, duties and responsibilities. The law under which a corporation is
established also shows the relationship of the corporation with other author-
ities and agencies of Government. A significant feature of such relationship
provided for in all laws is that the respective Minister, in some cases with
the consent of the Council of Ministers after having consulted the Board of
the corporation, may from time to time give the Board directions of general
character in accordance with the general policy of the Governmenbt which the
Board is supposed to carry out.   The law which applies to establishing and
operating the Public Sector Corporation is "The Public Corporation Act 1976".



ANNEX 15
Appendix 1
Page 3
Ccnditions and Terms of Service
5.        The basic conditions of service which are applied to the employees
of public sector corporations are similar to those applied to the Civil
Service.  But the salary and wage structures are more favorable, particularly
for corporations which operate predominantly on commercial principles. In
addition to salaries, and which can differ slightly between various corpora-
tions, further incentives, such as bonuses, housing, furniture, paid utili-
ties, paid trips from the field to the capital, etc., can be approved for the
employees. The basis for its implementation in each of the corpo-
rations is the classification of positions, job description, and periodical
evaluation of the operations of the corporations.
Establishment of the Proposed Western Savannah Development Corporation
6.        The Western Savannah Development Corporation (WSDC) which is proposed
under the Savannah Development Project (Project) would be similar in concept
to that of the public corporations (enterprises). It would have to follow all
the steps required which apply to the establishment of public corporations.
Considering the complexity of the work to be done under the proposed Project
and the difficulties the staff will encounter in implementing the Project, the
more favorable salary structure which is presently used for commercial type
corporations should be applied for the WSDC. In the case of the proposed
Project it would be difficult to specify the character of the WSDC as it would
be a mix, with various degrees of emphasis, of the work which is presently
done by Service and Public Utility corporations and by Corporations which
operate predominately on commercial principles. However, some important
features (i.e., education, training, and the process in gaining confidence of
people to participate in specific and innovative development actions) would
add to the work of the staff of the WSDC a complex, delicate and difficult
task. To achieve this objective, a special inducement would be needed and
justified to motivate the staff of the WSDC, working in unusually strenuous
conditions, to achieve the set goals.
Steps in Establishing Public Corporations 1/
7.        The procedures for establishing public corporations are:
(a) Appointment of the manager (designee) by the Ministry
where the corporation functionally belongs. In the case
of the WSDC for the Savannah Development Project the
manager (designee) would have to be appointed by the
Minister of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources.
(b) Preparation of materials required by Law (para 4) such as:
1/   This procedure would apply also for the WSDC.



ANNEX 15
Appendix 1
Page 4
(i) description of the objectives of the corporation;
(ii) proposal of type of corporation (service/public
utility or commercial); and
(iii) presentation of proposed salary and wage structure.
(c) Submission to the relevant Ministry; 1/
(d) Recommendation of the Minister of Agriculture, Food and
Natural Resources to the Minister of Public Service and
Administrative Reform to accept or modify the corpora-
tion's (WSDC's) proposed salaries and wage structure. At
the same time the detailed job description for classified
staff (officials) for the proposed corporation (WSDC) has
to be prepared. When agreement has been reached between
the Ministers of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources,
and Public Service and Administrative Reform about the
type of corporation and its wages and salaries structure,
the whole package has to be submitted for final approval
to the Central Bureau for the Administration of the Corpo-
rations, which is a department set up in the Ministry of
Public Service and Administrative Reform. Its objective
is to coordinate the activities of the different corpora-
tions from the administrative point of view, and its
functions also include the coordination of the terms of
service for the employees of various corporations by
preparing general principles and standards for the clas-
sification and evaluation of positions and jobs, coordina-
tion of training programs and inspection of the applica-
tion of the standards and regulations regarding personnel
in the corporations.
April 11, 1977
1/   The WSDC would have to submit its proposal to the Minister of
Ministry of Agriculture, Food and Natural Resources.

Draft Terms of Reference for Key Project Staff
1.        The Western Savannah Development Corporation which would manage and
implement both the IDA project in the Eastern District and the ODM/Abu Dhabi
project in the Southern District would include 17 key positions for which
international recruitement would be required should qualified Sudanese
nationals not be available. Out of these 17 positions, eight would be
required and financed under the proposed Project. These are:
Total

1       Head of Range Management Division             5
