2.        A brief description of the qualification and duties of the eight
professionals required under the Project is given below:
A.   Head of Livestock Division
(i) This post would be filled with a veterinarian with con-
siderable field experience in working with pastoralists



ANNEX 15
Appendix 2
Page 2
and holding post graduate training in tropical livestock
health and production. It is essential that the candidate
has a proven capacity for gaining the confidence of the
pastoralists and ability as a team leader.
(ii)  Apart from supervising the research program and the
veterinary services, his major responsibility would be as
leader of the Data Gathering Team. He would be responsible
for maintaining a constant dialogue with the pastoralists
on all relevant aspects of Project activities. He would
also teach at the Young Stockmen's Course.
(iii) He would also be responsible for guiding management and
training Sudanese key personnel and preparing for replacement.
(iv)  The above listed functions may be adjusted and/or extended
as the need arises.
B.   Head of Range Management Division
(i)  This post would be filled with a candidate holding post
graduate training in Range Management; particular emphasis
would be on his practical field experience with traditional
livestock producers in the area and his ability to gain
their confidence.
(ii)  He would be a member of the Data Gathering Team and his
main responsibility would be the establishment of the
Range Management Master Plan, and through continuous
contact with the pastoralists to enhance their acceptance
of innovations. He would supervise research and experi-
mentation and teach at the Young Stockmen's Course (Annex
7).
(iii) He would also be responsible for guiding management and
training Sudanese key personnel and preparing for replacement.
(iv)  The above listed functions may be adjusted and/or extended
as the need arises.
C.   Range Management Instructor
(i)  This post would be filled with a Range Specialist with a
minimum of 10 years experience including training of field
staff. He would be a generalist in his field, and emphasis
should be put on practical field experience.



ANNEX 15
Appendix 2
Page 3
(ii)  He would be responsible for the establishment of the 12
months course with the cooperation of officers from the
Range Division and utilizing short-term consultants.
(iii) During the Project (first phase of Development Program)
he would select and train national instructures with the
aim that the course would continue during a Second Phase
Project without his services.
(iv)  He would also be responsible for guiding management and
training Sudanese key personnel and preparing for
replacement.
(v)  The above listed functions may be adjusted and/or extended
as the need arises.
D.   Senior Cartographer
(i)  This post be filled with a cartographer holding a College
or University degree with specialization in photogrammetry
or cartography, and with a minimum of three years' work
experience including mapping, and applications of photo-
grammetry to mapping.
(ii)  The candidate would be responsible for and maintain
quality control of aerial photo surveys and photo mosaic
construction, would direct the compilation and production
of maps of Baggara Land System and northern rainy season
Qoz area, and would direct land demarcation mapping and
other mapping as required.
(iii) He would also be responsible for guiding management and
training Sudanese key personnel and preparing for
replacement.
(iv)  The above listed functions may be adjusted and/or extended
as the need arises.
E.   Senior Agronomist
(i)  This post would be filled with an agronomist with a mini-
mum of a university degree in agriculture or equivalent
and five years experience in dryland tropical agronomy.
(ii)  The candidate would be in charge of the Crop Experimental
Section at Ghazala Gawazat and would be responsible for:
(a)  preparing a crop research program for Eastern
and Southern districts of South Dafur in con-

search team and the Deputy Director General at
Ghazala Gawazat;

(b) supervision of all crop research work in Eastern
and Southern districts;
(c) a program of research aimed at developing more
productive farming systems; and
(d) training local staff.
(iii)  He would also be responsible for guiding management and
training Sudanese key personnel and preparing for
replacement.
(iv)  The above listed functions may be adjusted and/or extended
as the need arises.
F.   Weed Specialist
(i)  This post would be filled with a weed specialist with a
minimum of a university degree in agricultural botany or
equivalent and five years experience in weed control
research.
(ii)  The candidate would evaluate the importance of crop needs,
and formulate and carry out a program of weed research
designed to provide better weed control methods for small-
scale farmers. Emphasis would be placed on control of
buda (Striga hermonthica). He would also investigate
the possibility of using low cost herbicides with simple
applicators.
(iii)  He would also be responsible for guiding management and
training Sudanese key personnel and preparing for
replacement.
(iv)  The above listed functions may be adjusted and/or extended as
the need arises.
G.   Monitoring and Evaluation Specialists
Two specialists (one agricultural economist and one sociologist)
would be located at the Ministry of Agriculture to perform functions related
to the two projects. They would advise on and assist in setting up a Moni-
toring and Evaluation Unit (Unit), as well as in the training of the Unit's
staff and the conduct of its day-to-day activities. Specifically, their
duties would include advising the Unit in performing the following functions:
(i) developing procedures for monitoring and evaluation;



ANNEX 15
Appendix 2
Page 5
(ii) using data and other information supplied by the Western
Savannah Development Corporation, conducting field analyses,
assessing the performance of the projects and determining
their rate of acceptance;
(iii) elaborating whatever modifications that might be indicated
by the performance of the settlements and livestock
developing for achieving their objectives and improving
their operational effectiveness;
(iv) training staff of the Unit in monitoring and evaluaCion
procedures; and
(v) assisting Government institutions and other consultants in
preparing a Second Phase Project.
(a) Agricultural Economist
(i) This post would be filled with a person holding a
Masters degree in Agricultural Economics or the equiv-
alent and at least 5 years experience in the implemen-
tation of settlement and livestock projects in Africa.
The Agricultural Economist would have a combined advis-

Agriculture.
(ii) The Agricultural Economist would in particular.
(a) plan and supenrise the unit's activities;
(b) develop procedures for monitoring and evalua-

(c) train staff of the Unit in monitoring and eval-
uation procedures;
(d) cooperate with other consultants, engaged by the
Unit for special purposes and evaluate their in-
formations;
(e) participate in and prepare summaries of the eco-
nomic evaluation of the Project and the ODM/Abu
Dhabi financed project including the timing of
implementation;
(f) compare cost/benefit analysis with the projec-
tions;

(g) evaluate the financial aspects of the two proj-
ects separately;
(h) test and measure the budgets of the participants
against targets and expectations made during
appraisal;

(b)  Sociologist
(i) This post would be filled with a person holding minimum a
Masters degree in Social Anthropology with special emphasis
on nomadic societies with at least 5 years experience in
the implementation of settlement and livestock projects in
Africa.
(ii) The Sociologist would in particular:
(a) help in designing the procedures and the strategy
by which Project management will explain the
Project's concept and objective to the area
population, and by which it will implement the
Project so as to gain local cooperation and
minimize cultural resistance;
(b) identify and include the traditional tribal
organization, authority structures and power

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Crop and Livestock Marketing
I. Crop Marketing in the Project Area
General
1.        All produce sold in the Project area is handled exclusively by
the private sector, and considering the constraints and difficulties involved,
the system operates efficiently. Government intervention is limited to con-
trol of the larger local markets to assist them with the collection of taxes
and to the setting of floor prices (minimum buying prices) 1/ for groundnuts,
sesame and gum arabic. Government has established marketing companies
(Sudan Oil Seeds Company, Gum Arabic Company, etc.) to help maintain these
prices through the direct purchase of the crops at the line of rail. Except
for the larger towns of Nyala and Ed Da'ein, all other villages of reasonable
size have a market day once a week where produce is brought in by donkey,
camel, horse and cart, etc. In the harvest season when larger amounts of
produce are being assembled, market sales days are extended to two or three
days per week.
2.        It is primarily through an extensive network of informal financial
and personal relationships that crop (and livestock) marketing in the Sudan
is organized. Services vital to the operation of the system - including
credit, market information, insurance and transport - which in all developed
and in many developing countries are provided by Government, are provided here
by this privately organized informal infrastructure. The system (which
operates reasonably well) is one with a long tradition and is operated by the
group known as the jellaba, who have traded in the west for centuries. In the
towns and villages of the west they form a close-knit group who survive
principally by trade in crops, livestock and imported manufactured goods. As
a closed group with family and friendship links extending throughout the
country, it is inevitable that personal and financial relationships go together;
wherever possible, business is conducted with a friend or relation. These
1/   Each season the Ministry of Commerce and Supply in conjunction with the
Ministry of Finance establishes minimum buying prices for groundnuts,
sesame and gum at main buying centers. The prices are set by a committee
which examines world market prices and trends and takes into account
development policy, company overheads and export and development taxes.
Anticipated export prices are thus converted into an average ungraded
buying price at Port Sudan; this is translated into minimum buying prices
at main markets by deducting transport and handling charges and an
allowance for merchant profit.



ANNEX 16
Page   2
relationships control access to informal credit, initial working capital
and the opportunity to progress in the trade. It has ensured that the most
lucrative sectors of trade (which does not cover small retailing) have
remained in the hands of the jellaba for generations. Consequently, the
creation and maintenance of a network of obligations among people stra-
tegically placed in the economy is essential for any merchant.
3.        The marketing system which has emerged in response to these in-
frastructural and supply pattern constraints is one which is efficient,
flexible, but labor intensive. The large amount of labor used in market-
ing for example, is a direct response to the low density of supply. This
requires a large number of primary collectors of produce (middlemen or
agents) to bulk supplies for the main wholesalers. This system is effective
in so far as the collectors operate parallel (and competing) rather than
chain supply functions. The amount of labor involved is also a response to
the limited sources of income, the low opportunity cost of labor in the dry
season, and the ease of entry into trading once a small amount of working
capital has been acquired. Much of the flexibility of the system arises
directly out of the extensive use of labor and substantially fixed marketing
costs; the number and location of small buyers and agents change each year in
response to the size of the harvest and the main areas of production. The
major marketing costs involved, transport and the commissions paid to agents,
however are variable costs.
Major Crops (Table 1)
4.        Millet.  Millet is the most important staple crop grown in the
Project area. The crop is grown on some two-thirds of the total cultivated
area. 1/ However, during unfavorable crop seasons grain must be imported
into the area; this has been necesssary in three of the last seven years.
Most villages have flour mills for the processing of millet. In Nyala,
eleven mills treat about 8 tons of seed per day. Some 35 cooperative mill-
ing societies are now operating in the area with varying success.
5.        Groundnuts.  Groundnuts are the principal cash crop in the Project
area (Table 2); the crop is grown on somewhat less than one-third of the total
cultivated area, with about 90% of the harvest being traded on the wholesale
markets. Some 60-80% of total production is exported as kernels to Port
Sudan; the rest is crushed for oil which is used for local consumption or
exported unofficially to countries in the West. The groundnut cake resulting
from this processing is mostly sent for export through Port Sudan. It is
estimated that about 25% of the country's total production of groundnuts come
from South Darfur Province.
1/   Total cultivated area is estimated to range between 200,000 and 350,000 ha.
Source: Hunting Technical Services and the Ministry of Agriculture.



ANNEX 16
Page   3
6.        Despite an approximately 40% rise in the average farmgate price of
groundnuts between 1970/71 and 1974/75, there seems to be a slow decline in
the quantity of nuts exported from the area (Table 2). This would suggest
that farmers have concentrated on their staple crops of millet and sorghum as
a result of three very dry years since 1968/69, regardless of the increase in
the area under cultivation.
7.        Locally produced groundnuts are decorticated at 6 small processing
plants in Nyala, 8 in Ed Da'ein, and one each in Abu el Humer and Adila.
After decortication, groundnuts are sorted and the low and broken grade nuts
are crushed in the local oil mills. These mills, with a capacity of 2-3 tons
of groundnuts per day, have steadily increased their operations since the
Government took control of exports in 1969. The oil seed cake produced at
these mills is not subjected to export controls; over 90% of it is sent by
train to Port Sudan for further crushing and export to Europe. Recently the
Sudan Oil Seeds Company (SOSC) has invested in specialized grading equipment
which it installed at Port Sudan in an attempt to improve the quantity and
quality of handpicked selected nuts (HPS) to be exported (Annex 10). This
grade of groundnuts is presently around 10% of total exports. It is expected
that this new grading equipment might raise it gradually to about 20%. The
incremental groundnut production of about 50,000 tons (year 30) or about 5%
of the present production in the country would find a ready market.
8.        Gum Arabic is the second principal cash crop in the area.  The gum
harvest fluctuates generally in an inverse relationship with groundnut pro-
duction. In years when groundnut yields are high, gum arabic sales are lower
than normal; when groundnuts yields are low (usually in the dry years) there
is a corresponding increase in total gum production. This relationship can
be attributed to the fact that collecting gum often requires walking long
distances in areas where water is scarce. Once a farmer sees that his cash
requirements for the following year are covered by the groundnut harvest, he
has little incentive to go and seek additional income from the gum arabic
harvest. Between 1970 and 1974 gum arabic export prices rose by 300%, but
quantities exported decreased by about 60% due to organizational weaknesses
in the Gum Arabic Company and to oscillations in demand. Presently the Port
Sudan f.o.b. price is US$1,300 or about 50% lower than in 1974/75, which is
reflected in the farmgate price.
9.        Gum Arabic is normally cleaned and graded after being purchased
by the local traders. It is then sent by train to Port Sudan where further
impurities may be graded out by the Gum Arabic Company (GAC) before shipment.
Over the last two seasons a proportion of the exported gum has been subjected
to a spray drying process at the milk factory at Babanusa and the GAC is hope-
ful that the process can be expanded to treat a more significant proportion
of the harvest. Sudan supplies about 70% of the world market for natural
gum arabic, most of which goes unprocessed to Europe and North America. The
country's exports have declined from a peak of 50,000 tons in 1968 to some
20,000 tons in 1974 (Table 2). A lower quality product known as talh is
taken from the tree Acacia seyal; production averages about 3,000 tons per
annum, and this is exported exclusively to India. Total gum production of



ANNEX 16
Page   4
South Darfur is approximately 20-25% of the country's output.  The incre-
.cnntal gum arabic production of about 3,400 tons per annum (year 22) would
represent about 10% of the 1971-75 five-year average annually exported quantity
from the Sudan, but only 0.2% of the total world trade of gums both natural
and synthetic (Annex 10, para. 2).  It is difficult to assess future demand
for gum arabic on the major world markets. Periodic shortages along with
hi'gher prices have caused major users of gum arabic to look for substitute
materials rather than alternative sources of supply. The Sudan, however,
occupies a privileged position, as its gum is of the best quality available
and demand outstrips supply. Due to the relatively small incremental production
irnvolved, it is almost certain that the Project related incremental production
would always find a ready market.
Marketing Constraints
10.       The present floor pricing mechanism of the SOSC has only been in
operation for two years, during which time it has been subjected to a great
amount of pressure from the merchants (para 1), Presently, the SOSC has
representatives buying only from along the railway line, where at times they
have difficulty in supporting the guaranteed price because of lack of funds.
However, it is away from the rail that is the major concern. There the
farmers come into contact only with middlemen, and often they are forced to
take a lower price than the one to which they are theoretically entitled. The
same problems arise with gum arabic prices; the Gum Arabic Company (GAC) ap-
points agents on a commission basis and cannot compel them to attend markets.
Merchants and middlemen dominate all markets; in most cases they have the
confidence of the producers and the family and tribal relationship which are
of importance (Annex 4).
11.       Exportable farm commodities from the Project area have to be sent
to Khartoum (a distance of 1,300 km) or to Port Sudan (2,100 km). The mar-
keting costs in shipping produce over these great distances are high. While
it is estimated that a farmer living near Nyala receives only about iSd 35
(US$88) per ton of groundnuts, the total expense involved in shipping the
nuts to Port Sudan would be about LSd 100 (US$250) per ton (Annex 20). This
is due in part to the fact that local transport is difficult to hire and is
extremely expensive - especially for the small producer with only a few sacks
to market (Table 3). 1/  Most of the available trucks are owned by merchants
who concentrate on moving their own purchases. Rail transportation is very
unreliable; until recently, produce stored at depots would not have been
shipped by the time of the next harvest, causing merchants to be reluctant to
pay reasonable prices when they have not yet been able to sell the previous
season's crop. Storage facilities of any size are only available in Nyala.
Often merchants move stocks from Ed Da'ein to Nyala for storage and easier
access to rail vans. Communication within the area is poor, as only Nyala
is connected to a few villages along the railway line by phone, and all com-
munication to Khartoum are very poor.
1/   To transport 1 ton of groundnuts 60 km - the average distance from the
farm to Nyala or Ed Da'ein - costs about IYSd 6 (or US$15).



ANNEX 16
Page   5
Future Marketing Development
12.       The primary aim of crop market development for South Darfur should
be to improve the existing system and to examine the possibilities of giving
the farmers a larger share of the final price received (by traders and/or
exporters) for his surplus crops. For those commodities for which a minimum
floor price is set, either costs would have to be reduced within the floor
price mechanism or the price of the marketed product increased. Subsidies
would only be a short-term solution, and would eventually become a large
national burden. Costs could be reduced by improving and expanding the
infrastructural facilities needed to market the various crops; in particular,
better storage and handling facilities should be considered.
13.       Two specific steps should be taken to help ensure the more efficient
functioning of the crop marketing system.
(a) necessary changes should be made to improve the function-
ing of the floor price mechanism and to apply it gradually
to all farmers, and not only to those farmers living along
the railway line; and
(b) the possibilities of providing short-term credit 1/ to
farmers and combining it with the provision of inputs
to increase production should be examined.
A guaranteed export price and consequently the floor price (farm gate) should
be set before the season starts, and the Government should intervene by pro-
viding sufficient short-term funds through the existing commercial banks so
that the SOSC and the GAC could, in an emergency situation, provide adequate
transport for moving of groundnuts from the area.
Project Marketing Activities
14.       An important function of the Project would be to help improve crop
marketing in the Project area. However, solutions should be found within the
existing marketing framework in order to take full advantage of the valuable
services rendered by the existing merchant network. Any attempt to replace it
by some centralized system would be unrealistic and probably counter-productive.
A Marketing Division (MD) would be established within the Western Savannah
Development Corporation (WSDC). 2/ The Head of the Division would be a Senior
Accountant whose main responsibility would be to devise and supervise work
programs for the Division. To assist him, an expatriate consultant with
experience in the marketing of primary products in developing countries would
be employed for 3 months ech year. Specific responsibility for crop marketing
1/   Relief-type short term credit to replace "sheil" without elements to
increase production would be too risky and should not be considered.
2/   The WSDC would also be responsible for the development of livestock
marketing.



ANNEX 16
Page   6
in the MD would be given to the Crop Marketing Supervisors stationed in field
units at Nyala and Ed Da'ein; these supervisors would also be responsible for
the Pilot Bonded Storage Area Scheme (BSA). They would check the grading and
weighing of produce and would assist staff of the Land Management Division
with an education program in the new settlements. The MD staff would have to
rely to a large extent on four-wheel pick-up type vehicles to move around
continually in the field.
Project Pilot Program
15.       In order to help alleviate one serious marketing constraint in the
Project area, WSDC would act as a special agent for the SOSC in setting up 7
BSA's in several of the proposed new settlements; the settlements in which the
BSA's would be constructed would be selected by the WSDC in consultation with
the provincial authorities and the SOSC. The farmers would be encouraged to
bring their sacks of groundnuts and gum arabic to the BSA where they would be
inspected, weighted and payment made by a member of the staff of the WSDC's
Marketing Division. Each BSA would contain space for approximately 500-1,000
tons of produce. These BSA's would consist of no more than a weigh balance, a
locally made fence, and possibly a concrete base or plinth for storage. The
Marketing Division (MD) would operate a small revolving fund provided by the
ABS or other commercial banks to purchase crops coming to the BSA and would
pay a proportion (perhaps 20 percent) of the local minimum price upon delivery.
The crops in the BSA would be sold by auction to merchants or agents as at
other markets, and the difference between the realized price (less a nominal
charge for storage) and the advance would be paid to the producer. This
system would serve not only to test whether the bargaining strength of the
merchants early in the season could be reduced but would also enable a
better utilization of available transport for moving crops throughout the dry
season. Since the crops would be bulked at the BSA's, the process of purchase
and transport to the railway could be spread more evenly over a longer period
than at present. This system would have to be tested on a small pilot scale
to discover whether the informal relationships between producers and small
merchants, which might include services such as provision of credit, are so
strong that a marketing system which does not involve pre-harvest credit from
local commercial banks could not operate. Small huts would be built at each BSA
for overnight accommodation. Each field unit would be equipped with a set
of mobile weighing scales so that they could be in continual use. Temporary
labor wold be used wherever possible in the field, as it is not expected that
the BSA's would be open for much more than six months of the year. The re-
sults of this pilot operation would be cosely monitored and evaluated; if
successful, the concept could be expanded to other settlement areas.
II. Livestock Marketing within the Project Area
General
16.       Livestock production is entirely in the hands of private merchants.
The most notable feature of this trade is the difficulty involved in making



ANNEX 16
Page   7
contact with the scattered producers who are constantly on the move, and the
collection of adequate numbers of cattle, sheep and goats from a large area.
Livestock traded at the small towns and villages are bought for local consump-
tion and for assembly for trekking to the main markets of Omdourman, Nyala,
El Obeid and Medani which serve the urban centers. The large markets have
an elaborate system of trading, ranging from tribal or village merchants to
the cattle wholesalers. There are four distinct levels of cattle dealers:
(a) the Debabi or selling agent who works in one market on a small scale,
receiving credit from a larger merchant; (b) the Gelaji, the independent,
small-scale trader who buys in small lots, collects a herd and trades it in
the urban centers; (c) the tribal merchant who as agent for the tribe sells
a portion of the herd to local dealers; and (d) the Tagir Mwashi who is a
large-scale merchant and who controls the export trade. Supplementing the
marketing system are trading agents or Samsors who may represent either
sellers or buyers in negotiating the price for cattle. Much of the capital
which finances this operation is concentrated in Omdourman from where a small
number of large merchants handle about 50% of the total trade. Consequently,
many of the buyers of livestock in the producing area are agents of these
Omdourman merchants or middlemen who will ultimately sell them. The mer-
chants issue precise instructions to their agents and are in continual com-
munication with them. Less than 50% of transactions are made in official
markets.
17.       Meat marketing in the whole country is in the hands of the small
butchers who obtain their supplies directly from a trader who slaughters one
or two animals, or in the large markets from specialist wholesalers. Meat
prices are officially controlled and have remained constant for the last two
years. Market prices, however, are higher than the controlled prices. Per
capita consumption of meat (mostly beef) in the Sudan is estimated to be
about 19 kg or 290,000 m tons 1/; comparative figures in other African coun-
tries are: Ethiopia 16 kg, Kenya 14 kg, Malagasy Republic 19 kg, Somalia
20 kg, Tanzania 13 kg (Source FAO - Production Yearbook 1973). Consumption
is expected to rise by about 2.5 to 3% annually, indicating an expected
overall demand of about 410,000 metric tons by 1990.
18.       The main herds of cattle reach the buying centers in the west be-
tween July and October and during the subsequent two months move along the
stockroutes. Moving at this time they generally gain in condition. The
stockroutes cover a wide belt as herds range for grazing en route. Depend-
ing on the starting points in the area, herds take 40-60 days to reach the
Omdourman/Khartoum/Gezira area. The majority of animals arrive there
between August and February when prices consequently are low. Some are held
and finished on concentrates in the vicinity of Khartoum prior to sale or in
quarantine for export. Between February and August, prices are higher and
animals, especially sheep, are moved by rail and held for fattening. The
increasing urban population is a key determinant in the steadily increasing
demand for meat, particularly mutton, and the seasonal variations in supply
are reflected in sharp price changes. Total marketed offtake in the area is
presently estimated at 60,000 cattle, 80,000 sheep and 30,000 goats.
1/   Source:  HTS.



ANNEX 16
Page   8
19.       Although the railroad extends to Nyala, nearly all cattle travel
from South Darfur on the hoof to the Omdourman/Khartoum/Gezira area, or a
distance of some 1,000 km or more. The producers have little confidence in
the railway system because of the difficulties which have arisen in the past,
including long periods spent by the stock enroute.
20.       The use of air transport for the export trade in meat has developed
greatly during the past years. Most of the transport of chilled meat has
been by charter aircraft. There is undoubtedly a need for a close examination
to be made of the feasibility and economies of transporting meat by air both
internally in the Sudan and from the Sudan to export markets.
Marketing in the Southern and Eastern District of South Darfur 1/
21.       There are 4 registered livestock markets in each of the 2 Districts
(Southern and Eastern), together with many smaller unregistered markets (21
in the Eastern District) which operate only part time. The major markets are
at Nyala, El Obeid and Ed Da'ein, while in recent years Abu Matariq, Furdos
and Gimelaya have become important selling centers, especially during the wet
season. Only Nyala and El Obeid have auction markets; these are for cattle
and sheep and are not fully utilized. Agents from Omdourman, Khartoum and
other parts of the east have to operate through local middlemen, who are in
close contact with the producers and who buy for them on a commission basis.
Another intermediary guarantees the ownership of the cattle in the auction
markets and is paid a commission by the producer of 2 percent on the sale
value of the animals. No scales are available at the markets, and private
sales are based on size, age and condition. No grading system is in use and
the producer commonly receives less than a fair share of the consumers' price.
22.       Over the years the number of cattle being sold through the Nyala
market has declined; except in 1973-74 when cattle sales temporarily increased
due to the dry summer in 1973. A regression analysis carried out by HTS
based on the last nine years' figures showed that this decline has been at a
rate of 2.2 percent per annum. Considering the increasing number of cattle
in the area, this decline indicates that many producers prefer to circumvent
established markets when selling cattle and deal privately with middlemen and
independent traders outside the markets. The cattle figures show that mature
males make up about 75 percent of total sales annually, and the balance is
made up of old cows and young animals in about equal proportions.  (Table 4)
Sheep and goat numbers over the years have remained stable expect in 1975/76
a significant increase over the previous years occured. This could have
resulted from the export ban that was placed on cattle, sheep, beef, and
mutton in December 1974 with the aim of stabilizing domestic meat prices; the
ban was not successful in achieving its primary purpose and was lifted in
October 1976. The result was a heavy demand for sheep throughout the country
as cattle were mostly held off the market in expectation that the ban would
1/   The areas covered by the Project and the ODM/Abu Dhabi project.



ANNEX 16
Page   9
be lifted. The corresponding figures for the Ed Da'ein market show that
cattle numbers have remained stable, and unlike the Nyala Market in 1973-74,
no large increase in the throughput of cattle resulted from the dry summer of
1973. This confirms the assumption that the Rizeiqat pastoralists are truly
nomadic and move over large distances, whereas many of the cattle that come to
the Nyala Market belong to people growing crops or are trade herds kept by
merchants in close proximity to supply the needs of the town. The rather
large fall-off in numbers of sheep being sold at Ed Da'ein from 1972/73
onwards is probably due to the fact that with the high prices being paid in
those years more animals were purchased directly from the producers who were
content with the prices offered.
23.       The seasonality of supply throughout the Province is very well
defined. Available data (HTS) show that the major sales in the area take
place in October. At the Nyala Market there is a definite peak in September
of each year; however, because of the necessity to supply the larger local
population and as the trade herds are held near to the agricultural areas,
sales are more evely spread throughout the year than is the case at Ed Da'ein.
24.       Cattle and sheep prices at Nyala and Ed Da'ein are shown in Table
5. These figures show a sharp rise in prices over the last five years, and
particularly in 1974/75. While cattle sell at better prices at Nyala, the
sheep prices at Ed Da'ein have been well in excess of the corresponding
prices at Nyala for each of the five years. This could be explained by the
fact that the majority of sheep sold at Nyala are for the local trade and
would not be as well conditioned as the ones intended to be transferred to
the east. The selection of these stronger sheep would be centered around
Ed Da'ein, where the costs of transport are cheaper. The difference in these
local prices and those received for the same type of animals at Omdourman
normally varies from between 40 to 50 percent, thus providing a large margin
for the merchants and traders involved in their purchase and resale. These
large variations in prices indicate that the producer is hardly receiving a
fair price for his animals, despite the inherent risks that are involved in
moving of stock over a distance of 1,000 to 1,200 kilometers.
Future Development of Livestock Marketing in South Darfur
25.       The present livestock marketing system operates efficiently and
should remain much as is in the future. However, one of the most pressing
needs is to change the rules and regulations governing the operation of the
existing marketing system and to make sure that once the rules are changed
they are enforced. New licencing laws for all merchants and middlemen should
be drawn up as soon as possible by the Livestock and Meat Marketing Commission
(LMMC), which has been recently established. Local Rural Councils should have
the responsibility of enforcing these new laws. The middlemen should be
obliged to pay fees every market day to operate and to enter the markets.
They should be required to renew their licences every six months. Unlicensed
operators should not be allowed in the livestock market area.



ANNEX 16
Page 10
26.       Regulations are required for the use of the holding areas adjacent
to livTestock markets. These areas should be enclosed in future by fences and
the Rural Council would be responsible for providing shade and water facili-
ties in them. Producers and merchants holding animals in these areas for more
than one day should be required to pay a fee for the use of the facilities.
27.       Many of the presently unlicensed markets throughout the Project
area should be operated as soon as practicable by staff of the Rural Councils.
At the present time these markets are being operated by local merchants who
are empowered to collect taxes (Annex 19). This situation is open to abuse
and it is necessary that the local authorities take over the operation of
these facilities. It is estimated that the salaries and expenses incurred
could be recouped from the additional taxes collected.
28.       A number of physical improvements to existing facilities are needed.
Few of the existing livestock markets are subdivided so that animals can be
segregated during sale. Segregation would allow buyers to evaluate the
animals more readily than if they were all in the one confined space, while
other buyers could concentrate on purchasing sheep. There are a number of
larger selling centers such as Abu Matariq, Gimelaya and Furdus which have no
-sales enclosures at all. To exercise efficient control over the running of
the sales and physical control of animals, enclosures are required. These
need not be elaborate structures, but simple circular or square yards divided
into four sections.
29.       Livestock Marketing Supervisors should be appointed for Nyala and
Ed Da'ein. 1/ These supervisors would work in close cooperation with the
LMMC, the body which would be responsible for establishing new rules and
regulations for the operation of all livestock markets. Their principal
task would be to help locate irregularities within the present marketing
system and, in conjunction with officers of the Rural Councils, to devise
constructive plans to overcome these problems. They should not become
involved with the actual implementation of laws and regulations, but they
would be responsible for the collection of marketing data in the area. With
the establishment of a radio relay station at Nyala, they could use Radio
Omdourman to make known continuously to the producers and the middlemen what
price changes and stock movements are taking place. They should provide a
news-sheet at the livestock markets for the use of rural officers, producers
and middlemen. They would be responsible for the monthly collection of
livestock markets and rail movement figures which would then be supplied
as quickly as possible to the LMMC.
30.       At a later date (after 5-6 years), the establishment of more modern
livestock selling and holding facilities at both Nyala and Ed Da'ein would be
necessary. This would tie in with the proposed introduction of the new live-
stock block train service to the east. 2/ These complexes would be composed
1/   These supervisors should cooperate closely with the WSDC (para. 14).
2/   This service would possibly be included in a proposed IDA Livestock
Marketing Project scheduled for FY 1978, or might alternatively be
included in a Second Phase Project of the Development Program.



ANNEX 16
Page  11
of four sections: (i) holding areas for the livestock markets; (ii) the
zariba itself; (iii) modern crush and race for veterinary use; and (iv) the
holding area and pens for the railway system. All these facilities should
be connected by a series of well constructed laneways so that animals can be
moved quickly between each area and segregated as far as possible. So as to
minimize the risk of spreading contagious diseases it would be expected that
these complexes would be well serviced by the veterinary department and that
on all sale days a veterinarian would be in attendance before the sale com-
menced so as to eliminate any diseased animals from the holding area and
zariba.
31.       The opening up of new trek routes throughout the area should
contribute towards the possibility of drawing up a defined seasonal sales
roster for the area. This would help to ensure that at a majority of sales
a maximum number of buyers would be on hand to create sufficient competition
to minimise the violent fluctuations in price that are now so common in the
industry.
32.       The grouping of animals in the farigs so as to sell in large
numbers in specific markets should be encouraged by the veterinary teams
supplying animal health and extension services to the nomads in cooperation
with the Livestock Marketing Supervisors. Their activities could also be
coordinated with purchasing officers for the Animal Production Public Cor-
poration (APPC). Mobile weighing scales could be brought to the farigs and
the relative groups of animals weighed and a price offered to the owners.
The distinct advantages of selling on a liveweight basis could be carefully
demonstrated to the owners under these conditions and could be responsible
for the quicker acceptance of this practice within the industry.
33.       The upgrading of slaughtering facilities throughout the area must
receive attention. At a later stage the establishment of a small abattoir
at Nyala could be considered. However, the actual timing of construction
and the priority on a national basis would be determined by a study carried
out by LMMC at a later stage. This abattoir would (if the supply of animals
was sufficient and if the transport costs were reasonable) be built to export
standards so that in future it may be possible to supply meat directly from
it to some of the Central African countries. It would also supply Nyala
and send meat to Khartoum in times of seasonal shortages.
34.       Other slaughtering facilities at Ed Da'ein and larger villages
should be enclosed and supplied with adequate water so that reasonable
hygiene standards can be met. Regulating the hygiene standards of the
local retail outlets is necessary.
35.       The timing and costing of these structural improvements has not
been included in the proposed Project as their implementation needs further
consideration. They could, to a limited scope, be financed directly by the
Rural Councils from revenue collected by animal sales taxes; however, the
whole concept as proposed could be financed separately under a National
Livestock Marketing Project if it would be found economically feasible and
technically justifiable.
April 11, 1977

ANNEX 18
Page 1
SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Government Cash Flow 1/
1.        Government would finance ISd 5.1 million (US$12.7 million) or 33%
of the total Project costs 2/. After the implementation period, Government
would have to cover annual operating costs on average over a period of 19
years of about iSd 0.6 million (US$1.5 million) in 1977 prices as well as the
debt service payments to IDA and SFD. As no fees are expected to be charged
for Project services, the Project units would have no direct income. Govern-
ment would, however, recoup some of the expenditures in a number of ways.
The most important of these would be through:
(a) import taxes and duties levied on Project inputs;
(b) income taxes levied on salaries paid to Project
staff;
(c) crop and livestock taxes; and
(d) revenues from water charges.
Over the estimated life of the Project (30 years), the following incremental
revenues would be generated.
hSd million
Import taxes and duties                     16.5
Crop and livestock taxes                    22.3
Income tax from staff salaries              10.9
Revenues from water charges in
Project area                               9.4 3/
Sub-total                             59.1
Revenues from water charges in
the whole Province                        26.1 4/
Total                                  85.2
1/   All figures in current US$.
2/   No estimate about Government's contribution in a Second Phase Project
has been made at this stage.
3/   30 wateryards.
4/   170 wateryards.



ANNEX 18
Page 2
These would over 30 years cover the Government's debt service payments to
IDA and SFD, operating, maintenance and replacement costs after Project
completion, its contribution to the Project for local salaries and import
taxes and duties. Within the projected thirty years, Government's net cash
flow would be negative year 18 and would remain positive until thereafter.
In year 30 the cash flow would be cumulatively positive and amount to 4Sd 7.9
million. In Government's cash flow projection, income generated from the
additional 170 wateryards located in the whole Province has been considered as
Project related, as costs for additional equipment needed for the reinforcing
of the maintenance capability, the improvement of the management, and organiza-
tion of the wateryards and the better collection for water usage has been
included in Project cost.
2.        However, in comparing the revenue earning power of the Project
with total expenditures, it has to be borne in mind that the expenditures
proposed for the Western Savannah Development Corporation (WSDC) are also
needed to modernize those parts of the local administration in the Province
involved in agricultural production so as to enable them to handle development
oriented programs. As it is expected that the WSDC would be gradually phased
out as an autonomous entity as its various functions are taken over by existing
local institutions, Government's cash flow should be treated also as a develop-
ment oriented expenditure to create and support a capable and efficient local
administration, rather than as an indication of inadequate revenues generated
by the Project.
3.        All revenues from taxes and duties have been estimated at existing
levels. Considering the difficulties in estimating the amount of property
taxes based on the number of animals owned by pastoralists, and given the
existing incidence of tax evasion, the expected revenue from various taxes has
been calculated cautiously. It has been assumed that crop taxes ("ashur")
would be collected at a rate of 70% for the first 15 years and 80% in years 16
to 30; presently their rate of collection is around 50%. Furthermore it was
assumed that water charges would be collected at about 80% in 30 Project
wateryards and at 50% in the remaining 170 wateryards in the Province, and
that 20% of the water pumped would be lost. In the Sudan the collection of
personal income taxes of employees and of the social security contribution
is efficient, as payroll deductions are made; therefore this amount has been
fully accounted for. Also, market fees from animals sold have been estimated
at the present level of collection (about 50%), based on all animals to be
sold under the Project. Although the estimated amount of taxes could be in-
creased through better tax collection and increased commercialization in the
Project area, such an increase was not allowed for in the Project calculations.
April 11, 1977

SUDAN
SAVANNAH DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Taxes and Duties 1/
Introduction
1.        Taxes in the Sudan are levied by both the Central Government and
by local governments. The main sources of Central Government revenue are
direct and indirect taxes. Revenue from direct taxes has grown in importance
over the past five years from 20 to 30% of tax revenues. Indirect taxes
account for about 50% of total Government revenue and 70% of tax revenue.
Taxes on international trade (import and export duties) are the most impor-
tant, comprising 36% of total Government revenue. Taxes on production and
consumption are somewhat less important, but still exceed the value of direct
taxes. Central Government taxes are levied in South Darfur Province through
the office of the Ministry of Finance in Nyala. By their nature, their
incidence falls almost exclusively on urban dwellers and merchants, and on
Government employees. The vast majority of the population is liable only
for local government taxes.
2.        The main sources of local government revenue are property rates
and taxes on crops, animals and land. Average total local revenue for
South Darfur has been estimated at about 1Sd 1.4 million (US$3.5 million)
per year. The revenue of Eastern District Council, in 1974/75 amounted to
over 1Sd 600,000 (US$1.5 million).
Local Revenue, Eastern District Council, 1970/71 to 1974/75
ISd '000        US$ '000
i970/71                 380                950
1971/72                 360                900
1972/73                 400              1,000
1973/74                 400              1,000
1974/75                 600             1,500
1/   Source of Data:  Hunting Technical Services and Ministry of Finance.



ANNEX 19
Page 2
There are three taxes which affect most people. These are:
(a) the livestock sales tax;
(b) the tribal animal tax; and
(c) the crop sales tax.
Livestock Sales Tax
3.         This tax is levied at a fixed rate which is set annually for the
whole province. The rate had remained stable for a number of years but was
revised upwards in 1973/74. The tax is paid by the buyer and represents
(1974/75) just over .5 percent of the value of animals traded at Ed Da'ein.
Although a rate for calves is given, in practice all cattle pay the full tax.
Recently, a local "self-help" contribution tax has been added to the basic
tax by the Council; this is 10 pt (US$0.25) per head, plus the usual charge
of 1 pt (US$0. 025) per receipt issued. This tax has also been levied on
other items including sacks of sugar, flour, etc. An additional tax is
payable by butchers who slaughter in the town slaughterhouse.
4.         The sales tax is collected by clerks of the Rural Council at the
four main livestock markets in Eastern District 1/; a legal sales receipt
cannot be obtained unless the tax has been paid. In the other twenty or
so registered markets in the area, the rights to collect the sales tax
and to issue legal sales receipts are sold to private individuals. The
collection rights in the largest of these markets (Abu Gabra and Kileikli
Abu Salama) were sold for ISd 1,000 and iSd 900 in 1975/76.  The value of tax
receipts sold may be two or three times the price of the market, and thus the
council loses a certain amount of potential tax revenues by utilizing this
procedure. On the other hand, however, it saves money which would otherwise
have to be spent mobilising clerks to attend the markets. As markets grow in
size, they may be taken over and controlled by the Council. Until 1965/66,
Ed Da'ein livestock market itself was privately operated; it is likely that
some of the larger sold markets may be taken over by the Council in the near
future.
1/   Nyala, Ed Da'ein, Abu latariq, and Gimelaya.

South Darfur 1975

(hSd per head)   (SSd per head)

Donkeys                                        0.125
5.        Evasion,  By comparing the current estimated offtake of livestock
in Eastern District Council with the recorded and estimated sales at regis-
tered markets, it is thought that approximately half the cattle, one quarter
of the sheep and an unknown number of goats do not pass through markets
when they are sold. This represents presently a loss of revenue in the
region of about LSd 18,000 (US$45,000) per year. Evasion is obviously a
simple matter since livestock traders and agents buy stock from farms and
from nomad camps, and the inconvenience of registering sales is probably as
much the reason for non-payment of tax as the desire to avoid payment, since
the tax is levied at such a low rate. Checks are supposed to be made at
Omdourman to prevent livestock entering the "zariba" which have not been
taxed, and there are guards patrolling the approaches to Omdourman for this
purpose. However, it is known that there are simple ways of avoiding this
obstacle by using re-cycled receipts.
6.        At the markets, all sales are taxed and the tax receipt is taken
as evidence of ownership. The stamp of the "demin" or guarantor on the
receipt also provides some security for the buyer against the risk of having
bought a stolen animal. An undoubted advantage to those merchants who pur-
hase livestock markets, apart from the tax revenue, is the fact that they
are in a position to issue valid tax receipts to themselves to cover all their
dealings. At present, no systematic check is made on the number of receipt
books used at each of the sold markets (at least, in the Eastern District of
South Darfur) so the difference between the auction price of the market and
the tax revenue collected cannot be estimated.



ANNEX 19
Page 4
Tribal Animal Tax
7.        This is essentially a wealth tax, based on ownership of livestock.
It is levied each year at a specified rate for each class of livestock
throughout the province, with young and draught animals exempt. The maximum
rates were originally laid down in 1925 and applied until recently. Presently
they are as follows:
Sudan (Maximum)         South Darfur
- --------------ISd--
Camels                          1.00                    0.375
Horses                         0.50                     0.625
Cattle                         0.50                     0.600
Donkeys                         0.25                    0.125
Sheep                          0.15                     0.065
Goats                          0.10
The collection of this tax is the only remaining function of the traditional
administration system. The tribes are divided into omodiyas and sheikships
(Annex 4) and tax estimates are based on the returns made by them for each
subdivision of the tribe. Eastern District is the Council to which the omdas
of the Plzeiqat pay the tribal animal tax.  Clearly the Council officers have
no way of accurately estimating livestock numbers. In practice the total tax
liability, which is determined largely by the demands of the Provincial Coun-
cil, is counted in livestock and divided among the 15 omdas in the Eastern
District Council on the basis of previous estimates. The omdas themselves
divide up the liability among the sheiks who are responsible for its collec-
tion, and they are assisted in this by the manadeeb who patrol the migration
routes checking on tax payment. The bulk of tax payments are made between
July and November when the pastoralists are selling livestock. The sheiks
receive 20 percent of the value and the omdas ISd 6 (US$15) per month for
their services. Despite the recent large increases in the tax assessments,
the taxes are usually paid and there are no serious arrears.
8.        Evasion.  As long as the total tax estimate is paid, there is
in effect no evasion of the tax. Although some livestock owners may well
pay no tax at all, there is some prestige attached to possession of a tax
receipt, which often persuades even owners of very small numbers of stock
to pay the tax. It is estimated that only one-fifth of the estimated number



ANNEX 19
Page 5
of cattle and sheep are assessed for tax. Consequently, there is great scope
for increasing the rateable value of each omda by raising his taxable assess-
ment, and this has been a popular way of quickly increasing the provincial
revenue. As it stands, the tax has no effect at all upon the number of
animals because the assessment rate is so low, amounting to no more than
1Sd 2 (US$5) over the life of a mature bull if it is in fact paid. In
addition, the present means of collecting the tax is very erratic so that it
is not directly related to the ability to pay. To be effective as a tool of
social policy, it would have to be based on actual ownership and levied at a
proportional, progressive or retrogressive rate.
Crop Tax
9.        Crop tax rates are set annually for each council area, and some
variation in the pattern of output is reflected in the tax rates applied.
Together they represent just over 10 percent of the value of rainfed cash
crops and just less than 10 percent of the value of marketed staple grains.
There are two crop taxes:
(a) Gebana. Although this is called a tax, it is, in
effect, a market handling charge to cover weighing,
porterage, etc., and is set at a fixed sum per kantar
for each crop. Hashab, hides and honey pay only this
tax.
(b) Ashur. This is an ad valorem tax on rainfed crops,
including the staple grains which are marketed.
The following were the rates in the Eastern District Council in 1974/75.
Gebana                    Ashur
(per kantar)              (per kantar)
45 kg                    45 kg

(Groundnuts, sesame,

shatta, salsa, lubia).

ANNEX 19
Page 6
The crop tax rates have been raised twice in the last four years and the tax
on dukhn and dura was not imposed until 1971/72. The largest increase has
been on hashab which has moved from 5 pt per kantar (pre 1971/72) to 13 pt
(1972/72) to 25 pt (1974/75). Ashur on rainfed crops has been raised from
8 percent to 10 percent.
10.       Evasion.  The taxes are paid by the crop buyers when the crop is
weighed at each of the registered crop markets. Merchants who buy crops out-
side the markets are obliged to take them to the nearest market (or more
usually Ed Da'ein) to have them check weighed and taxed. Since the number
of major crop merchants in the area is small, the opportunities for evasion
(assuming the market clerks are not acting corruptly) are small. It is
known that sometimes agents pay the crop tax in their own name instead of
the merchant's so as to reduce the liability for income or business profits
tax. There are road posts outside Nyala and in Ed Da'ein to check that the
taxes have been paid on crops being moved by lorry, and direct checks are
made on the oil mills and warehouses to prevent groundnuts and sesame from
going there untaxed. In general, it is not thought that large quantities
of the major cash crops go untaxed. The staples are only consistently taxed
when traded by merchants and the small quantities sold at local markets are
not taxed systematically.
11.       Revenues from groundnuts were decisive and amounted to about 57%
of total estimated tax revenues in the Eastern District Council in 1974/75.
Total revenues from all taxes collected amounted to SdL 236,000 (US$690,000),
of which about 30% was from livestock sales and about 70% from crop sales
(Table 1).
January 28, 1977

Economic Analysis
1.        The main assumptions used in the economic rate of return calcula-
tions are summarized below; further details are available in Tables 1 to 5.
2.        Program Life.  The economic life of the proposed Development Program
(Program) was assumed to be 30 years. In this analysis the costs and benefits
of both the proposed Project and of a Second Phase Project were included.
3.        Costs.  Costs were based on prices estimated as of March 1977.
They include all of the incremental capital and operating costs which would
be incurred on behalf of the Program, with the exception of the costs of:
(i) technical assistance to marketing companies; (ii) the health survey;
and (iii) operation of water facilities not directly related to the settle-
ment and livestock components of the Program. The economic effect of additional
employment generated by the Program and the expected foreign exchange earnings
have been included in the rate of return calculation. Incremental family
labor was costed at zero as this represents labor which would otherwise remain
unutilized. Farm labor was valued at market prices. Costs exclude taxes and
other transfers within the economy. A physical contingency of 10% was
included in the rate of return calculations.
4.        Benefits.  The groundnut prices used in the analysis were based on
projected prices over the development period (Table 7). Gum arabic prices
used were the actual prices for the 1975/76 season; these prices were held
constant for the entire development period (Table 8). Livestock prices
utilized were the actual current market prices in Nyala; these were also
held constant throughout the development period. The millet production
foregone during settlement was not included, as it would be offset by the
food procured under the World Food Program.
5.        Foreign Exchange.  Foreign exchange was shadow priced at a rate
of ISd 1 = US$2 (compared to the current rate of bSd 1 = US$2.5) in the
economic cost and benefit calculations to reflect a more realistic value
of foreign exchange for the economy. The rate of return without shadow
pricing of foreign exchange was also computed (Table 2).
Rates of Return
6.        The following rates of return for the proposed program were
calculated:
(a) Overall economic rate of return for both the Project and the
Second Phase Project combined (Table 1). The rate of return = 14.5%.



ANNEX 20
Page 2
(b) Overall financial rate of return (no shadow pricing of
fcreign exchange) for both the Project and the Second Phase
Project combined (Table 2). The rate of return = 15%.
(c) Economic rate of return for the settlement and crop compo-
nents for both the Project and the Second Phase Project
combined (Table 3). The rate of return = 9%.
(d) Economic rate of return for the settlement and crop
components of the Project only (Table 4). Rate of
return = 4.6%.
(e) Economic rate of return for the livestock component for
both the Project and the Second Phase Project combined
(Table 5). Rate of return = 22.5%.
7.        The overall rate of return for the Project and the Second Phase
Project combined (14.5%) is a reflection of the rates of return of two
components: (i) settlement and crop production (rate of return 9%); and (ii)
livestock development (rate of return 22.5%). These two components, however,
are not independent; one of the main purposes of the settlement program is
the opening of stockroutes for nomadic cattle, and any reduction of the
settlement and crop component would adversely affect the livestock produc-
tion. It is not possible at this stage to quantify this adverse effect and
no sensitivity analysis has been made on the settlement rate or on the
number of families settled. Therefore, a reduction of the settlement and
crop component would not necessarily improve the overall rate of return.
8.        The calculation of an overall rate of return for the Project only
was not considered appropriate as such a calculation would not reflect
the potential benefits of the entire development program - a period of 10
to 12 years. In the Project period, settlement work would begin in year two
and by the end of year 5 or 6 only about 25% of the settlers would be
allocated land; at that time only a modest start-up production would have
been achieved. At that time also it is expected that the adaptive research
program for crop production would come forward with proposals to improve
the traditional farming methods and to increase production. In the initial
years, any success in modernizing livestock production would be modest,
as efforts would be geared towards testing the attitudes of the pastoralists
and preparing the basis for a sustained growth in subsequent years. There-
fore, no separate assessment of Project livestock benefits could be cal-
culated. Initial investments in Project related infrastructure, research,
management and organization, veterinary services, etc. would be high, and
results would materialize only gradually over a longer period of time.
Sensitivity Analysis
9.        The analysis of the rate of return was tested against changes
in costs and benefits due to variations in prices, production, slippage
of benefits and non-adoption of improved farming techniques by settlers.
Details of the sensitivity analysis are presented below and are summarized
in Table 6.



ANNEX 20
Page 3
10.       Sensitivity of the overall economic rate of return (ERR) of the
Project and the Second Phase Project combined. An increase of 10% in all
costs would reduce the ERR by 1% p^    from' l!1,5% to 13.5%.  A decrease of 10%
in benefits would also reduce the ERR by 1% point from 14.5% to 13.5%. A
decrease in livestock sales of 10% or the non-adoption of improved cultivation
techniques by settlers would reduce the ERR by 1.7% points from 14.5% to
12.8%. A one year delay in benefits would reduce the ERR by 1.5% points from
14.5% to 13%.
11.       Sensitivity of the settlement and crop component, the Project and
the Second Phase Project combined. The non-adoption of improved techniques by
farmers would reduce the ERR of the settlement and crop component by 4.5%
points from 9% to 4.5%.
12.       Sensitivity of the livestock component.  A decrease of 10% in live-
stock sales would reduce the ERR of the livestock component by 3.8% points
from 22.5% to 18.7%.
Project Risk
13.       The Project would involve a number of substantial risks which could
affect its rate of implementation and possibly its overall success. The most
important of these would be the extent to which the various proposals would
prove acceptable and the rate at which it would be practically feasible to
introduce them. There would be some uncertainty as to whether the new
settlers would be able to achieve the projected yields; this in turn would
have an effect on cropping patterns. There is also a possibility that
rapidly fluctuating crop prices could have an adverse effect on settlers'
incomes and hence on their willingness to participate. More basic perhaps,
is the fact that the willingness of farmers to settle in new areas is not
known precisely. In particular, farmers may not wish to abandon some of the
area they occupy at present, and in this case the trekking routes could not
be opened as planned. Once settled in the new areas, farmers may reject the
WSDC's advice and continue their present cultivation practices, thus allowing
the area around the newly opened wateryards to deteriorate. Because of the
degree of risk and uncertainty involved, a two-phase development appears to
offer the advantage of increased flexibility as well as a reduction in the
risk which would be incurred should the first stage prove unsuccessful. The
ability and capability of the WSDC simultaneously to staff and implement an
innovative Project would be a major risk. The WSDC's management capability
would, in fact, be one of the crucial determinating factors in the success
of the Project. This management risk is somewhat difficult to quantify, and
was therefore not included directly in the sensitivity analysis; its primary
effect would be a delay in benefits. It is expected to be somewhat lessened,
however, through the provision of professional and supporting staff for the
WSDC under the Project.
14.       In trying to lessen the risk one solution would have been to reduce
both the size and the scope of the Project by either curtailing or postponing
the implementation of individual components; another solution might have been



ANNEX 20
Page 4
to reduce the area covered by the Project. Both of these alternatives would,
however, be self-defeating. The scope of some individual components was
greatly reduced during appraisal and some which were not immediately perti-
nent to the Project's success were fully deleted. However, as all activities
which are proposed for financing under the Project are highly inter-dependent,
any further reductions would endanger the effectiveness of the entire effort.
It was also felt that the activities should be initiated as scheduled, for
otherwise there would be a real danger that crop producers and pastoralists
would respond in ways which could jeopardize the synchronization of the devel-
opment concept as a whole.
15.       The Project would also be difficult in that it would require a
conservative nomadic people to institute substantial changes in their tradi-
tional practices. In fact, without these changes in the nomads' traditional
livestock practices and cultural outlook, the rangelands would continue to
deteriorate as a result of over-grazing and other poor livestock management
practices. Hence, one of the major objectives of the Project would be to
achieve a better balance between livestock herds and the carrying capacity
of land by means of persuasion, education and training, and by providing
incentives to stockowners to sell more cattle. Efforts would also be made to
involve the traditional (tribal) authorities in the development effort, and
the ability of Project managers to ensure their active cooperation would be
crucial. However, these risks must be balanced against greater certainty
that without the Project the long-term decline of the Project area would be
accelerated, and the incomes and way of life of the inhabitants seriously
jeopardized.
April 11, 1977

Project Economic Rate of Return Calculation: Footnotes to Table I
1/   This calculation is based on prices estimated as of March 1977 and in
1977 constant prices. The economic prices are net of taxes and other
transfers to the economy. The shadow exchange rate of iSd I = US$2.0
has been used in the analysis in estimating the difference between
foreign and domestic prices.
2/   Project Management includes the following section of the Western Savannah
Development Corporation: Director's office, Khartoum office, Administration
and Finance Division, Procurement Division, Marketing Division, 50% of
Civil Works Division, Deputy Director General's office and Administrative
Unit. Up to year twelve, 100% of the corresponding economic costs have
been taken into account, except the costs of the Procurement Division
which also handles the ODM/Abu Dhabi-financed project and for which only
50% of economic costs are allocated to the Project. After year twelve,
only 50% of Project management costs are charged to the Project, as most
management overheads directed at settlement and crops until year twelve
would be carried out at other areas for which no benefits are quantified.
Management overheads for livestock production and water facilities would
remain unchanged throughout the Project's life.

project and for which only 50% of economic costs are allocated to the
Project. Cost of food procured by the World Food Program is not in-
cluded. After year twelve, only 20% of the costs of the above compo-
nents excluding the World Food Program Unit, which no longer will be
necessary, are charged to the Project, as most activities related to
settlement and crops would be directed at other areas for which no
benefits are quantified.

the same as in Table 3.  Between year 5 and year 12 (included), 25% of Project management costs shown in Table 3 are charged to Phase I because most of
the activities will be concentrated on the 7,725 families settling during Phase II.  After year 12, only 12% of these costs are charged to
Phase I.
3/  Sections of the DA and cost items included in the Settlement and Crops cost stream are listed in footnote 3 of Table 1.  After year 5, costs
of the World Food Program Unit are not included.Up to year 5, costs are the same as in Table 3. Between year 5 and year 12 (included), 25% of
the costs shown in Table 3 are charged to Phase I.  After year 12, 5% of the costs are charged to Phase I (compared to 20% of costs charged
to Phases I and II combined in the analysis presented in Table 1).
